Prophethood

Al-Nasafi's Proof from the Story of Musa
Al-Nasafi's scriptural arguments for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses the story of Musa (AS) to establish that seeing Allah is rationally possible and not impossible.

Core Idea
God's response to Musa implies possibility, not eternal impossibility.

Al-Nasafi draws five powerful clues from the Qur'anic narrative of Musa asking to see Allah. Each clue, from Musa's own request to God's lack of rebuke, points to the same conclusion: ru'ya is permissible and not impossible. This lesson unpacks these arguments and shows why the verse 'You will not see Me' does not negate all future vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice how al-Nasafi moves from one argument to the next without interruption.
The passage is a continuous block of reasoning.
2
Identify the five arguments
List the five distinct proofs al-Nasafi extracts from the story. They are: (1) Musa's request itself, (2) God's answer lacking the word 'impossible', (3) conditional suspension on the mountain, (4) absence of rebuke, and (5) the manifestation (tajalli).
Each argument is a separate logical move.
3
Discuss the key terms
Examine the terms 'jawaz', 'mustahil', 'ta'liq', and 'tajalli'. Understand how they function in kalam discourse about divine attributes.
These terms are foundational for the debate on ru'ya.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Many think 'lan tarani' proves eternal impossibility. Al-Nasafi argues otherwise. Discuss why the absence of 'mustahil' in God's reply is significant.
The common misunderstanding is explicitly countered in the passage.
5
Reflect on the evidence
Consider how al-Nasafi uses the narrative to support the Ash'ari position that ru'ya is possible in the afterlife. Why does he include the interpretation from Ibn Furak about the mountain?
This cements the argument that vision is not inherently impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقيم الأدلة على جواز رؤية الله من القرآن والسنة المطهرة. من ذلك قول موسى عليه السلام: ﴿ رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] ففي الآية اعتقاد الله لأن الجهل منتف عن الرسول وإخبار الله إياه أنه لن يراه، ولم يخبر الله سبحانه أن رؤيته مستحيلة مع أن الحاجة للبيان واضحة، فدل على جواز رؤيته تعالى، كذلك فإن الله تعالى علق الرؤية على أمر جائز وهو استقرار الجبل، وتعليق الفعل على ما هو جائز الوجود يدل على جوازه، وكذلك فإنه تعالى ما أيأس موسى ولا عاتبه حينما سأله رؤيته، وهذا يدل على جواز الرؤية، ولو كان الأمر مستحيلا أو جهلا من موسى عليه السلام، لكان العتاب، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ فَلَمَّا تَجَلَّى ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] والتجلي هو الظهور، ويفسره النسفي نقلا عن ابن فورك عن أبي الحسن الأشعري: أن الله تعالى أعطى الجبل روحا وقدرة على الرؤية فرأى الله.
He establishes the proofs for the permissibility of seeing God from the Qur'an and the purified Sunnah. Among these is the saying of Moses, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' (Q. 7:143). For in the verse there is an acknowledgment of God, because ignorance is negated from the Messenger, and God informed him that he would not see Him, yet God did not inform that seeing Him is impossible, despite the clear need for clarification—so this indicates the permissibility of seeing Him. Likewise, God suspended vision upon a permissible matter, namely the mountain's settling; suspending an action upon something possible indicates its possibility. Also, He did not disappoint Moses nor rebuke him when he asked to see Him; this indicates the permissibility of vision, for if the matter were impossible or Moses were ignorant, there would have been rebuke. And similarly His statement: 'And when his Lord manifested Himself' (Q. 7:143), and al-tajallī is manifestation. Al-Nasafi interprets it, transmitted from Ibn Fūrak from Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, that God gave the mountain a soul and the ability to see, and it saw God.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nasafi's positive proofs from the Qur'an for the permissibility of seeing Allah. He uses the story of Moses (AS) asking to see God in Surah al-A'raf (7:143). He gives several arguments extracted from the narrative: (1) Moses' request itself is evidence because prophets are free from ignorance—if it were impossible, Moses would not have asked. (2) God answered that Moses would not see Him, but did not say it is impossible (mustaḥīl); given the need for clear clarification, if it were impossible, God would have stated so. (3) God made vision conditional upon the mountain's stability, which is a possible (jāʾiz) event; linking something to a possible act indicates the possibility of that thing. (4) God did not rebuke Moses for asking; rebuke would have come if the request were impossible or ignorant. (5) The phrase 'When his Lord manifested Himself' (tajallā) implies a form of appearance. Al-Nasafi then cites a transmission from Ibn Fūrak from al-Ashʿarī that God gave the mountain a soul and the ability to see, and it actually saw God, demonstrating that ru'ya is possible.

Key Terms
أرني
جواز
تعليق
تجلي
مستحيل
جاهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the verse 'lan tarani' (You will not see Me) proves that seeing Allah is absolutely impossible in this life and the next.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that 'lan' only negates immediate future vision, not eternal impossibility. God did not say 'You cannot see Me' (mustahil), and He made vision conditional on a possible event, indicating it is possible in principle.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's argument, why does God's reply 'lan tarani' not prove that ru'ya is impossible?
A) Because Moses was not a prophet.
B) Because God did not say 'mustahil' (impossible).
C) Because the mountain actually saw God.
D) Because God rebuked Moses.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi uses five clues from the story of Musa to demonstrate that seeing Allah is rationally possible and not inherently impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you secretly assume that some things are too big or impossible for Allah to grant, or do you truly believe in His absolute power as this lesson shows? What will you ask Him today that you previously thought was impossible?
?
Allah did not rebuke Musa for asking to see Him. Are you afraid to ask Allah for deep spiritual experiences—like true knowledge of Him or closeness in prayer—because you think it's presumptuous? What is holding you back from making that request right now?
?
When Allah says 'no' to a request of yours, do you interpret it as a final rejection or as a temporary 'not yet'? How does this lesson's distinction between 'lan tarani' (you will not see Me now) and 'mustahil' (impossible) change how you understand His responses to your prayers?

Prophethood

The Wisdom of Sending Prophets: Ahl al-Haqq's Position
Nasafi states the Ahl al-Haqq position on prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ahl al-Haqq affirm the sending of prophets as wise and permissible, supported by divine proofs.

Core Idea
Sending prophets is wise and permissible, affirmed by Ahl al-Haqq.

This lesson establishes the foundational view of the orthodox Sunni position on prophethood. Before diving into objections, we need to see what Ahl al-Haqq affirm: that sending messengers is not only possible but wise. The passage opens with two key questions and answers them directly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the two questions and the answer from Ahl al-Haqq.
Notice the concise style: the author frames the issue as a debate.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that sending messengers is 'wisdom and correctness' (ḥikma wa ṣawāb) and permissible (jāʾiz). Note that Ahl al-Haqq affirm both the moral and legal possibility.
3
Understand the support
Allah sent messengers with good tidings and warnings, and supported them with proofs (ḥujaj). This is the basis for the claim.
These proofs refer to miracles and rational arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إثبات النبوة والرسالة. هل من الحكمة إرسال الرسل؟ وهل الإرسال جائز؟ قال أهل الحق: إن إرسال الرسل حكمة وصواب، وهو جائز، وقد أرسل الله تعالى رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين، وأيدهم بالحجج. وقالت طوائف من الخُلَعَاء ومنهم المتصوفة في رأي النسفي بامتناعه إذ فيه أمر ونهي وهو سفه، إذ لا نفع للآمر والناهي، وهنا يهاجم النسفي المتصوفة بقوله: وعند اكتشاف القناع عن مكنون ضمائر كثير من المستترين بالتصوف، وارتفاع الخفاء عن مستور سرائرهم، ويعلم أنه من هذه الفرقة. ومن الذين يقولون بامتناع إرسال الرسل البراهمة، حيث يؤكدون أن العقل يعرف المحاسن فيعملها، ويعرف المساوئ فلا يعملها فالتكليف يكون بالعقل ولا حاجة إلى الرسل لأنهم إن جاءوا موافقين للعقل فبالعقل عنهم غنية، وإن جاءوا مخالفين له كان إرسالهم عبثا لأن العقل حجة الله وحججه تعالى لا تتناقض. ومنهم أيضا الطبيعيون حيث يقولون: إن المتنبي يعارض النبي إذا كان الأمر على مقتضى الواقع، أما إذا خرج عن مقتضى الواقع ففيه تغيير طبائع الأشياء، وطبائع الأشياء لا تتغير وقال بعضهم بأن قلب الطبائع في مقدور الله، إلا أنه أيضا في مقدور السحرة ولا يمكن التمييز بين الأمرين. ومن المنكرين من يقول: بأن ما يقال إنه معجزة لم يعرض على كل البشر، ولو عرض لوجد المعارض، وبذلك لا رسول.
Discussion on establishing prophethood and messengership. Is it from wisdom to send messengers? And is sending permissible? The people of truth said: Sending messengers is wisdom and correctness, and it is permissible. Indeed, Allah sent messengers as bringers of good tidings and warners, and supported them with proofs. And groups of the corrupt – among them the Sufis according to al-Nasafi – said that it is impossible, for it involves command and prohibition which is foolishness, since there is no benefit for the commander and prohibitor. And here al-Nasafi attacks the Sufis with his saying: 'And upon uncovering the veil from the hidden secrets of many of those who conceal themselves under Sufism, and removal of concealment from their hidden inner thoughts, it is known that they are from this group.' And among those who say that sending messengers is impossible are the Brahmins, as they affirm that reason knows good deeds and performs them, and knows evil deeds and does not perform them. So obligation is by reason and there is no need for messengers, because if they come in agreement with reason, then reason suffices for them, and if they come in opposition to it, then their sending is futile, because reason is the proof of Allah, and His proofs do not contradict. And among them are the naturalists, as they say: The one who claims prophethood opposes the prophet if the matter is according to reality; but if it departs from reality, then it involves changing the natures of things, and natures of things do not change. And some of them said that the transformation of natures is in the power of Allah, but it is also in the power of sorcerers, and it is impossible to distinguish between the two matters. And among the deniers are those who say: What is said to be a miracle has not been presented to all people, and if it were presented, it would find opposition, and thus no messenger.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on prophethood (nubuwwa). It first poses two fundamental questions: Is sending prophets wise? Is it permissible? Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox) affirm both. The passage then presents three objections from opponents. First, the khula'a (corrupt ones), among whom Nasafi identifies some Sufis (mutasawwifa), argue that commands and prohibitions are foolish because God gains no benefit. Nasafi retorts by unmasking these Sufis as insincere. Second, the Brahmins (Barahima) hold that reason alone is sufficient to discern good and evil, so prophets are unnecessary: if a prophet agrees with reason, reason suffices; if he contradicts reason, his message is invalid because God's proofs do not conflict. Third, the naturalists (tabi'iyyun) claim that if a claimant to prophethood acts within nature, he competes with a prophet; if he changes nature, that alters the immutable natures of things – or if nature can be changed, sorcerers can do the same, making miracles indistinguishable. Finally, a generic denial: miracles are not verified for all, so no prophetic claim is proven. Thus, the passage catalogues rationalist objections that the Ash'ari-Maturidi tradition must refute.

Key Terms
رسل (messengers)
حكمة (wisdom)
جائز (permissible)
حجج (proofs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ahl al-Haqq consider sending prophets as obligatory upon God.
Correction: The passage says it is 'wisdom and permissible' (jāʾiz), not obligatory. God is not compelled; He sends prophets out of wisdom and grace.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, sending prophets is:
A) Obligatory upon God
B) Foolish and impossible
C) Wise and permissible
D) Only for warning, not good news

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Haqq affirm that sending prophets is wise and permissible, supported by divine proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If sending prophets is an act of divine wisdom, do you live as if you truly trust that Allah's guidance through them is the most fitting path for your life? Where do you rely more on your own wisdom than on what the prophets taught?
?
The prophets came as warners. What specific warning from a prophet's message are you avoiding or ignoring in your daily choices? What would change if you took that warning as personally addressed to you today?
?
Allah supported prophets with proofs. Have you personally examined any of those proofs to strengthen your conviction? If not, what is holding you back from seeking certainty about the truth of prophethood?

Refuting the Corrupt Sufis' Objection
Nasafi presents and refutes the objection that prophetic commands are foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the corrupt ones (khula'a) consider sending prophets foolish and how Nasafi responds by exposing their hypocrisy.

Core Idea
The claim that divine commands are foolish is refuted by unmasking the insincere.

After establishing the orthodox view, Nasafi turns to the first objection: from a group he calls the 'corrupt ones' (khula'a), who include some claiming to be Sufis. They argue that command and prohibition are useless because God gains no benefit. Nasafi’s response is sharp: he accuses them of hiding their true corrupt beliefs under the cloak of Sufism.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the argument that sending prophets involves command and prohibition, which is 'foolishness' (safah) because no benefit accrues to the commander.
This argument assumes God acts only for His own benefit, which is a false premise.
2
Identify the group
The passage identifies the objectors as 'khula'a' (corrupt ones) and singles out some Sufis (mutasawwifa) as part of this group.
Nasafi is not attacking all Sufis, only those who hide corruption under Sufi garb.
3
See the response
Nasafi retorts by saying that when the veil is lifted, many who conceal themselves under Sufism will be revealed as belonging to this corrupt group.
This is a rebuttal ad hominem, but in kalam it's acceptable to expose hypocrisy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إثبات النبوة والرسالة. هل من الحكمة إرسال الرسل؟ وهل الإرسال جائز؟ قال أهل الحق: إن إرسال الرسل حكمة وصواب، وهو جائز، وقد أرسل الله تعالى رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين، وأيدهم بالحجج. وقالت طوائف من الخُلَعَاء ومنهم المتصوفة في رأي النسفي بامتناعه إذ فيه أمر ونهي وهو سفه، إذ لا نفع للآمر والناهي، وهنا يهاجم النسفي المتصوفة بقوله: وعند اكتشاف القناع عن مكنون ضمائر كثير من المستترين بالتصوف، وارتفاع الخفاء عن مستور سرائرهم، ويعلم أنه من هذه الفرقة. ومن الذين يقولون بامتناع إرسال الرسل البراهمة، حيث يؤكدون أن العقل يعرف المحاسن فيعملها، ويعرف المساوئ فلا يعملها فالتكليف يكون بالعقل ولا حاجة إلى الرسل لأنهم إن جاءوا موافقين للعقل فبالعقل عنهم غنية، وإن جاءوا مخالفين له كان إرسالهم عبثا لأن العقل حجة الله وحججه تعالى لا تتناقض. ومنهم أيضا الطبيعيون حيث يقولون: إن المتنبي يعارض النبي إذا كان الأمر على مقتضى الواقع، أما إذا خرج عن مقتضى الواقع ففيه تغيير طبائع الأشياء، وطبائع الأشياء لا تتغير وقال بعضهم بأن قلب الطبائع في مقدور الله، إلا أنه أيضا في مقدور السحرة ولا يمكن التمييز بين الأمرين. ومن المنكرين من يقول: بأن ما يقال إنه معجزة لم يعرض على كل البشر، ولو عرض لوجد المعارض، وبذلك لا رسول.
Discussion on establishing prophethood and messengership. Is it from wisdom to send messengers? And is sending permissible? The people of truth said: Sending messengers is wisdom and correctness, and it is permissible. Indeed, Allah sent messengers as bringers of good tidings and warners, and supported them with proofs. And groups of the corrupt – among them the Sufis according to al-Nasafi – said that it is impossible, for it involves command and prohibition which is foolishness, since there is no benefit for the commander and prohibitor. And here al-Nasafi attacks the Sufis with his saying: 'And upon uncovering the veil from the hidden secrets of many of those who conceal themselves under Sufism, and removal of concealment from their hidden inner thoughts, it is known that they are from this group.' And among those who say that sending messengers is impossible are the Brahmins, as they affirm that reason knows good deeds and performs them, and knows evil deeds and does not perform them. So obligation is by reason and there is no need for messengers, because if they come in agreement with reason, then reason suffices for them, and if they come in opposition to it, then their sending is futile, because reason is the proof of Allah, and His proofs do not contradict. And among them are the naturalists, as they say: The one who claims prophethood opposes the prophet if the matter is according to reality; but if it departs from reality, then it involves changing the natures of things, and natures of things do not change. And some of them said that the transformation of natures is in the power of Allah, but it is also in the power of sorcerers, and it is impossible to distinguish between the two matters. And among the deniers are those who say: What is said to be a miracle has not been presented to all people, and if it were presented, it would find opposition, and thus no messenger.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on prophethood (nubuwwa). It first poses two fundamental questions: Is sending prophets wise? Is it permissible? Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox) affirm both. The passage then presents three objections from opponents. First, the khula'a (corrupt ones), among whom Nasafi identifies some Sufis (mutasawwifa), argue that commands and prohibitions are foolish because God gains no benefit. Nasafi retorts by unmasking these Sufis as insincere. Second, the Brahmins (Barahima) hold that reason alone is sufficient to discern good and evil, so prophets are unnecessary: if a prophet agrees with reason, reason suffices; if he contradicts reason, his message is invalid because God's proofs do not conflict. Third, the naturalists (tabi'iyyun) claim that if a claimant to prophethood acts within nature, he competes with a prophet; if he changes nature, that alters the immutable natures of things – or if nature can be changed, sorcerers can do the same, making miracles indistinguishable. Finally, a generic denial: miracles are not verified for all, so no prophetic claim is proven. Thus, the passage catalogues rationalist objections that the Ash'ari-Maturidi tradition must refute.

Key Terms
خلعاء (corrupt ones)
متصوفة (Sufis)
أمر ونهي (command and prohibition)
سفه (foolishness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's commands are pointless because He does not benefit.
Correction: God's commands are for the benefit of creation, not for His own benefit; calling them foolish stems from a corrupt perspective.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the corrupt ones (khula'a) against sending prophets?
A) Prophets always contradict reason
B) Command and prohibition are foolish because God gains no benefit
C) Miracles are impossible
D) Prophets are not necessary for guidance

Key Takeaway
The objection that prophetic commands are foolish is based on a false premise; such views often come from hidden corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a divine command as unnecessary or outdated because you didn't see immediate benefit? What does this passage reveal about the condition of your heart?
?
The objectors masked their inner corruption under the cloak of Sufism. What masks do you wear to justify your disobedience to Allah’s commands?
?
If your inner secrets were exposed today, would your objections to Allah's commands be seen as sincere doubt or hidden corruption?

Prophethood

Refuting the Brahmins and Naturalists on Prophethood
Nasafi presents the Brahmins' and naturalists' objections to prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rationalist objections to prophethood – from the Brahmins (sufficiency of reason) and the naturalists (impossibility of miracles) – and their flaws.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot replace revelation, and miracles are distinguishable from sorcery.

Nasafi now addresses two more sophisticated objections. The Brahmins argue that reason is sufficient for moral knowledge, making prophets redundant or contradictory. The naturalists argue that miracles would require changing natures, which is impossible or indistinguishable from sorcery. Both objections hinge on overestimating reason or misunderstanding divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Brahmin objection
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the Brahmin position: reason knows good and evil, so prophets are either redundant (if they agree with reason) or contradictory (if they oppose).
The Brahmins were a Hindu sect; this is a classic kalam argument.
2
Read the naturalist objections
The naturalists present two versions: one says miracles change natures, which is impossible; the other says God can change natures but so can sorcerers, making miracles indistinguishable.
Notice the two sub-arguments.
3
Consider the generic denial
Some deny prophethood by claiming that miracles are not universally verified, so no claimant is proven.
This is a skeptical argument based on lack of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إثبات النبوة والرسالة. هل من الحكمة إرسال الرسل؟ وهل الإرسال جائز؟ قال أهل الحق: إن إرسال الرسل حكمة وصواب، وهو جائز، وقد أرسل الله تعالى رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين، وأيدهم بالحجج. وقالت طوائف من الخُلَعَاء ومنهم المتصوفة في رأي النسفي بامتناعه إذ فيه أمر ونهي وهو سفه، إذ لا نفع للآمر والناهي، وهنا يهاجم النسفي المتصوفة بقوله: وعند اكتشاف القناع عن مكنون ضمائر كثير من المستترين بالتصوف، وارتفاع الخفاء عن مستور سرائرهم، ويعلم أنه من هذه الفرقة. ومن الذين يقولون بامتناع إرسال الرسل البراهمة، حيث يؤكدون أن العقل يعرف المحاسن فيعملها، ويعرف المساوئ فلا يعملها فالتكليف يكون بالعقل ولا حاجة إلى الرسل لأنهم إن جاءوا موافقين للعقل فبالعقل عنهم غنية، وإن جاءوا مخالفين له كان إرسالهم عبثا لأن العقل حجة الله وحججه تعالى لا تتناقض. ومنهم أيضا الطبيعيون حيث يقولون: إن المتنبي يعارض النبي إذا كان الأمر على مقتضى الواقع، أما إذا خرج عن مقتضى الواقع ففيه تغيير طبائع الأشياء، وطبائع الأشياء لا تتغير وقال بعضهم بأن قلب الطبائع في مقدور الله، إلا أنه أيضا في مقدور السحرة ولا يمكن التمييز بين الأمرين. ومن المنكرين من يقول: بأن ما يقال إنه معجزة لم يعرض على كل البشر، ولو عرض لوجد المعارض، وبذلك لا رسول.
Discussion on establishing prophethood and messengership. Is it from wisdom to send messengers? And is sending permissible? The people of truth said: Sending messengers is wisdom and correctness, and it is permissible. Indeed, Allah sent messengers as bringers of good tidings and warners, and supported them with proofs. And groups of the corrupt – among them the Sufis according to al-Nasafi – said that it is impossible, for it involves command and prohibition which is foolishness, since there is no benefit for the commander and prohibitor. And here al-Nasafi attacks the Sufis with his saying: 'And upon uncovering the veil from the hidden secrets of many of those who conceal themselves under Sufism, and removal of concealment from their hidden inner thoughts, it is known that they are from this group.' And among those who say that sending messengers is impossible are the Brahmins, as they affirm that reason knows good deeds and performs them, and knows evil deeds and does not perform them. So obligation is by reason and there is no need for messengers, because if they come in agreement with reason, then reason suffices for them, and if they come in opposition to it, then their sending is futile, because reason is the proof of Allah, and His proofs do not contradict. And among them are the naturalists, as they say: The one who claims prophethood opposes the prophet if the matter is according to reality; but if it departs from reality, then it involves changing the natures of things, and natures of things do not change. And some of them said that the transformation of natures is in the power of Allah, but it is also in the power of sorcerers, and it is impossible to distinguish between the two matters. And among the deniers are those who say: What is said to be a miracle has not been presented to all people, and if it were presented, it would find opposition, and thus no messenger.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on prophethood (nubuwwa). It first poses two fundamental questions: Is sending prophets wise? Is it permissible? Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox) affirm both. The passage then presents three objections from opponents. First, the khula'a (corrupt ones), among whom Nasafi identifies some Sufis (mutasawwifa), argue that commands and prohibitions are foolish because God gains no benefit. Nasafi retorts by unmasking these Sufis as insincere. Second, the Brahmins (Barahima) hold that reason alone is sufficient to discern good and evil, so prophets are unnecessary: if a prophet agrees with reason, reason suffices; if he contradicts reason, his message is invalid because God's proofs do not conflict. Third, the naturalists (tabi'iyyun) claim that if a claimant to prophethood acts within nature, he competes with a prophet; if he changes nature, that alters the immutable natures of things – or if nature can be changed, sorcerers can do the same, making miracles indistinguishable. Finally, a generic denial: miracles are not verified for all, so no prophetic claim is proven. Thus, the passage catalogues rationalist objections that the Ash'ari-Maturidi tradition must refute.

Key Terms
براهمة (Brahmins)
طبيعيون (naturalists)
معجزة (miracle)
عقل (reason)
طبائع الأشياء (natures of things)
سحرة (sorcerers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that reason alone can replace divine guidance entirely.
Correction: While reason can know some moral truths, it cannot provide the detailed guidance and confirmation that miracles and prophethood provide.

Checkpoint
According to the naturalists, what problem arises if God can change the natures of things?
A) Prophets would be unnecessary
B) Miracles would be indistinguishable from sorcery
C) Reason would be contradicted
D) No one would believe in miracles

Key Takeaway
The Brahmins overestimate reason, and the naturalists create false dilemmas about miracles; both are refuted elsewhere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever trusted your own intellect to the point of dismissing divine guidance? The Brahmins thought reason was enough. In what areas of your life do you rely on your own understanding rather than seeking Allah's revelation – and what is that costing you?
?
When you hear about miracles from the Prophet (peace be upon him), do you find yourself doubting because you didn't witness them yourself? The generic deniers said miracles must be shown to all. Is your faith conditional on sensory proof? How does that affect your trust in the Messenger?
?
The naturalists feared that if a miracle changes nature, it might be sorcery. Do you ever dismiss blessings or signs in your daily life as mere coincidence rather than recognizing them as divine interventions? What would change if you started seeing the extraordinary in the ordinary as acts of Allah?

Ahl al-Haqq and the Khula'a/Sufi Objection
Ahl al-Haqq's affirmation and Khula'a/Sufi objection with Nasafi's critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of Ahl al-Haqq on the wisdom of sending prophets and the objection from the Khula'a and some Sufis who consider it foolish.

Core Idea
Sending prophets is wisdom, but some claim it is pointless command and prohibition.

This lesson begins with the established Sunni view that sending messengers is both wise and permissible. It then presents a radical objection from the Khula'a and certain Sufis who argue that divine commands are useless. The author also includes a sharp critique of those who hide behind Sufism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Notice the contrast between Ahl al-Haqq and the Khula'a.
The passage includes an editorial comment by Nasafi.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where Ahl al-Haqq states their view and where the Khula'a/Sufi objection begins.
The Khula'a argument is that commanding and prohibiting is foolish.
3
Understand the Khula'a reasoning
Why do they say it is foolish? Because there is no benefit for the commander or prohibitor.
This is based on a flawed assumption that divine actions must be self-interested.
4
Note the author's attack
Al-Nasafi criticizes those who disguise themselves as Sufis. Understand his point about exposing hidden consciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The discourse on establishing prophethood and messengerhood: Is it from wisdom to send messengers? And is sending permissible? Ahl al-Haqq said: Sending messengers is wisdom and correctness, and it is permissible, and Allah Almighty sent messengers as bringers of good tidings and warners, and supported them with proofs. And groups of the Khula'a, among them the Sufis in the opinion of al-Nasafi, said it is impossible because it involves command and prohibition which is foolishness, since there is no benefit for the commander and the prohibitor. And here al-Nasafi attacks the Sufis saying: When the cover is removed from the hidden consciences of many who conceal themselves by Sufism, and the concealment is lifted from their hidden secrets, it will be known that they are from this sect. And among those who say that sending messengers is impossible are the Barahima, who affirm that reason knows good deeds and does them, and knows evil deeds and does not do them, so obligation is by reason and there is no need for messengers because if they come in agreement with reason then reason suffices of them, and if they come in opposition to it then their sending is futile because reason is the proof of Allah and His proofs do not contradict. And among them also are the Naturalists who say: The false prophet opposes the prophet if the matter is in accordance with reality, but if it departs from reality then it involves changing the natures of things, and the natures of things do not change; and some of them said that changing natures is within the power of Allah, but it is also within the power of sorcerers and it is impossible to distinguish between the two matters. And among the deniers are those who say: That which is said to be a miracle has not been presented to all of humanity, and if it were presented a challenger would be found, and thus there is no messenger.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a classification of different views on the possibility and wisdom of sending messengers. It begins by stating the topic: establishing prophethood and messengerhood, and the question of whether sending messengers is wise and permissible. Ahl al-Haqq affirm that it is wisdom and permissible. Then it presents three groups of deniers: (1) The Khula'a and some Sufis (according to Nasafi) who deem it impossible because it involves command and prohibition which they consider foolishness; Nasafi criticizes them as pretenders. (2) The Barahima, who argue that reason alone is sufficient to know good and evil, so messengers are either redundant (if agreeing with reason) or contradictory (if opposing). (3) The Naturalists, who argue that true prophets do not contradict reality, and if a claim involves changing natures it could be sorcery, and miracles are not universally testable. The passage ends with the argument that if miracles are not shown to all, no messenger can be established.

Key Terms
النبوة
الرسالة
أهل الحق
الخُلَعَاء
المتصوفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, sending messengers is:
A) Impossible
B) Foolishness
C) Wisdom and correctness
D) Necessary for God

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Haqq affirm that sending prophets is an act of divine wisdom, while the Khula'a and some Sufis deny it based on a flawed understanding of divine commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever inwardly questioned the wisdom of a divine command, feeling it was burdensome or pointless? In that moment, were you not sharing the Khula'a's assumption that commands must have an immediate benefit to be valuable? Today, before you pray or obey any command, pause and say: 'This is from the wisdom of Allah, even if I do not see the benefit.' Will you make that commitment?
?
The Khula'a argued that commanding and prohibiting is foolish because it does not benefit Allah. Do you find yourself obeying Allah only when you personally gain something, or when it is easy? What would it mean for you to obey purely because it is wisdom from your Creator, regardless of any tangible reward? Is there a specific command you have been neglecting because you thought it pointless? How will you act on it today?
?
When you read al-Nasafi's attack on those who hide behind Sufism to reject prophetic guidance, does it make you examine your own heart? Do you ever twist religious concepts to avoid obligations? Ask yourself: 'Am I truly submitting to the message of the prophets, or am I selectively following only what suits me?' Let this question guide your next act of obedience.

Prophethood

The Barahima Objection: Reason Suffices
Barahima's denial of prophethood based on sufficiency of reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Barahima's argument that reason alone is sufficient for knowing good and evil, making messengers redundant or contradictory.

Core Idea
If reason knows good and evil, prophets are either unnecessary or illogical.

The Barahima, an Indian school of thought, argued that human reason can independently discern moral values. Therefore, prophets are either redundant if they agree with reason, or contradictory if they oppose it. This lesson presents their objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Barahima argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice their key premise: reason knows good and evil.
2
Understand the two alternatives
They say if prophets agree with reason, reason is enough; if they disagree, their sending is futile.
This is a logical dilemma.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
They conclude that reason is God's proof and cannot contradict other proofs.
This challenges the need for revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the Barahima, what is the role of reason?
A) It cannot know good and evil
B) It knows good and evil and suffices for obligation
C) It is unnecessary
D) It contradicts revelation

The Barahima argue that reason alone can guide morality, making prophets either redundant or contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever trusted your own reasoning over a clear prophetic teaching? What was the result, and what does that reveal about your heart's posture toward revelation?
?
If you were to honestly examine your life, in what area do you act as if your reason is sufficient and prophets are unnecessary? What step will you take this week to submit that area to divine guidance?
?
The Barahima argue that reason and revelation cannot contradict. But have you ever dismissed a command from Allah because it seemed 'irrational' to you? How can you cultivate trust in revelation even when it challenges your intellect?

Prophethood

The Naturalists Objection: Changing Natures and Sorcery
Naturalists' objection: miracles either conform to reality or involve change of nature indistinguishable from sorcery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Naturalists' argument that true prophets do not contradict reality, and that claims of changing natures could be sorcery, undermining the distinction between miracle and magic.

Core Idea
If a miracle changes nature, it might be sorcery, so you cannot tell a true prophet from a magician.

The Naturalists raise two objections: first, a true prophet must align with reality; second, if a miracle changes the nature of things, it could be sorcery, and there is no way to distinguish between divine miracle and magic. This lesson presents their skeptical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Naturalists' first argument
They say the false prophet opposes reality, but the true prophet does not. Understand their view of miracles.
2
Read the second argument
Some Naturalists admit God can change natures, but sorcerers can too, creating confusion.
This challenges the evidential value of miracles.
3
Reflect on the distinction problem
Think about how to differentiate a miracle from sorcery according to this objection.

Source Text

Ahl al-Haqq affirm that it is wisdom and permissible. Then it presents three groups of deniers: (1) The Khula'a and some Sufis (according to Nasafi) who deem it impossible because it involves command and prohibition which they consider foolishness; Nasafi criticizes them as pretenders. (2) The Barahima, who argue that reason alone is sufficient to know good and evil, so messengers are either redundant (if agreeing with reason) or contradictory (if opposing). (3) The Naturalists, who argue that true prophets do not contradict reality, and if a claim involves changing natures it could be sorcery, and miracles are not universally testable. The passage ends with the argument that if miracles are not shown to all, no messenger can be established.

Key Terms
الطبيعيون
المتنبي
النبي
طبائع الأشياء
السحرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think miracles are impossible; Naturalists admit they could be possible but indistinguishable from magic.
Correction: The objection is not that miracles cannot happen, but that they cannot be recognized as such.

Checkpoint
What is the Naturalists' second argument regarding miracles?
A) Miracles are always false
B) Changing natures is impossible even for God
C) Changing natures is possible for both God and sorcerers, making them indistinguishable
D) Miracles are not needed

Key Takeaway
The Naturalists argue that even if miracles can happen, they cannot be reliably distinguished from sorcery, thus weakening their proof for prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle, what is the basis of your certainty that it is from Allah and not from magic? This lesson shows that external signs alone may be insufficient. Do you have an internal conviction that goes beyond appearances? Search your heart: is your faith rooted in the message itself or only in extraordinary events?
?
The Naturalists argue that miracles cannot be distinguished from sorcery. How would you respond to someone who says that all claims of miracles are just tricks? Without relying on a previous belief in Islam, how would you convince them? This forces you to think: what is the ultimate proof of prophethood for you personally?
?
Imagine you witnessed an event that seems to break the laws of nature. Would you automatically follow the person who performed it? This lesson challenges you to examine your own criteria. Are you easily impressed by the supernatural, or do you judge by the content of the message? Be honest: have you ever been swayed by a charismatic speaker or a 'miracle' without verifying the truth of their teaching?

The Universal Miracles Objection
Denier argument that miracles lack universal witness, so no prophet is proven

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if a miracle is not shown to all people, it cannot prove prophethood because an opponent could always be found.

Core Idea
A miracle must be witnessed by all to be proof; otherwise, it is not conclusive.

This final objection from the passage argues that alleged miracles have not been presented to all of humanity. If they were, someone would have challenged them, so no messenger can be established. This lesson explores that skeptical point.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation. The deniers claim that miracles are not universally witnessed.
2
Understand the assumption
They assume that if a miracle were genuine, it would be shown to all and challenged by someone.
This is a hypothetical challenge.
3
Consider the implication
Because no universal presentation occurred, they conclude no prophet is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إثبات النبوة والرسالة هل من الحكمة إرسال الرسل ؟ وهل الإرسال جائز؟ قال أهل الحق : إن إرسال الرسل حكمة وصواب، وهو جائز، وقد أرسل الله تعالى رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين، وأيدهم بالحجج. وقالت طوائف من الخُلَعَاء ومنهم المتصوفة في رأي النسفي بامتناعه إذ فيه أمر ونهي وهو سفه، إذ لا نفع للآمر والناهي، وهنا يهاجم النسفي المتصوفة بقوله: وعند اكتشاف القناع عن مكنون ضمائر كثير من المستترين بالتصوف، وارتفاع الخفاء عن مستور سرائرهم، ويعلم أنه من هذه الفرقة. ومن الذين يقولون بامتناع إرسال الرسل البراهمة، حيث يؤكدون أن العقل يعرف المحاسن فيعملها، ويعرف المساوئ فلا يعملها فالتكليف يكون بالعقل ولا حاجة إلى الرسل لأنهم إن جاءوا موافقين للعقل فبالعقل عنهم غنية، وإن جاءوا مخالفين له كان إرسالهم عبثا لأن العقل حجة الله وحججه تعالى لا تتناقض. ومنهم أيضا الطبيعيون حيث يقولون : إن المتنبي يعارض النبي إذا كان الأمر على مقتضى الواقع، أما إذا خرج عن مقتضى الواقع ففيه تغيير طبائع الأشياء، وطبائع الأشياء لا تتغير وقال بعضهم بأن قلب الطبائع في مقدور الله، إلا أنه أيضا في مقدور السحرة ولا يمكن التمييز بين الأمرين. ومن المنكرين من يقول : بأن ما يقال إنه معجزة لم يعرض على كل البشر، ولو عرض لوجد المعارض، وبذلك لا رسول
The discourse on establishing prophethood and messengerhood: Is it from wisdom to send messengers? And is sending permissible? Ahl al-Haqq said: Sending messengers is wisdom and correctness, and it is permissible, and Allah Almighty sent messengers as bringers of good tidings and warners, and supported them with proofs. And groups of the Khula'a, among them the Sufis in the opinion of al-Nasafi, said it is impossible because it involves command and prohibition which is foolishness, since there is no benefit for the commander and the prohibitor. And here al-Nasafi attacks the Sufis saying: When the cover is removed from the hidden consciences of many who conceal themselves by Sufism, and the concealment is lifted from their hidden secrets, it will be known that they are from this sect. And among those who say that sending messengers is impossible are the Barahima, who affirm that reason knows good deeds and does them, and knows evil deeds and does not do them, so obligation is by reason and there is no need for messengers because if they come in agreement with reason then reason suffices of them, and if they come in opposition to it then their sending is futile because reason is the proof of Allah and His proofs do not contradict. And among them also are the Naturalists who say: The false prophet opposes the prophet if the matter is in accordance with reality, but if it departs from reality then it involves changing the natures of things, and the natures of things do not change; and some of them said that changing natures is within the power of Allah, but it is also within the power of sorcerers and it is impossible to distinguish between the two matters. And among the deniers are those who say: That which is said to be a miracle has not been presented to all of humanity, and if it were presented a challenger would be found, and thus there is no messenger.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a classification of different views on the possibility and wisdom of sending messengers. It begins by stating the topic: establishing prophethood and messengerhood, and the question of whether sending messengers is wise and permissible. Ahl al-Haqq affirm that it is wisdom and permissible. Then it presents three groups of deniers: (1) The Khula'a and some Sufis (according to Nasafi) who deem it impossible because it involves command and prohibition which they consider foolishness; Nasafi criticizes them as pretenders. (2) The Barahima, who argue that reason alone is sufficient to know good and evil, so messengers are either redundant (if agreeing with reason) or contradictory (if opposing). (3) The Naturalists, who argue that true prophets do not contradict reality, and if a claim involves changing natures it could be sorcery, and miracles are not universally testable. The passage ends with the argument that if miracles are not shown to all, no messenger can be established.

Key Terms
المعجزة
البشر
المعارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the deniers' claim about miracles?
A) Miracles do not exist
B) Miracles have been shown to all and challenged
C) Miracles have not been shown to all, so no messenger is proven
D) Miracles are only for prophets

Key Takeaway
The deniers argue that because miracles are not universally witnessed and challenged, they cannot serve as proof of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly doubted a miracle of the Prophet ﷺ because you didn't witness it yourself? How does this lesson confront that doubt? Will you now submit to the testimony of reliable transmitters, or continue to demand your own universal proof?
?
If you were to apply the denier's standard of evidence to your own beliefs about Allah, how many of them would survive? Are you willing to accept that revelation requires a kind of witness different from physical sight?
?
When you read this objection, did you feel a flicker of agreement—that maybe miracles are not enough? Where does that impulse come from in your heart? Ask Allah to purify it and resolve now to cling to the prophetic proofs even when your eyes have not seen.

The Definition of Prophethood as Mediation
Al-Nasafi defines prophethood and its purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of prophethood as mediation (sifara) between God and people with intellect, and its purpose to remedy what reason cannot grasp.

Core Idea
Prophethood is God's mediation to complete what reason alone cannot achieve.

Al-Nasafi begins his discussion of prophethood by defining it clearly. This definition frames the entire debate: prophethood is not an arbitrary intervention but a purposeful mediation for human benefit. Let's see how he establishes this foundational concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic phrase defining prophethood as 'sifara' (mediation). Notice the terms 'safara' and 'dhawi al-albab' (those of understanding).
2
Understand the purpose
The definition includes a purpose: 'to remove their defects in what their minds fall short of concerning the benefits of their abode.' Reflect on why this purpose is essential.
This sets up the need for revelation.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Ask yourself: if reason were sufficient, would prophethood be needed? This definition implies it is not.
This is the starting point for proving necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر النسفي حل شبه الخصوم، ثم يبدأ في إقامة الدلالة على صحة الرسالة فيعرفنا أولا بأنها سفارة العبد بين الله تعالى وبين ذوي الألباب، ليزيح بها عللهم فيما قصرت عنه عقولهم من مصالح دارهم. ويبين ثانيا أنها ممكنة، فإن ورود التكليف بالإيجاب والحظر ممن له الملك ليس مما تأباه العقول، والمالك يتصرف في ملكه أين شاء، ومتى شاء، وكيف شاء، وبأي وجه يريد سواء بإعطاء العقل قدرة معرفة كل شيء، أو بإرسال الرسل. وبعد أن يقيم الأدلة على أن الرسالة ممكنة يناقش شبه الخصوم، ليثبت أن إرسال الرسل ليس بمستحيل في نفسه، ولا سفه في ذاته، وهو في حيز الممكنات. ثم هل الرسالة من قبل الممكنات في العقل أم هي من جملة الواجبات؟ يقول النسفي : إن متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى القلانسي قالوا: إنها من جملة الممكنات. ويذهب القائلون بأن العقل آلة معرفة الحسن و القبح ووجوب شكر النعم إلى القول بوجوبها، لأنها من مقتضيات حكمة الحكيم وهو الله، ويستحيل أن لا يوجد ما كان وجوده من مقتضيات حكمته، لما أن انعدامه يدخل في باب السفه. ويستطرد النسفي في إقامة الأدلة رادا كل كلام الخصوم، مثبتا أن العقل مهما بلغ فإنه لا يستطيع الاستقلال بمعرفة كل شيء، وذلك لأنه يلحقه الضعف، وإذا كان الناس ينقسمون إلى ثلاثة أقسام. همهم موصوفون بالذكاء متفرغون للبحث، وآخرون موصوفون بالذكاء إلا أن جل ما فيه مصلحة الدنيا، وآخرون موصوفون ببلادة الأفهام. فإن القسم الأول من الندرة، والقسمين الآخرين هم أكثر الناس، ولا يستطيعون معرفة أمور دنياهم ودينهم إلا بالرسل، وهكذا يستمر النسفي في حديث
And al-Nasafi mentions the solution to the doubts of the opponents, then begins to establish the evidence for the validity of messengerhood, informing us first that it is the mediation of the servant between Allah Almighty and those of understanding, to remove their defects in what their minds fall short of concerning the benefits of their abode. And he shows second that it is possible, for the occurrence of obligation by command and prohibition from the One who possesses dominion is not something that minds reject, and the owner disposes in his dominion as he wills, when he wills, how he wills, and in any manner he desires, whether by giving the mind the ability to know everything, or by sending messengers. And after he establishes the proofs that messengerhood is possible, he discusses the doubts of the opponents to prove that sending messengers is not impossible in itself, nor foolishness in itself, and it is in the realm of possibilities. Then: Is messengerhood among the possibilities in reason or is it among the obligations? Al-Nasafi says: The theologians of Ahl al-Hadith except al-Qalanisi said that it is among the possibilities. And those who claim that reason is an instrument for knowing good and evil and the obligation of gratitude for blessings go to the opinion of its obligation, because it is from the requirements of the wisdom of the Wise, who is Allah, and it is impossible for that whose existence is from the requirements of His wisdom not to exist, because its non-existence enters into the domain of foolishness. And al-Nasafi continues in establishing proofs, refuting all speech of the opponents, affirming that reason, no matter how far it reaches, cannot independently know everything, because weakness affects it. And if people are divided into three categories: their concern—those described with intelligence, devoted to research; others described with intelligence but most of their concern is worldly benefit; and others described with dullness of understanding. Indeed the first category is rare, and the other two categories are most people, and they cannot know matters of their world and their religion except through messengers. And thus al-Nasafi continues in his discourse.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a structured presentation of prophethood. First, it defines prophethood as mediation (sifara) between God and those of understanding, to remedy what their minds cannot grasp of their benefits. Second, it proves that prophethood is possible (mumkin) because the Owner of dominion can command and forbid as He wishes, and reason does not reject that. The owner may give reason full knowledge or send messengers. After establishing possibility, al-Nasafi moves to the question: is it merely possible or obligatory? He reports that most Ahl al-Hadith theologians except al-Qalanisi say it is possible. Those who hold that reason knows good and evil and that gratitude is obligatory argue that it is obligatory because it follows from God's wisdom. Al-Nasafi then continues to refute opponents by arguing that reason is weak and cannot know everything independently. He divides people into three categories: (1) intelligent and devoted to research (rare), (2) intelligent but focused on worldly matters, and (3) dull-minded. The latter two categories are the majority, and they need messengers to know matters of religion and worldly life.

Key Terms
سفارة
ذوي الألباب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of prophethood according to al-Nasafi?
A) A command from God without reason
B) The mediation of a servant between God and those of understanding
C) The ability of reason to know everything
D) A social contract among people

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is defined as a purposeful mediation to supply what reason cannot know.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a question about the purpose of your life that your mind cannot fully resolve, do you genuinely turn to prophethood as the mediation that fills that gap? What concrete step can you take this week to rely more on prophetic guidance than on your own reasoning in that area?
?
Al-Nasafi says prophethood removes defects in what reason falls short of. What specific defect in your understanding of the afterlife has reason alone left unresolved, and how does the prophet’s mediation address it? Are you actively seeking that remedy through learning and reflection?
?
The purpose clause says prophethood supplies knowledge about 'the benefits of their abode'—this world and the next. Are you living as though prophethood indeed guides you to those benefits, or do you rely more on personal judgment? What would change in your daily decisions if you made prophetic guidance your primary lens for success in both worlds?

The Rational Possibility of Prophethood
Al-Nasafi argues prophethood is rationally possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that prophethood is possible because God, as Owner, may command and forbid as He wills.

Core Idea
If God is the Owner, nothing prevents Him from sending messengers.

After defining prophethood, al-Nasafi turns to prove its possibility. He employs a simple but powerful analogy: an owner may act in his property as he wishes. This establishes that prophethood is not rationally impossible. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for possibility
Read the Arabic passage showing that obligation (taklif) from the Owner is not rejected by reason.
Key term: 'al-malik' (the Owner) refers to God.
2
Understand the owner analogy
Al-Nasafi says the owner disposes in his dominion as he wills. Compare this to God's sovereignty. Ask: can an owner restrict or command in his own property?
This establishes God's absolute right to command.
3
Consider the two modes
The passage mentions two ways God can guide: by giving reason full knowledge or by sending messengers. Note that both are within His power.
Possibility does not require actual sending; it just means it is not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Obligation vs. Possibility: The Debate on Prophethood
Al-Nasafi presents the debate on whether prophethood is possible or obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate over whether prophethood is rationally obligatory or merely possible, and the grounds for each position.

Core Idea
Prophethood is either possible or obligatory, depending on how one views reason's capacity.

After establishing possibility, al-Nasafi presents a key dispute: is prophethood merely possible (mumkin) or obligatory (wajib) by reason? He reports two camps: most Ahl al-Hadith say possible, while those who affirm rational knowledge of good/evil and gratitude say obligatory. Let's see the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage and find the two views: (1) most Ahl al-Hadith say it is possible; (2) others (rationalists) say it is obligatory.
Note the exception: al-Qalanisi is excluded from the first group.
2
Understand the rationalist argument
Those who say obligatory base it on God's wisdom. If something is required by wisdom, its absence would be foolishness. So prophethood must exist.
This links to the broader discussion of divine attributes.
3
Reflect on implications
Ask: does this debate affect how we view reason's role? The rationalist view gives reason more authority.
Al-Nasafi later critiques this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر النسفي حل شبه الخصوم، ثم يبدأ في إقامة الدلالة على صحة الرسالة فيعرفنا أولا بأنها سفارة العبد بين الله تعالى وبين ذوي الألباب، ليزيح بها عللهم فيما قصرت عنه عقولهم من مصالح دارهم. ويبين ثانيا أنها ممكنة، فإن ورود التكليف بالإيجاب والحظر ممن له الملك ليس مما تأباه العقول، والمالك يتصرف في ملكه أين شاء، ومتى شاء، وكيف شاء، وبأي وجه يريد سواء بإعطاء العقل قدرة معرفة كل شيء، أو بإرسال الرسل. وبعد أن يقيم الأدلة على أن الرسالة ممكنة يناقش شبه الخصوم، ليثبت أن إرسال الرسل ليس بمستحيل في نفسه، ولا سفه في ذاته، وهو في حيز الممكنات. ثم هل الرسالة من قبل الممكنات في العقل أم هي من جملة الواجبات؟ يقول النسفي : إن متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى القلانسي قالوا: إنها من جملة الممكنات. ويذهب القائلون بأن العقل آلة معرفة الحسن و القبح ووجوب شكر النعم إلى القول بوجوبها، لأنها من مقتضيات حكمة الحكيم وهو الله، ويستحيل أن لا يوجد ما كان وجوده من مقتضيات حكمته، لما أن انعدامه يدخل في باب السفه. ويستطرد النسفي في إقامة الأدلة رادا كل كلام الخصوم، مثبتا أن العقل مهما بلغ فإنه لا يستطيع الاستقلال بمعرفة كل شيء، وذلك لأنه يلحقه الضعف، وإذا كان الناس ينقسمون إلى ثلاثة أقسام. همهم موصوفون بالذكاء متفرغون للبحث، وآخرون موصوفون بالذكاء إلا أن جل ما فيه مصلحة الدنيا، وآخرون موصوفون ببلادة الأفهام. فإن القسم الأول من الندرة، والقسمين الآخرين هم أكثر الناس، ولا يستطيعون معرفة أمور دنياهم ودينهم إلا بالرسل، وهكذا يستمر النسفي في حديث
And al-Nasafi mentions the solution to the doubts of the opponents, then begins to establish the evidence for the validity of messengerhood, informing us first that it is the mediation of the servant between Allah Almighty and those of understanding, to remove their defects in what their minds fall short of concerning the benefits of their abode. And he shows second that it is possible, for the occurrence of obligation by command and prohibition from the One who possesses dominion is not something that minds reject, and the owner disposes in his dominion as he wills, when he wills, how he wills, and in any manner he desires, whether by giving the mind the ability to know everything, or by sending messengers. And after he establishes the proofs that messengerhood is possible, he discusses the doubts of the opponents to prove that sending messengers is not impossible in itself, nor foolishness in itself, and it is in the realm of possibilities. Then: Is messengerhood among the possibilities in reason or is it among the obligations? Al-Nasafi says: The theologians of Ahl al-Hadith except al-Qalanisi said that it is among the possibilities. And those who claim that reason is an instrument for knowing good and evil and the obligation of gratitude for blessings go to the opinion of its obligation, because it is from the requirements of the wisdom of the Wise, who is Allah, and it is impossible for that whose existence is from the requirements of His wisdom not to exist, because its non-existence enters into the domain of foolishness. And al-Nasafi continues in establishing proofs, refuting all speech of the opponents, affirming that reason, no matter how far it reaches, cannot independently know everything, because weakness affects it. And if people are divided into three categories: their concern—those described with intelligence, devoted to research; others described with intelligence but most of their concern is worldly benefit; and others described with dullness of understanding. Indeed the first category is rare, and the other two categories are most people, and they cannot know matters of their world and their religion except through messengers. And thus al-Nasafi continues in his discourse.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a structured presentation of prophethood. First, it defines prophethood as mediation (sifara) between God and those of understanding, to remedy what their minds cannot grasp of their benefits. Second, it proves that prophethood is possible (mumkin) because the Owner of dominion can command and forbid as He wishes, and reason does not reject that. The owner may give reason full knowledge or send messengers. After establishing possibility, al-Nasafi moves to the question: is it merely possible or obligatory? He reports that most Ahl al-Hadith theologians except al-Qalanisi say it is possible. Those who hold that reason knows good and evil and that gratitude is obligatory argue that it is obligatory because it follows from God's wisdom. Al-Nasafi then continues to refute opponents by arguing that reason is weak and cannot know everything independently. He divides people into three categories: (1) intelligent and devoted to research (rare), (2) intelligent but focused on worldly matters, and (3) dull-minded. The latter two categories are the majority, and they need messengers to know matters of religion and worldly life.

Key Terms
الممكنات
الواجبات
متكلمي أهل الحديث
القلانسي
الحسن و القبح
شكر النعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the debate is about whether God must send messengers (i.e., obligating God).
Correction: The debate is about rational judgment: is the sending rationally necessary (obligatory) or merely possible? It is not about divine obligation in the sense of God being compelled.

Checkpoint
What is the position of most Ahl al-Hadith theologians (except al-Qalanisi) regarding messengerhood?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is impossible
C) It is possible
D) It is necessary for reason

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is viewed as possible by most Ahl al-Hadith, but rationally obligatory by those who affirm reason's ability to know good/evil and gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the countless blessings in your life—health, food, faith—do you feel a pressing need to know and thank the One who gave them? The passage tells us that gratitude itself implies a duty to seek the Giver. Have you acted on that duty today, or do you let blessings pass without acknowledgment?
?
The view that prophethood is obligatory argues that it would be foolish for God not to send messengers. Do you live as though you truly believe that God has sent a clear message to you through the Prophet? Or do you treat prophethood as a mere possibility—relevant but not essential? What would change in your daily choices if you saw every command of the Prophet as a necessary part of God's wisdom?
?
Debates about mental possibility versus obligation can feel abstract. But the outcome shapes how seriously you take revelation. If you agree with the 'possible' view, do you unconsciously lower the urgency of obedience? If you lean toward 'obligatory', does it create a sense of accountability that actually moves you to act? Which view currently governs your heart in the quiet moments of decision?

Reason's Limitations and the Necessity of Revelation
Al-Nasafi argues for the necessity of prophets due to human intellectual diversity and limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's argument that reason is limited and most people need prophets to know religious and worldly matters.

Core Idea
Reason is too weak and most people need messengers for guidance.

Continuing his defense of prophethood, al-Nasafi now argues for its necessity from human limitation. He classifies people into three types based on intellectual ability and shows that only a tiny minority can independently reason about deep matters. The majority requires prophetic guidance. This passage grounds the need for revelation in human psychology and sociology.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of people
Find the three categories: (1) intelligent and devoted to research, (2) intelligent but worldly-focused, (3) dull-minded.
Note the term 'bulad al-afham' for dullness.
2
Assess the implications
The first category is rare; the other two are the majority. Why does this imply need for prophets?
Consider that even the second category cannot fully grasp religious matters.
3
Connect to the broader conclusion
Al-Nasafi states that these people cannot know matters of their world and religion except through messengers. This is a practical necessity.
The argument is not just theoretical but empirical.

Debate on Whether Prophethood is Possible or Obligatory
Al-Nasafi presents the views of Ahl al-Hadith and the opposing school on the modality of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main rational positions on prophethood—possibility and obligation—and how they are grounded in different epistemological commitments.

Core Idea
Prophethood's modality hinges on whether reason can judge good and evil.

Al-Nasafi presents a key dispute among Muslim theologians: is prophethood rationally possible or obligatory? After establishing that prophethood is not impossible, he reports two positions. The majority of Ahl al-Hadith deem it possible, while those who hold that reason can discern good and evil argue it is obligatory based on God's wisdom. This passage sets the stage for deeper theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Nasafi frames the central question after establishing possibility.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ثم هل الرسالة من قبل الممكنات في العقل أم هي من جملة الواجبات؟'—this is the pivot.
2
Identify the two views and their proponents
Find the two distinct positions: one attributed to 'mutakallimi ahl al-hadith siwa al-Qalanisi' and the other to 'al-qa'iluna bi anna al-aqila alat ma'rifat al-husn wa al-qubh'. Note who holds each view.
Al-Qalanisi is an exception—he is an Ash'ari theologian with a different view.
3
Examine the reasoning for each view
For the possibility view, it is simply stated as a position. For the obligation view, the reasoning is given: it is required by God's wisdom, and failing to send messengers would be foolishness. Understand why the opponent considers this 'safah'.
The obligation view relies on the principle that God must act wisely, and wisdom entails sending messengers.
4
Reflect on the epistemological divergence
Consider what underlies these two positions: one group trusts reason to know good/evil and obligation of gratitude, the other does not. This is a fundamental difference in Islamic theology.
This debate is not just about prophethood but about the role of reason in ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد أن يقيم الأدلة على أن الرسالة ممكنة يناقش شبه الخصوم، ليثبت أن إرسال الرسل ليس بمستحيل في نفسه، ولا سفه في ذاته، وهو في حيز الممكنات. ثم هل الرسالة من قبل الممكنات في العقل أم هي من جملة الواجبات؟ يقول النسفي : إن متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى القلانسي قالوا: إنها من جملة الممكنات. ويذهب القائلون بأن العقل آلة معرفة الحسن و القبح ووجوب شكر النعم إلى القول بوجوبها، لأنها من مقتضيات حكمة الحكيم وهو الله، ويستحيل أن لا يوجد ما كان وجوده من مقتضيات حكمته، لما أن انعدامه يدخل في باب السفه.
And after he establishes the proofs that the message is possible, he discusses the doubts of the opponents, to establish that sending messengers is not impossible in itself, nor foolishness in its essence, and it is within the realm of possibles. Then, is the message from the category of possibles according to reason, or is it from the category of obligations? Al-Nasafi says: Indeed, the theologians (mutakallimi) of Ahl al-Hadith except al-Qalanisi said: It is from the category of possibles. And those who hold that reason is an instrument for knowing good and evil and the obligation of gratitude for blessings go to the view of its obligation, because it is from the requirements of the wisdom of the Wise, Who is God, and it is impossible that what exists as a requirement of His wisdom should not exist, because its non-existence enters into the category of foolishness.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the debate on whether prophethood is rationally possible or obligatory. After proving possibility, al-Nasafi engages opponent doubts to confirm sending messengers is neither impossible nor foolish. He then poses the central question: is risala from the category of possible things (mumkinat) or obligatory things (wajibat)? He reports that the Mutakallimi of Ahl al-Hadith (except al-Qalanisi) say it is possible. The opposing view, held by those who affirm reason's ability to know good and evil and the obligation to thank God, argues that prophethood is obligatory because it stems from God's wisdom. For them, failing to send messengers would contradict wisdom and constitute foolishness (safah), which is impossible for God.

Key Terms
الممكنات
الواجبات
الحسن و القبح
الحكمة
السفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The view that prophethood is 'obligatory' implies that God is compelled or forced to act, compromising His free will.
Correction: The obligation is a rational necessity based on God's attribute of wisdom, not an external compulsion. It is impossible for God to act foolishly; thus, wisdom necessitates sending messengers, but this necessity is from God's own nature, not external constraint.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the position of the Mutakallimi Ahl al-Hadith (except al-Qalanisi) regarding prophethood?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is impossible
C) It is possible
D) It is rationally necessary

Key Takeaway
The debate over prophethood's modality hinges on whether reason alone can determine God's actions, with the obligation view relying on the principle of wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If reason could prove that God must send messengers, would your faith be more intellectual or more trusting? Which kind of faith does this passage challenge you to cultivate?
?
When you thank God for His blessings, do you feel it as a rational duty or a heartfelt response? How does this lesson's discussion on 'the obligation of gratitude' (وجوب شكر النعم) confront your daily attitude toward Allah?
?
If prophethood were only 'possible' and not 'obligatory,' would you still feel an urgent need to study the life and teachings of the Prophet? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on God's grace versus your own reasoning?

Three Intellectual Categories and the Need for Prophets
Al-Nasafi divides people into three categories to prove universal need for prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses a threefold classification of human intellects to argue that all people, regardless of intelligence, are in need of prophetic guidance.

Core Idea
Even the sharpest mind cannot know everything—revelation is for everyone.

Al-Nasafi anticipates an objection: if human reason is so powerful, why need prophets? He responds by classifying people into three intellectual types. The most intelligent are rare and cannot know all truths; the majority, preoccupied or dull, definitely need guidance. This passage establishes the universal necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three categories al-Nasafi lists.
The passage is a continuous argument; don't skip the conclusion.
2
Identify the Three Categories
List the three types: intelligent and devoted to research, intelligent but worldly, and dull-minded. Note that the first is rare.
3
Understand the Universal Need
Why does even the first category need prophets? Because they cannot know 'the realities of everything.'
This closes the loophole: no one is self-sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستطرد النسفي في إقامة الأدلة رادا كل كلام الخصوم، مثبتا أن العقل مهما بلغ فإنه لا يستطيع الاستقلال بمعرفة كل شيء، وذلك لأنه يلحقه الضعف، وإذا كان الناس ينقسمون إلى ثلاثة أقسام. همهم موصوفون بالذكاء متفرغون للبحث، وآخرون موصوفون بالذكاء إلا أن جل ما فيه مصلحة الدنيا، وآخرون موصوفون ببلادة الأفهام. فإن القسم الأول من الندرة، والقسمين الآخرين هم أكثر الناس، ولا يستطيعون معرفة أمور دنياهم ودينهم إلا بالرسل، وهكذا يستمر النسفي في حديث شامل لكل الاتجاهات، ويشمل كذلك إثبات أن الصنف الأول في حاجة كبيرة إلى إرسال الرسل، لأنهم لا يستطيعون معرفة حقائق كل شيء.
And al-Nasafi continues in establishing the proofs, refuting all speech of the opponents, affirming that the intellect, no matter how far it reaches, cannot independently know everything, because weakness afflicts it. And when people are divided into three categories: (1) those described with intelligence and devoted to research, (2) others described with intelligence but the majority of their concern is the benefit of this world, and (3) others described with dullness of understanding. The first category is rare, and the other two categories are the majority of people, and they cannot know the matters of their world and their religion except through messengers. Thus al-Nasafi continues in a comprehensive discussion covering all directions, and also includes proving that the first category is in great need of sending messengers, because they cannot know the realities of everything.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage contains al-Nasafi's argument that human intellect is insufficient for complete knowledge, thus prophethood is necessary. He divides people into three categories regarding intellect: (1) intelligent and dedicated to research, (2) intelligent but mostly preoccupied with worldly benefits, and (3) dull of understanding. The first group is rare; the other two are the majority and cannot know their worldly and religious affairs without messengers. Al-Nasafi even stresses that the first group is also in great need of prophets because even they cannot know all realities. Thus, the need for revelation is universal.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Nasafi's argument is elitist—only the unintelligent need prophets.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explicitly states that even the most intelligent researchers need prophets because they cannot know all realities.

?

?
The first category is rare and devoted to research, yet still in need of prophets. If you consider yourself a seeker of knowledge, does your study of Islam lead you to greater reliance on revelation or a false sense of self-sufficiency? What step can you take to humble your intellect before the divine message?

Al-Nasafi's Refutation of the Obligation of Al-Aslah
Al-Nasafi denies any obligation on God and affirms His gratuitous generosity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi denies that anything is obligatory upon God, particularly the Mu'tazili claim of al-aslah, and how this affirms God's gratuitous generosity.

Core Idea
Nothing is obligatory upon God; He is the Creator and Bestower.

The Mu'tazila argued that reason can know good and evil and that God must do what is most beneficial for His servants. Al-Nasafi counters this by asserting that God is not bound by any obligation; He is the Creator who bestows favors freely. This passage establishes the Maturidi position on divine generosity against Mu'tazili rationalism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Note the two opposing views presented.
The passage is short but dense with theological implications.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili claim
Notice that the Mu'tazila say sending messengers is obligatory because it is al-aslah (most beneficial) for servants, and it is obligatory upon God to do al-aslah.
Al-aslah is a key term meaning 'the most beneficial'.
3
Grasp al-Nasafi's counter
Al-Nasafi states: 'Nothing is obligatory upon God, because He is the Creator, the Bestower.' Understand why this refutes the Mu'tazili position.
The term al-mutafaddil emphasizes gratuitous generosity.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this teaching protects God's absolute sovereignty and generosity. Ask: If God has no obligations, what does that say about His actions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينتهي النسفي إلى القول بإمكان استدراك الحسن و القبح بالعقل خلافا لقول المعتزلة الذين يقولون بوجوب إرسال الرسل لأنه الأصلح للعباد، ويجب على الله فعل الأصلح. أما النسفي يقول : لا يجب على الله شيء لأنه الخالق المتفضل.
And al-Nasafi concludes to the statement of the possibility of recognizing good and evil by reason, contrary to the statement of the Mu'tazila who say that sending messengers is obligatory because it is the most beneficial (al-aslah) for the servants, and it is obligatory upon God to do the most beneficial. As for al-Nasafi, he says: Nothing is obligatory upon God, because He is the Creator, the Bestower.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nasafi's position on the Mu'tazili doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial). The Mu'tazila argue that reason can know good and evil, and that it is obligatory for God to send messengers as the most beneficial act for humanity. Al-Nasafi rejects this by affirming that nothing is obligatory upon God; He is the Creator who bestows favors freely (al-mutafaddil). He holds that reason can recognize good and evil, but this does not impose obligations on God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Rejecting the obligation of al-aslah implies God is unjust or arbitrary.
Correction: Al-Nasafi affirms that God is gratuitously generous; His acts are not arbitrary but flow from His perfection.

Checkpoint

A) He agrees it is obligatory
B) He says it is possible but not obligatory
C) He says nothing is obligatory upon God

Key Takeaway

Think of a time you felt God 'should have' given you something you desired. What if you truly believed He is al-Mutafaddil, giving only out of His free generosity—no strings attached? Would that reshape your disappointment? What would you need to let go of to embrace that truth?
