Definition and Divisions of the Miracle
Al-Nasafi defines miracle, its basis in incapacity, and its two kinds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a miracle in Islamic theology, including its basis in incapacity, its role as a proof of prophethood, and its two types.

Core Idea
A miracle is a challenge proving prophethood through human incapacity to match it.

Al-Nasafi now turns to a crucial topic: the miracle (mu'jiza). In this passage, he gives its theological definition, identifies incapacity as its foundation, and distinguishes two kinds. Understanding this will clarify how miracles function as evidence for prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: basis, definition, divisions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the basis of a miracle
Al-Nasafi says the basis is 'al-'ajz' (incapacity). Ask yourself: why does a miracle require the inability of others to replicate it?
This distinguishes a true miracle from mere marvels.
3
Break down the definition piece by piece
The definition has several components: appearance contrary to custom, in the abode of responsibility, to show truthfulness, with others refusing and unable to oppose.
Each word is deliberate in kalam.
4
Distinguish the two types
One type is an unusual act (e.g., splitting the moon). The other is incapacitation from a usual act (e.g., failing to produce a Quran like it).
Both serve as proof, but through different mechanisms.
5
Reflect on the term 'nukul' (refusal)
Why does the definition include 'nukul'? Because a challenge must be issued; the miracle is not merely a wonder but a test in a context of opposition.
This connects to the concept of tahaddi (challenge).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك يتعرض للحديث عن المعجزة، فيبين لنا مآخذها، وحدها، وأقسامها، ووجه كيفية دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها. أما مآخذها فهو العجز وحدها عند المتكلمين : أنها ظهور أمر خلاف العادة في دار التكليف لإظهار صدق مدعي النبوة مع نكول وعجز الغير عن معارضته بمثله. وتنقسم إلى قسمين: الأول: فعل غير معتاد والثاني: تعجيز عن الفعل المعتاد.
Then after that he turns to discussing the miracle (al-mu'jiza), and explains to us its bases (makhadh), its definition (hadd), its divisions (aqsam), and the aspect of how it indicates the truthfulness of the one who brings it. As for its basis, it is incapacity (al-'ajz). Its definition according to the theologians: it is the appearance of a matter contrary to custom in the abode of responsibility (dar al-taklif) to show the truthfulness of the claimant of prophethood, along with the refusal (nukul) and incapacity of others to oppose it with its like. And it is divided into two kinds: first: an unusual act; second: incapacitation from a usual act.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines miracle (mu'jiza) and its divisions. Al-Nasafi explains its basis (makhadh: incapacity), its definition (hadd: a breach of custom in this world to affirm a prophet's truthfulness, with others unable to match it), and its two divisions: (1) an unusual act (fi'l ghayr mu'tad) like splitting the moon, and (2) incapacitation from a usual act (ta'jiz 'an al-fi'l al-mu'tad) like failing to produce a similar Quran.

Key Terms
المعجزة
العجز
خلاف العادة
دار التكليف
النكول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any supernatural event is automatically a miracle.
Correction: A miracle is specifically a supernatural event that occurs in a context of challenge (tahaddi) to prove prophethood, and others are unable to replicate it.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the basis (makhadh) of a miracle?
A) Divine power
B) Incapacity (al-'ajz)
C) Customary occurrence
D) Prophetic request

Key Takeaway
A miracle is a revealed sign of truth, defined by its basis in human incapacity and its function as a proof in a challenge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the miracle of the Qur’an, do you feel the weight of its challenge (tahaddi) in your own heart? Have you sincerely examined your own inability to produce something like it, and what conclusion did you reach about the truth of the message?
?
The basis of a miracle is your own incapacity. In what areas of your life do you rely on your own ability rather than recognizing that Allah’s signs confront you with your helplessness? What would change if you truly internalized that your inability to produce a like is a deliberate proof from your Lord?
?
Al-Nasafi says the miracle occurs in 'dar al-taklif'—this world of responsibility. If miracles are signs meant to guide you to truth, what specific responsibility do you bear after knowing that the Prophet came with such clear proofs? Have you responded to that responsibility with full submission?

The Possibility and Necessity of Muhammad's Prophethood
Al-Nasafi argues that Muhammad's prophethood was possible and necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi proves that Muhammad's prophethood was neither impossible nor superfluous, but rather necessary and aligned with reason.

Core Idea
Muhammad's prophethood was possible because it followed a long line of prophets, and urgent because humanity needed guidance.

After refuting general objections to prophethood, al-Nasafi turns to the specific case of Muhammad. He poses two key questions: Was his mission impossible? And was his time free from need for him? The answers are straightforward and rooted in reason and history. This short passage lays out a concise argument that will help us appreciate how kalam defends the necessity of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the rhetorical questions al-Nasafi uses.
The passage is very condensed; each sentence carries weight.
2
Identify the two questions
Al-Nasafi asks: was the mission impossible? And was the time in no need of him? Locate these in the Arabic.
The first question is about possibility, the second about necessity.
3
Follow the answers
See how al-Nasafi answers: previous prophets show it's possible; urgent need shows it's necessary. The final phrase 'awla bi-dhawi al-'uqul' adds that it is most fitting for rational people.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The phrase 'awla bi-dhawi al-'uqul' might be misread as excluding non-intellectuals. Understand that it means his message aligns with reason, not that only intellectuals need him.
This connects to the misconception field below.

Source Text

And was the time in no need of him? Commentary
This passage addresses the prophethood of Muhammad specifically. After refuting general objections, al-Nasafi raises two questions: Was his mission impossible? Was his time free of need for him? The answer is clear: previous prophets existed, so prophethood is not impossible, and people were in urgent need of guidance. Muhammad was the most deserving (awla) of people of intellect—i.e., his message was most appropriate for rational beings.

Nasafi's Refutation of Mu'tazila on Sending Messengers
Nasafi argues against Mu'tazila on obligation of sending messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that nothing is obligatory upon God, in contrast to the Mu'tazila view that sending messengers is obligatory because it is the most beneficial for creation.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to do anything; His actions are pure grace.

In this passage, Nasafi directly challenges the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial). While the Mu'tazila argue that God must send messengers because it is the best for humanity, Nasafi insists that God, as the Creator and Gracious Giver, is under no obligation whatsoever. This refutation is central to the Maturidi position on prophethood and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the Mu'tazila claim that God must send messengers, and Nasafi's counterclaim that nothing is obligatory upon God.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila rationale
The Mu'tazila argue that sending messengers is the 'most beneficial' (al-aslah) for servants, and that God must do what is most beneficial. Consider why they think this.
This is based on their view that good and evil are rational categories.
3
Grasp Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi says 'لا يجب على الله شيء' – nothing is obligatory upon God. He does not deny the need for messengers, but denies that God is compelled to send them. His action is pure grace (tafaddul).
This is a key Maturidi principle: God's actions are not subject to external obligations.
4
Contrast the two views
Write down the key difference: Mu'tazila: obligation based on benefit; Nasafi: no obligation, all from grace.
This clarifies the Maturidi stance on divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شامل لكل الاتجاهات، ويشمل كذلك إثبات أن الصنف الأول في حاجة كبيرة إلى إرسال الرسل، لأنهم لا يستطيعون معرفة حقائق كل شيء. وينتهي النسفي إلى القول بإمكان استدراك الحسن و القبح بالعقل خلافا لقول المعتزلة الذين يقولون بوجوب إرسال الرسل لأنه الأصلح للعباد، ويجب على الله فعل الأصلح. أما النسفي يقول : لا يجب على الله شيء لأنه الخالق المتفضل.
Comprehensive of all tendencies, and it also includes proving that the first category is in great need of sending messengers, because they cannot know the realities of everything. And al-Nasafi concludes to the possibility of perceiving good and evil by reason, contrary to the saying of the Mu'tazila who say that sending messengers is obligatory because it is the most beneficial for servants, and it is obligatory upon God to do the most beneficial. But al-Nasafi says: Nothing is obligatory upon God because He is the Creator, the Gracious Giver.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage explains al-Nasafi's position on the necessity of sending messengers, contrasting it with the Mu'tazila view. The author begins by stating that the first category (i.e., humanity) has a great need for messengers because they cannot know all truths by themselves. Then he notes that al-Nasafi holds that good and evil can be rationally perceived, contradicting the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila argue that God must send messengers because it is the most beneficial (al-aslah) for creation and that God is obliged to do what is best. But al-Nasafi counters by asserting that nothing is obligatory upon God at all, because He is the Creator and the Gracious Giver (al-mutafaddil) who gives freely without obligation. Thus, the sending of messengers is not an obligation upon God but a grace.

Key Terms
إرسال الرسل
الحسن والقبح
الأصلح
المتفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Nasafi denies that messengers are needed at all.
Correction: Al-Nasafi affirms the need for messengers but denies that God is obligated to send them; sending them is a grace, not a duty.

Checkpoint

B) God is not obligated to do anything, including sending messengers.
C) Sending messengers is obligatory because it is the most beneficial.
D) Good and evil cannot be known by reason.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's core argument is that God, as the Creator and Gracious Giver, is under no obligation to send messengers, contrary to the Mu'tazila view of necessary benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If the sending of prophets to guide you is not a duty God had to fulfill but a pure gift, how does that change the way you receive the Quran and the Sunnah? Do you treat them as a favor or a right? Pause and thank God for His grace in sending guidance.
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God must always do the most beneficial for you. Is that comforting? Or does it secretly make you feel that God is under your control—that you can predict His actions? Let Nasafi's statement sink in: God is not obligated. Are you willing to trust a God who is free to give or withhold as He wills, without owing you an explanation?

Miracles and Tawatur as Sources of Certain Knowledge
Nasafi explains the epistemological basis for miracles via tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the miracles of previous prophets are known with certainty through mass transmission (tawatur).

Core Idea
Miracles are historically verified by tawatur, yielding necessary knowledge.

Al-Nasafi explains that the miracles of the prophets were not merely claims; they were witnessed by entire communities and passed down through unbroken chains of transmission. This historical method provides a level of certainty that is immediate and indubitable. In this lesson, we examine how tawatur functions as a proof for prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the sequence: witnessing, inability to oppose, then tawatur.
2
Identify the key elements
Find the elements: God's support through miracles, contemporaneous witnessing, inability to replicate or oppose, and transmission via tawatur.
3
Understand 'ilm daruri
Tawatur yields necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri), which is immediate certainty not based on inference. Compare it with inferential knowledge.
This is a key concept in Islamic epistemology.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: How does this argument secure the historicity of miracles? Why does it matter for proving prophethood?
Reflect on the role of empirical testimony in theological proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يتحدث النسفي عن بعض معجزات الرسل، وأن الله أيدهم بها، وشاهدها من عاشوا في زمانهم، ولم يستطيعوا الإتيان بمثلها، أو – معارضتها بأي وجه من الوجوه، ونقلت الأخبار بالتواتر الذي يوجب العلم الضروري.
And after that al-Nasafi speaks about some miracles of the messengers, and that God supported them with them, and they were witnessed by those who lived in their time, and they were unable to produce the like of them, or to oppose them in any way, and the reports were transmitted by tawatur (mass transmission) that necessitates certain knowledge (al-'ilm al-daruri).
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage explains the epistemological reliability of miracles. Al-Nasafi states that the miracles of previous messengers were witnessed by their contemporaries, who were unable to replicate or contradict them. Furthermore, the reports of these miracles were transmitted via tawatur (mass transmission), a chain of narration so widespread and consistent that it yields necessary knowledge (al-'ilm al-daruri) — that is, immediate certainty, not based on inference. Thus, the historicity of these miracles is beyond doubt.

Key Terms
معجزات الرسل
التواتر
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that belief in miracles is purely a matter of faith without historical grounding.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that miracles are historically verified by eyewitness testimony and mass transmission, providing a rational basis for certainty.

Checkpoint

A) They were recorded in scripture only.
B) They were witnessed by contemporaries and transmitted by tawatur.
C) They were proven by logical arguments.
D) They were only known to the prophets themselves.

Key Takeaway
The miracles of prophets are historically documented by mass transmission (tawatur), which yields certain knowledge without need for inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The contemporaries of the prophets witnessed miracles and could not deny them. If you had been among them, would your heart have surrendered or would you have found excuses? What does that reveal about your current state of faith?
?

The Possibility and Necessity of Muhammad's Prophethood
Nasafi argues that Muhammad's prophethood was possible and necessary, with Quranic support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues that Muhammad's prophethood was both possible and necessary, using Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Muhammad's prophethood was not impossible and the world needed him desperately.

Before examining the proofs of Muhammad's prophethood, Nasafi first addresses the question: was it even possible? And was it needed? He answers with clear reasoning and Quranic verses. This lesson walks through that foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Pose the two questions
Read the opening questions about whether Muhammad's sending was precluded from existence or whether the time was self-sufficient without him. Notice that these are rhetorical, setting up the answer.
These questions frame the entire discussion.
2
Answer from precedent
Nasafi answers that many prophets came before Muhammad, so prophethood is not an impossible category. The very existence of prior messengers shows that sending another is possible.
3
Answer from need
He then argues that there was a pressing need for Muhammad, citing people's own oaths that they would follow a warner. This shows the people themselves acknowledged their need.
The Quranic verses are key evidence.
4
Reflect on the Quranic proofs
Examine the three Quranic verses quoted: Al-Ahqaf 9, Fatir 24, and Fatir 42. Each verse reinforces either the possibility or the necessity. Notice how Nasafi uses them to support his argument.
These verses are central to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد أن ينتهي من رد شبه الخصوم يتحدث عن رسالة سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ويسأل هل كانت بعثة ممتنعة الوجود، مستحيلة الثبوت؟ وهل كان الزمن في غنية عنه؟ والإجابة واضحة أن قبله عليه السلام كان الكثير من الأنبياء والرسل فالرسالة ليست ممتنعة، وإن الحاجة كانت ماسة إليه، وكان أولى بذوي العقول الاستجابة الفورية لقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُلْ مَا كُنتُ بِدْعَا مِنَ الرُّسُلِ } [الأحقاف: ۹] ولقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِن مِنْ أُمَّةٍ إِلَّا خَلَا فِيهَا نَذِيرٌ ﴾ [فاطر: ٢٤]. ولقد كان مجيء الرسول عقيب تمني القوم ذلك، بعد أن اشتد بهم الظلام، واستشرى الظلم يقول تعالى: ﴿ وَأَقْسَمُوا بِاللَّهِ جَهْدَ أَيْتهم لين جَاءَهُمْ نَذِيرٌ لَيَكُونُنَّ أَهْدَى مِنْ إحدَى الْأُمَمِ ﴾ [فاطر: ٤٢] ، ثم يبين النسفي أن أي دعوى يلزمها أمور أربعة، وقد توفرت في رسالة سيدنا محمد عليه السلام. أولها - كون الدعوى في حيز الإمكان. وثانيها إتيان المدعى ببينة مطابقة للدعوى وثالثها . أن تكون دواعي الجرح منتفية عن بينته ورابعها . سلامة الدعوى والبينة عن مناقض يرد عليهما، أو على أحدهما.
And after he finishes refuting the doubts of the opponents, he speaks about the message of our master Muhammad, peace be upon him, and asks: Was his sending precluded from existence, impossible in establishment? And was the time in no need of him? And the answer is clear: before him, peace be upon him, there were many prophets and messengers, so the message is not precluded, and the need was pressing for him, and it was more fitting for people of intellect to respond immediately to His saying: 'Say, I am not a novelty among the messengers' [al-Ahqaf 9], and to His saying: 'And there was no nation but that a warner had passed within it' [Fatir 24]. And indeed the coming of the messenger was following their wish for that, after darkness intensified upon them and injustice spread; He says: 'And they swore by God their strongest oaths that if a warner came to them, they would be more guided than any of the nations' [Fatir 42]. Then al-Nasafi shows that any claim requires four matters, and they were fulfilled in the message of our master Muhammad, peace be upon him. First: the claim being within the realm of possibility. Second: the claimant bringing proof consistent with the claim. Third: that the motives for [impugning] the proof be absent from it. Fourth: the soundness of the claim and the proof from a contradiction that refutes them or one of them.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defends the necessity of Muhammad's prophethood. After refuting objections, al-Nasafi poses two rhetorical questions: Was Muhammad's prophethood impossible? Was the time free of need for him? The answer is no, because there were many prophets before him, so prophethood is not an impossible category, and the people were in desperate need as indicated by Quranic verses (al-Ahqaf 9 and Fatir 24) that show messengers were sent to all nations and that people had sworn to follow a warner. Then al-Nasafi establishes a general principle: any claim (especially prophethood) must fulfill four conditions: (1) the claim must be possible in itself; (2) the claimant must bring proof that matches the claim; (3) that proof must be free from any motives for rejection (i.e., no one can impugn it due to bias); (4) the claim and proof must be free from any contradiction that would refute them. He asserts that all four conditions were met in Muhammad's case.

Key Terms
بعثة
ممتنعة الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muhammad's prophethood was impossible because no new prophet was needed.
Correction: Nasafi shows that many prophets came before, so it is possible, and the pressing need for guidance made it necessary.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why was Muhammad's prophethood not impossible?
A) Because miracles proved it.
B) Because many prophets came before him.
C) Because people expected him.
D) Because the Quran predicted it.

Key Takeaway
Muhammad's prophethood was both possible (because it followed the pattern of earlier prophets) and necessary (because of humanity's desperate need for guidance).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world was in desperate need of Muhammad's prophethood, how much do you truly rely on his guidance today? Are you living as if his message is your only hope, or have you become complacent?
?
The people swore to follow a warner, yet many rejected him. In what area of your life do you swear to follow Allah's guidance but then fail to act when the call comes?
?
Nasafi says people of intellect should respond immediately. What is holding you back from an immediate, whole-hearted response to the Prophet's message in one specific area of your life?

The Four Conditions for a Valid Claim
Nasafi enumerates the four conditions every claim must satisfy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four conditions that any valid claim must fulfill, and how they apply to Muhammad's prophethood.

Core Idea
Any claim must be possible, have matching proof, be free from bias, and avoid contradiction.

After establishing the necessity of Muhammad's prophethood, Nasafi lays out a general principle: any claim (especially a prophetic one) must meet four conditions. These conditions ensure the claim is rationally sound and the evidence trustworthy. This lesson presents and explains each condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the four conditions
Carefully read the passage where Nasafi lists the four conditions. Notice that he introduces them as required for any claim.
2
Understand each condition
Go through each condition one by one: possibility of the claim, consistent proof, absence of motives for impugnment, and freedom from contradiction. Think about why each is necessary.
These conditions are logical prerequisites for accepting any claim as true.
3
Apply to Muhammad's case
Nasafi asserts that all four conditions were fulfilled in Muhammad's message. Consider how each condition is satisfied: prophethood is possible, the Quran is a matching proof, there are no ulterior motives, and the message is consistent.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can distinguish the four conditions from common misconceptions.

The Analogy of Miracles: Proving Muhammad's Prophethood
Al-Nasafi uses analogy from previous prophets to prove Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses a logical analogy from the miracles of Moses and Jesus to establish the prophethood of Muhammad.

Core Idea
Miracles that break natural law are the common proof for all prophets.

Al-Nasafi employs a concise analogy to affirm Muhammad's prophethood. He first lays down a logical principle: if two individuals share the same evidence (dalīl) and cause (ʿilla), they share the same conclusion (madlūl) and effect (maʿlūl). Then he applies it: Moses and Jesus were proven prophets by miracles that defy natural order; therefore, Muhammad, who also brought such miracles, is equally a prophet. This method shows consistency in divine practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once, then read the literal English translation. Get a feel for the original wording.
Notice the concise style of al-Nasafi.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage has two parts: a general principle and its application. Find where the principle ends and the application begins.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'dalīl', 'ʿilla', 'madlūl', 'maʿlūl'. These are fundamental in kalam reasoning. They form the backbone of the analogy.
The terms are paired: proof with cause, and result with effect.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Consider that Moses and Jesus brought miracles that break the material order. Muhammad also brought such miracles. Therefore, the same conclusion (prophethood) applies.
This is a standard argument in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد أن يتحدث عن الأمور الأربعة السابقة بالتفصيل يقول: "لا شك أن من ساوى غيره في الدليل، والعلة ساواه في المدلول والمعلول، وموسى وعيسى عليهما السلام ثبتت رسالتهما لوجود المعجزات الناقضات للمادة، فكذا سيدنا محمد عليه السلام.
Then after he discusses the four previous matters in detail, he says: "There is no doubt that whoever equals another in the proof (dalil) and cause ('illa) equals him in the thing proved (madlul) and effect (ma'lul). And Moses and Jesus, peace be upon them, their prophethood was established due to the existence of miracles that break the material order, so likewise our master Muhammad, peace be upon him."
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents a logical analogy. Al-Nasafi states a general principle: if two individuals are equal in the evidence (dalil) and the underlying cause ('illa), then they are equal in the conclusion (madlul) and effect (ma'lul). Applying this: Moses and Jesus were proven prophets by miracles that break the natural order; therefore, Muhammad, who also brought such miracles, is equally a prophet. The analogy is based on the shared presence of miracles (the cause) and their implication (prophethood).

Key Terms
دليل
علة
مدلول
معلول
معجزات ناقضات للمادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this analogy is weak because the miracles are not identical or because it is circular.
Correction: In kalam, such analogical reasoning (qiyās) is used to demonstrate consistency in divine practice. The shared presence of miracles (the cause) is sufficient to affirm prophethood, without requiring identical miracles.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi use the example of Moses and Jesus to prove Muhammad's prophethood?
A) He claims Muhammad performed the same miracles as them.
B) He argues that since they were proven by miracles, and Muhammad also had miracles, he is likewise a prophet.
C) He says Muhammad was foretold by them.
D) He relies solely on the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's analogy demonstrates that miracles are a consistent proof of prophethood across all prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Imagine you stand before Allah and are asked about the proof of Muhammad's prophethood. Would you be able to point to the same evidence that convinced the scholars? Are you living today as if you are certain of that truth, or as if it were only a historical opinion? Let this lesson call you to a deeper, personal ownership of your faith.

Classification of Miracles: Sensory and Intellectual
Nasafi divides Muhammad's miracles into sensory and intellectual with subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi classifies the miracles of Prophet Muhammad into sensory and intellectual categories, each with further subdivisions.

Core Idea
Miracles of the Prophet are both seen and reasoned.

This passage presents al-Nasafi's systematic classification of the proofs of Muhammad's prophethood. He divides them first into two broad types: those perceptible by the senses and those grasped by the intellect. Each type is then broken down into subcategories, showing the comprehensive nature of the prophetic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the literal translation. Note the flow from sensory to intellectual miracles.
The passage is a summary of al-Nasafi's longer discussion.
2
Identify the two main categories
The passage states that the miracles are either sensory (hissiyya) or intellectual ('aqliyya). Pay attention to how each is introduced.
3
Note the three subcategories of sensory miracles
Sensory miracles are divided into three: outside his self, in his self, and in his character. Think of examples for each.
These cover physical wonders, his personal attributes, and his moral perfection.
4
Note the nine subcategories of intellectual miracles
Intellectual miracles include his state, lineage, supplications, reports, post-time phenomena, place, time, book, and law. Count them as you read.
The list is extensive, showing the richness of rational proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يذكر أن الآيات الدالة على نبوة سيدنا محمد حسية، وعقلية، ويفسر ويقسمها ويضرب أمثلة الحسية بأنها ما يظهرها الله تعالى للأعين من العجائب الخارقة للمادة، إلى ثلاثة أقسام: أ - خارج ذاته. ب - في ذاته. ج - في أخلاقه، أ لكل قسم، وبعد أن ينتهي من ذكر المعجزات الحسية، يتحدث عن المعجزات العقلية فيقسمها إلى أقسام منها ما هو راجع إلى حاله، وما هو راجع إلى نسبه، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى دعواته، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى إخباره، ومنها معجزات ظهرت بعد زمانه، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى مكانه ، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى زمانه، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى كتابه الذي أتى به من عند الله، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى شريعته. ويتكلم في هذه المعجزات كلها بالتفصيل المقنع، من ذلك مثلا أنه كان مستجاب الدعوة، ويحكي النسفي بعض أدعية الرسول يذكر منها ما نقل بالتواتر
And after that he mentions that the verses (ayat) indicating the prophethood of our master Muhammad are sensory (hissiya) and intellectual ('aqliyya), and he explains and divides them and gives examples. The sensory are what God, exalted, shows to the eyes of the wondrous things that break matter, into three sections: A) outside his self, B) in his self, C) in his character, [he gives] examples for each section. And after he finishes mentioning the sensory miracles, he speaks about intellectual miracles and divides them into sections: among them what pertains to his state (hal), what pertains to his lineage (nasab), among them what pertains to his supplications (da'awat), among them what pertains to his reports (ikhbar), among them miracles that appeared after his time, among them what pertains to his place (makan), among them what pertains to his time (zaman), among them what pertains to his book (kitab) which he brought from Allah, and among them what pertains to his law (shari'a). And he discusses all these miracles in convincing detail, for example that he was answered in supplication (mustajab al-da'wa), and al-Nasafi relates some of the supplications of the Messenger, mentioning among them what was transmitted by tawatur.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage classifies the proofs of Muhammad's prophethood into two main categories: sensory (hissiyya) and intellectual ('aqliyya). Sensory miracles are physically observable wonders that break natural laws. They are further subdivided into three types: (a) events outside the prophet's own person (e.g., splitting the moon), (b) events in his person (e.g., his body's properties), (c) events in his character (e.g., his moral perfection). Intellectual miracles are rational proofs, which are further divided: those relating to his state (e.g., his knowledge), his lineage (e.g., his noble descent), his supplications (e.g., his answered prayers), his reports (e.g., prophecies), miracles after his time (e.g., preservation of his message), his place (e.g., Mecca), his time (e.g., the age of ignorance), his book (the Quran), and his law (shari'a). The author mentions that al-Nasafi provides detailed examples, such as the prophet's answered prayers, and notes that some were transmitted by tawatur.

Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that there are many other miracles, both sensory and intellectual, including those related to his person, character, supplications, and more.

Equality in Evidence for All Prophets
Al-Nasafi states the principle that equal evidence implies equal conclusions, applied to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses the principle of equality in evidence and cause to logically establish Muhammad's prophethood by analogy to Moses and Jesus.

Core Idea
Equal evidence for prophethood means equal conclusion—Muhammad is a prophet like Moses and Jesus.

Al-Nasafi presents a concise logical argument: if two cases share the same evidence and underlying cause, they must yield the same conclusion. He applies this to prophethood: Moses and Jesus were proven prophets through miracles that break the material order, and since Muhammad also performed such miracles, his prophethood follows necessarily. This reasoning defends Muhammad's prophethood against those who accept previous prophets but reject him, demanding consistency in judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the rhythm of the argument sink in.
Notice the key terms: دليل, علة, مدلول, معلول.
2
Identify the principle and its application
The passage first states a general principle (equality in evidence and cause equals equality in conclusion and effect), then applies it to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad. Find both parts.
The principle is a logical law; the application is a concrete example.
3
Reflect on logical consistency
Ask yourself: why would someone accept Moses and Jesus as prophets but reject Muhammad? How does al-Nasafi's argument counter that inconsistency?
The argument demands that if the evidence is the same, the judgment must be the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد أن يتحدث عن الأمور الأربعة السابقة بالتفصيل يقول: "لا شك أن من ساوى غيره في الدليل، والعلة ساواه في المدلول والمعلول، وموسى وعيسى عليهما السلام ثبتت رسالتهما لوجود المعجزات الناقضات للمادة، فكذا سيدنا محمد عليه السلام."
Then after he speaks about the previous four matters in detail, he says: "There is no doubt that whoever equals another in evidence and cause equals him in the conclusion and effect. Moses and Jesus, peace be upon them, their prophethood was established due to the existence of miracles that break the material order, so likewise our master Muhammad, peace be upon him."
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy (qiyas) applied to prophethood. Al-Nasafi states a general principle: if two cases share the same evidence (dalil) and underlying cause (illa), they necessarily share the same conclusion (madlul) and effect (ma'lul). He then applies this: Moses and Jesus were proven prophets because they performed miracles that break the natural order. Since Muhammad also performed such miracles, he must be a prophet just as they were. This argument counters those who accept previous prophets but reject Muhammad—they must be consistent in their reasoning.

Key Terms
دليل
علة
مدلول
معلول
معجزات ناقضات للمادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Nasafi claims Muhammad's miracles are exactly identical to those of Moses and Jesus (e.g., same type, same magnitude).
Correction: The argument does not require identical miracles; it only requires that the evidence be of the same kind—miracles that break the material order. The specific miracles can differ; what matters is that they both serve as proof of prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi defends Muhammad's prophethood by logical analogy: the same type of miracle evidence proves the same conclusion of prophethood, demanding consistency from those who accept earlier prophets.

?

Sensory Miracles: Visible Proofs of Prophethood
Al-Nasafi defines sensory miracles and divides them into three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's definition and threefold classification of sensory miracles and how they serve as visible proofs of prophethood.

Core Idea
Sensory miracles are extraordinary wonders Allah displays to the eyes.

Al-Nasafi begins his classification by distinguishing sensory miracles from intellectual ones. He defines sensory miracles as wonders that break the material order and are perceived by sight. Then he subdivides them into three categories: those external to the prophet, those in his own person, and those in his character.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining sensory miracles
Read the Arabic passage that defines sensory miracles and listen to the translation. Notice the three subdivisions enumerated.
The passage uses enumeration letters (أ, ب, ج).
2
Identify the three subdivisions
Find the three categories: external to the self, in the self, and in morals. Understand what each means.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why sensory miracles are considered proofs of prophethood and how they complement intellectual miracles.
Sensory miracles appeal to direct observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يذكر أن الآيات الدالة على نبوة سيدنا محمد حسية، وعقلية، ويفسر ويقسمها ويضرب أمثلة الحسية بأنها ما يظهرها الله تعالى للأعين من العجائب الخارقة للمادة، إلى ثلاثة أقسام: أ - خارج ذاته. ب - في ذاته. ج - في أخلاقه، أ لكل قسم، وبعد أن ينتهي من ذكر المعجزات الحسية، يتحدث عن المعجزات العقلية فيقسمها إلى أقسام منها ما هو راجع إلى حاله، وما هو راجع إلى نسبه، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى دعواته، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى إخباره، ومنها معجزات ظهرت بعد زمانه، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى مكانه ، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى زمانه، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى كتابه الذي أتى به من عند الله، ومنها ما هو راجع إلى شريعته.
And after that he mentions that the signs indicating the prophethood of our master Muhammad are sensory and intellectual, and he explains and divides them and gives examples. The sensory are what Allah Almighty manifests to the eyes of miraculous wonders that break the material order, into three divisions: A – external to his self. B – in his self. C – in his morals. For each division (he gives examples). And after he finishes mentioning the sensory miracles, he discusses the intellectual miracles and divides them into divisions: some relate to his state, some relate to his lineage, some relate to his supplications, some relate to his news/information, some are miracles that appeared after his time, some relate to his place, some relate to his time, some relate to his book that he brought from Allah, and some relate to his shari'a.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a classification (taqsim) of the proofs of prophethood into two main types: sensory miracles (hissiyya) and intellectual miracles (aqliyya). Al-Nasafi first defines sensory miracles as wonders that Allah causes to be seen by the eyes, which break the material order. He then subdivides them into three categories: (a) those outside the prophet himself, (b) those within his own person, and (c) those related to his character and morals. After the sensory miracles, he turns to intellectual miracles—these are not directly visible but appeal to reason. He lists many subdivisions: those related to his personal state (e.g., poverty or richness), his lineage (e.g., being from Quraysh), his supplications (e.g., answered prayers), his prophecies (akhbar), miracles that occurred after his time (such as the preservation of his message), his place (Mecca and Medina), his time (the era of revelation), his book (the Qur'an), and his shari'a (the Islamic law). This structured classification shows that prophetic evidence is both seen and reasoned.

Key Terms
معجزات حسية
خارقة للمادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, sensory miracles are defined as:
A) wonders that appeal to reason
B) wonders manifested to the eyes that break the material order
C) miracles that appear after the prophet's time
D) miracles related to the prophet's lineage

Key Takeaway
Sensory miracles are visible wonders that break the material order, divided into three types: external, internal, and moral.

Intellectual Miracles: Rational Proofs of Prophethood
Al-Nasafi enumerates the various subdivisions of intellectual miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's extensive classification of intellectual miracles and how they appeal to reason rather than sight.

Core Idea
Intellectual miracles are proofs that require rational reflection, not direct vision.

After completing his discussion of sensory miracles, al-Nasafi turns to intellectual miracles — proofs that are not seen but reasoned. He lists an extensive set of categories: those relating to the prophet's state, lineage, supplications, predictions, posthumous signs, place, time, the Qur'an, and the Shari'a. Each category provides a different angle of rational evidence.

Read the passage on intellectual miracles

2
Identify the nine categories
Count the categories and write them down. They range from personal attributes to the Shari'a.
Some categories overlap slightly.
3
Reflect on their rational nature
Consider why these are called 'intellectual' miracles. They require reasoning to see how they indicate prophethood.
They are not directly visible like sensory miracles.

Answered Prayers as Sensory Miracles
Al-Nasafi provides examples of sensory miracles through the Prophet's answered prayers for Mudar and against Kisra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two specific sensory miracles of the Prophet Muhammad (PBUH) cited by Al-Nasafi: the prayer against Mudar and against Kisra, and how they serve as proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Answered prayers are direct sensory evidence of prophethood.

Al-Nasafi now moves from theoretical discussion to concrete historical examples. He presents two cases where the Prophet's prayers were immediately fulfilled in a visible, tangible way. These are called sensory miracles because they were witnessed by the senses of those present, establishing a clear proof of prophethood through mass-transmitted reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the content.
Notice the phrase 'ما نقل بالتواتر'—these events are reported through mass transmission.
2
Identify the two examples
Find the prayer against Mudar and the prayer against Kisra. Note the specific wording of each supplication and the immediate result.
The result for Mudar was a severe drought; for Kisra, the tearing of his kingdom.
3
Understand the miraculous nature
Consider why these events are considered miracles: the prayers were answered directly and publicly, affecting large groups.
These are called 'sensory' because people saw the effects—drought, death of livestock, and the fall of an empire.
4
Recognize the role of tawatur
Al-Nasafi emphasizes that these reports are transmitted by tawatur (mass transmission). This ensures certainty in their occurrence.
Tawatur eliminates doubt about the historical reality of these miracles.
5
Reflect on the editorial note
The passage ends with a statement from the editor confirming the authenticity of these hadiths. Notice how scholarship supports the evidence.
This reinforces that the examples are not mere stories but verified records.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من ذلك مثلا أنه كان مستجاب الدعوة، ويحكي النسفي بعض أدعية الرسول يذكر منها ما نقل بالتواتر، فقد قال عليه السلام في حق مضر : "اللهم أشدد وطأتك على مضر، واجعل عليهم سنین کسنی "يوسف" فأمسك الله عنهم القطر حتى جف النبات وهلكت المواشي، ولم ينكشف عنهم الجدب إلا بدعائه عليه السلام لهم، ومن ذلك أيضا دعاؤه على كسرى لما مزق كتابه فقال عليه السلام "اللهم مزق ملكه كل ممزق" وقال: "أما إنكم ستمتلكون أرضه، فمزق الله ملكه وملكه المسلمون. وبالرجوع إلى كتب السنة الصحيحة تبين لي صحة هذه الأحاديث سندا ومتنا كما هو مبين في التحقيق.
Among that, for example, is that he (the Prophet) was answered in supplication, and al-Nasafi relates some of the Prophet's supplications, mentioning from them what was transmitted by tawatur. He said, peace be upon him, concerning Mudar: "O Allah, make your punishment severe upon Mudar, and make upon them years like the years of Joseph." So Allah withheld rain from them until vegetation dried up and livestock perished, and the drought was not lifted from them except by his supplication, peace be upon him, for them. And also from that is his supplication against Kisra when he tore his letter. He said, peace be upon him: "O Allah, tear apart his kingdom completely." And he said: "Indeed, you will possess his land." So Allah tore apart his kingdom, and the Muslims possessed it. And upon consulting the books of authentic Sunna, it became clear to me the authenticity of these hadiths in chain and text, as is shown in the verification.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage provides specific sensory miracles in the form of answered prayers (istijabat al-da'wa). Al-Nasafi notes that the Prophet's supplications were answered, and these are reported through tawatur (mass transmission). The first example: the Prophet prayed against the tribe of Mudar, asking for severe punishment and years of famine like those of Joseph. The result was immediate drought and destruction, and the famine only ended when the Prophet prayed for them again. The second example: when the Persian king Kisra tore the Prophet's letter, the Prophet prayed that his kingdom be torn apart, and foretold that the Muslims would conquer it. This occurred historically. The passage ends with an editorial note confirming the authenticity of these hadiths based on verification from reliable Sunna collections. These miracles are called 'sensory' because they were witnessed by the senses—people saw the drought and the fall of the Persian empire.

Correction: The passage emphasizes that the drought and the fall of the Persian empire were direct results of the Prophet's supplications, as clearly stated in the text and confirmed by tawatur transmission.

Prophecies as Intellectual Miracles: Evidence from Qur'an and Hadith
Al-Nasafi enumerates several fulfilled prophecies as intellectual miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how accurate prophecies about future events, made by an unlettered Prophet, serve as intellectual proofs of his prophethood.

Core Idea
The Prophet's detailed prophecies, fulfilled in history, are clear intellectual miracles demonstrating divine knowledge.

Al-Nasafi presents a series of prophecies that the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) made, both in the Qur'an and in his sayings. These predictions came true exactly as stated, and they form a powerful argument for his prophethood. In this lesson, we examine these prophecies and reflect on how they provide evidence for the truth of Islam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Context
Al-Nasafi begins by noting that glad tidings of the Prophet appeared in earlier scriptures and that he knew past events despite being unlettered. This sets the stage for the miraculous nature of his future predictions.
The mention of being 'ummi' highlights that his knowledge was not acquired through human learning.
2
Read the Qur'anic Prophecies
Read the three Qur'anic prophecies: the defeat of the polytheists at Badr (Q.54:45), the victory of the Romans after defeat (Q.30:1-3), and the promise of return to Mecca (Q.28:85). Notice how each was fulfilled exactly as stated.
These prophecies were made before the events occurred, and their fulfillment is historically documented.
3
Examine the Hadith Prophecies
Read the hadith prophecies: the folding of the earth foretelling Muslim conquests, the announcement of al-Najashi's death, and the prediction about 'Ammar ibn Yasir. These are additional independent predictions that came true.
Each prophecy is a separate piece of evidence; collectively they form a strong cumulative argument.
4
Reflect on the Cumulative Argument
Ask yourself: how likely is it that an unlettered man could accurately predict multiple distinct future events? The fact that all these prophecies came true supports the claim that his knowledge came from divine revelation.
This is the core of the intellectual miracle: reason alone points to a supernatural source.
5
Consider the Significance of Being Unlettered
The Prophet's illiteracy eliminates the possibility that he fabricated these prophecies from prior study. This amplifies the miracle and reinforces the divine origin of his message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويورد النسفي بعض البشارات الواردة بنبوته عليه السلام في التوراة، والزبور والإنجيل. ويورد كذلك معرفته بالأخبار الماضية. وهو أمي لا يعرف القراءة ولا الكتابة، ومعرفته بما سيقع في المستقبل، ومن ذلك قوله تعالى: سَيُهْرَمُ الْجَمْعُ وَيُوَلُّونَ الدبر ﴾ [القمر: ٤٥] وكان نزولها قبل بدء الحرب في بدر، وقد تحقق ذلك، وانتصر المسلمون وولي الكافرون في غزوة بدر مع قلة عدد المسلمين وعنادهم. وكقوله تعالى الم غُلِبَتِ الرُّومُ فِي أَدْنَى الْأَرْضِ وَهُم مِنْ بَعْدِ عَلَبِهِمْ سَيَغْلِبُونَ ) [الروم، ۱: ۳] وقد كان كما أخبر عليه السلام. وكقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِي فَرَضَ عَلَيْكَ الْقُرْءَ ان لَرَادُكَ إِلَى مَعَادٍ [القصص: ٨٥] أي سيرجعك إلى مكة وقد رجع إليها عزيزا منتصرا، وكذلك في نصوص الأحاديث الشريفة، ومنها مثلا قوله عليه السلام: "زويت لي الأرض فرأيت مشارقها ومغاربها، وسيبلغ ملك أمتي ما زوي لي منها، وكان الأمر كما أخبر وفتوحات الصديق، وعمر وعثمان وعلي، وما تم في عهد الدولة الأموية يؤكد ذلك. ومن ذلك أيضا أنه عليه السلام نعى النجاشي وأخبر أصحابه بموته، وبعد ذلك جاءت الأخبار بموته، ومن ذلك قوله لعمار بن ياسر "تقتلك الفئة الباغية"
And al-Nasafi mentions some of the glad tidings of his prophethood, peace be upon him, in the Torah, the Psalms, and the Gospel. And he also mentions his knowledge of past events. He was unlettered, not knowing reading or writing, and his knowledge of what would occur in the future. Among that is the saying of Allah: "The assembly will be defeated, and they will turn their backs" [al-Qamar 54:45] – and its revelation was before the start of the battle of Badr, and that was realized; the Muslims were victorious and the disbelievers turned their backs in the battle of Badr despite the small number of Muslims and their hardships. And like His saying: "Alif Lam Meem. The Romans have been defeated in the nearest land, and they, after their defeat, will be victorious" [al-Rum 30:1-3] – and it happened as he, peace be upon him, informed. And like His saying: "Indeed He who has ordained the Qur'an for you will return you to a place of return" [al-Qasas 28:85] – meaning He will return you to Mecca, and he returned to it honored and victorious. And likewise in the texts of noble hadiths, among them for example his saying, peace be upon him: "The earth was folded for me and I saw its East and West, and the dominion of my nation will reach what was folded for me thereof" – and the matter was as he informed, and the conquests of Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, 'Ali, and what was accomplished during the Umayyad state confirm that. And also from that is that he, peace be upon him, announced the death of al-Najashi and informed his companions of his death, and after that news of his death came. And from that is his saying to 'Ammar ibn Yasir: "The rebellious group will kill you."
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents intellectual miracles, specifically prophecies (akhbar bil-mustaqbal), as proofs of prophethood. Al-Nasafi begins by mentioning that glad tidings of the Prophet appeared in earlier scriptures (Torah, Psalms, Gospel)—this shows consistency with prior revelation. He then highlights that the Prophet, despite being unlettered (ummi), had accurate knowledge of past and future events. Several Qur'anic prophecies are cited: (1) the defeat of the polytheists at Badr (Q.54:45) revealed before the battle; (2) the Roman victory after defeat (Q.30:1-3); (3) the promise of return to Mecca (Q.28:85) which was fulfilled. Then from hadith: the earth was folded for the Prophet and he saw its extent, and his nation's dominion would reach that—this was fulfilled in Islamic conquests. Also, he informed of the death of the Negus of Abyssinia (al-Najashi) before news arrived, and told 'Ammar ibn Yasir that he would be killed by the rebellious group. All these are intellectual proofs because they require reflection on the accuracy of the information. The fact that an unlettered man made these predictions correctly demonstrates divine knowledge.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These prophecies are mere coincidences or lucky guesses.
Correction: The multiplicity and specificity of these prophecies, all made by an unlettered man, make coincidence highly improbable. They form a cumulative argument that demands a divine source.

Key Takeaway

?

?

If you knew with certainty that the Prophet's words are from Allah, would it change the way you read the Qur'an and hadith? What specific step will you take this week to act on that certainty?

Prophetic Miracles and the Principle of Karamat
Al-Nasafi lists prophetic miracles and defines karamat as Prophet's miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that karamat of saints are considered miracles of the Prophet, and that prophetic miracles include predictions and posthumous events.

Core Idea
Every saintly wonder is actually a miracle of the Prophet.

Al-Nasafi provides two examples of the Prophet's miracles: the precise prediction of the caliphate's duration and a posthumous event involving the martyrs. He then clarifies that any supernatural event from a saint is attributed to the Prophet's blessing, reinforcing the supremacy of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the two examples and the concluding principle.
2
Identify the examples
Notice the first example: the caliphate lasting thirty years, which came true. The second: the exhumation of martyrs after fifty years.
The passage also includes a verification note about hadith authenticity.
3
Understand the principle
Al-Nasafi states that every karamah (wonder) from a wali (saint) is actually a miracle of the Prophet. This shows that all supernatural acts in the community trace back to prophethood.
This is a key Ash'ari-Maturidi doctrine.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this principle matters: it prevents elevating saints to the level of prophets while still honouring their miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقتل في صفين، ومنها تحديده الخلافة بعده بقوله: الخلافة" بعدي ثلاثون سنة"، فكان كما أخبر عليه السلام، وقد رجعت إلى كتب السنة الصحيحة وتأكدت من صحة هذه الأحاديث سندا ومتناً كما هو مبين في التحقيق. ويذكر النسفي بعض ما ظهر من معجزاته بعد وفاته ومنها على سبيل المثال إخراج شهداء بدر وطابا بعد خمسين سنة. والنسفي كغيره من المتكلمين يعتبر كل كرامة ظهرت لولي فهي معجزة للنبي عليه السلام.
He was killed in Siffin?— and among them (his miracles) is his determination of the caliphate after him by his saying: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years,' and it was as he informed, peace be upon him. And I have referred to the sound books of the Sunnah and verified the authenticity of these hadiths in chain and text as is shown in the research. And al-Nasafi mentions some of what appeared of his miracles after his death, including for example the exhumation of the martyrs of Badr and Tabā? after fifty years. And al-Nasafi, like other mutakallimun, considers every karamah that appears from a wali to be a miracle of the Prophet, peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage lists examples of the Prophet's miracles and establishes a theological principle. First, al-Nasafi mentions the prophecy about the caliphate lasting thirty years, which indeed occurred. He asserts its authenticity from reliable Sunni books. Second, he cites a posthumous miracle: the exhumation of the martyrs of Badr and Tabā (possibly Taybah) after fifty years. Then he states the broader principle that every karamah of a wali (saint) is considered a mu'jizah (miracle) for the Prophet, because the wali's miracle stems from the Prophet's blessing. This is a standard Ash'ari-Maturidi view that differentiates karamat from prophetic miracles.

Key Terms
معجزة
كرامة
ولي
خلافة
حديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think karamat are independent miracles showing the saint's own rank.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that every karamah is actually a miracle of the Prophet, deriving from his prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, how are karamat of awliya related to the Prophet's miracles?
A) They are independent miracles showing the saint's rank.
B) They are actually miracles of the Prophet.
C) They are not considered miracles at all.
D) They are satanic illusions.

Key Takeaway
Karamat of saints are considered miracles of the Prophet, affirming prophethood as the source of all supernatural events in the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness or hear about extraordinary acts attributed to pious people, do your heart and mind instantly connect those acts to the Prophet's prophethood, or do you tend to admire the individual? How can you train yourself to see all goodness as flowing from him?
?
The Prophet's miracles continue through the awliya. Are you actively seeking to be one of those awliya by following his sunnah so closely that your very existence becomes a testament to his prophethood? What specific sunnah will you commit to reviving today?
?
If all karamat are really the Prophet's miracles, then your own small wonders—like a sudden guidance or a answered prayer—are also tied to him. Do you feel the weight of that connection? How will you thank him and his Lord for including you in his legacy?

The World Before the Prophet's Advent
Al-Nasafi describes the darkness before the Prophet's mission and the elephant story as prelude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi portrays the global spiritual crisis before the Prophet's mission and the role of the elephant story as a divine sign.

Core Idea
The world was in darkness; the Prophet was the only hope.

Al-Nasafi sets the scene for the necessity of prophethood by describing the profound corruption and ignorance that had overtaken humanity. He highlights the blessedness of Makka through the story of the elephant, a well-known historical event that he treats as a prelude to the Prophet's coming. This passage prepares us to appreciate the Prophet's role as a mercy to all worlds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage attentively
Read the Arabic passage twice. First for overall meaning, then note the specific examples al-Nasafi gives of corruption.
Pay attention to how he contrasts the darkness with the Prophet's arrival.
2
Identify the two main sections
Observe that the passage first discusses the place (Makka and the elephant story), then describes the state of the world (Jews, Christians, idolaters).
This structure builds the argument for prophethood.
3
Reflect on the necessity of prophethood
Ask yourself: why does al-Nasafi list these specific corruptions? How do they justify the need for a new prophet?
The answer lies in the final lines: the Prophet is the remedy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث النسفي عن مكانة المكان الذي بعث منه عليه السلام، ويذكر أشياء كثيرة منها قصة أصحاب الفيل التي ورد ذكرها في القرآن، وتكلم عنها شعراء الجاهلية، ولم يستطع التاريخ إنكارها، ولقد كانت إرهاصا لمقدم محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم من هذه البلدة الطيبة. ويتحدث عن الظروف التي بعث فيها الرسول عليه السلام، وتتلخص في ظلم وظلام ينتشر في جميع العالم، وخاصة بلاد العرب والفرس، والهند، والترك حتى إن من كان منهم على دين كاليهود حرفوا الكلم عن مواضعه، وكذبوا المسيح، وكالنصارى الذين اختلفوا في ديانتهم، وقالوا بالصاحبة والولد، وقالوا بألوهية عيسى. إذا كانت كل الأوضاع تشير إلى ضرورة تُصَبِّح لاتجاه الحياة نحو نفسها، ونحو الله. فكان ، هو الأمل، وهو البسمة، وهو السكينة، وهو الأمان، والرحمة وهو السعادة الدنيوية والأخروية للجميع.
And al-Nasafi speaks about the station of the place from which he, peace be upon him, was sent, and mentions many things including the story of the companions of the elephant mentioned in the Qur'an, about which the pre-Islamic poets spoke, and history could not deny it, and it was a prelude to the coming of Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, from this good town. And he speaks about the circumstances in which the Messenger was sent, peace be upon him, which can be summarized as injustice and darkness spreading throughout the entire world, especially the lands of the Arabs, Persia, India, and the Turks, to the extent that those who were on a religion, like the Jews, distorted words from their places and denied the Messiah, and like the Christians who differed in their religion and said (Allah has) a wife and a son, and said to the divinity of Jesus. As all conditions pointed to the necessity of life turning toward itself and toward Allah. So he (the Prophet) was the hope, the smile, the tranquility, the security, the mercy, and the worldly and otherworldly happiness for all.

Commentary
This passage sets the context for the need of prophethood. Al-Nasafi describes two aspects: first, the blessed place of Makka with the story of the elephant as an irhas (preparation) for the Prophet. Second, the global spiritual darkness: Jews corrupted scriptures, Christians adopted false doctrines (wife and son, divinity of Jesus). This widespread corruption necessitated a divine guide. The Prophet appears as the hope and mercy for all humanity. The passage is descriptive, emphasizing the dire need that the Prophet fulfilled.

Comprehensiveness of Islamic Law as Proof of Prophethood
Al-Nasafi enumerates the components of religion and argues that Muhammad's perfect fulfillment proves his prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the comprehensiveness of Muhammad's message across all aspects of religion serves as a rational proof of his prophethood.

Core Idea
A perfect, comprehensive message points to a truthful messenger.

Al-Nasafi explains that the essence of religion in all faiths revolves around beliefs, acts of worship, transactions, deterrents, good manners, and praiseworthy traits. He argues that Muhammad brought all these in a manner that astonishes sound intellects, and this comprehensiveness itself becomes a proof of his truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the list of components that make up religion according to al-Nasafi.
Notice that the list includes six items.
2
Identify the logical flow
Trace al-Nasafi's argument: he defines religion's universal components, then states that Muhammad's fulfillment is perfect, and concludes that this establishes his truthfulness beyond doubt.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why presenting a message that covers every essential aspect of religion and does so perfectly is a strong indicator of prophethood.
Think about what alternative explanations might be possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يتكلم عما هو راجع إلى شريعة الإسلام فيقول: إن مدار أمور الدين والملة في جميع الأديان يكون متعلقا بالاعتقادات والعبادات، والمعاملات، والمزاجر، والآداب الحسنة، والشيم الحميدة، وقد أتى محمد بكل هذه الأمور بما تتعجب منه العقول الصحيحة، وتقدره أجل التقدير. ويتحدث بالتفصيل عن كل أمر من الأمور السابقة، وبعد ذلك يقول بالحرف: - وبالوقوف على ما تقدم عرف أنه عليه السلام كان صادقا فيما ادعاه من رسالة ربه معرفة لا يشوبها شك، ويعتبر الموانع عن الإزعان لدعوته عليه السلام ترجع دائما إلى أمور منها: أ - العصبية الجنسية. ب الأنفة المانعة من اتباع الغير. ج والإلف والهوى. د - الكسل عن لوازم التكليف.
And after that he speaks about what pertains to the law of Islam, saying: Indeed the pivot of matters of religion and creed in all religions is connected to beliefs, acts of worship, transactions, deterrents, good manners, and praiseworthy traits. And Muhammad brought all these matters in a way that sound intellects marvel at and appreciate with the utmost appreciation. And he discusses in detail each of the previous matters, and after that he says verbatim: By reflection on what has preceded, it is known that he, peace be upon him, was truthful in what he claimed of the message from his Lord, with a knowledge untainted by doubt. And he considers the obstacles to acceptance of his call, peace be upon him, always revert to things including: a) racial partisanship, b) pride preventing following others, c) habit and desire, d) laziness regarding the requirements of responsibility.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents two main points: the comprehensiveness of Islamic law and the proof of Muhammad's prophethood, along with obstacles to belief. Al-Nasafi states that the essence of religion in all faiths revolves around beliefs ('itiqadat), acts of worship ('ibadat), transactions (mu'amalat), deterrents (mazajir), good manners (adab hasan), and praiseworthy traits (shiyam hamida). Muhammad brought all these in a perfect form that astonishes sound intellects. After detailing each, he concludes that reflecting on the previous evidence yields certain knowledge of his truthfulness. Then he lists four obstacles that prevent people from accepting his call: (1) racial or national partisanship ('asabiyya jinsiyya), (2) pride that prevents following others (anifa), (3) habit and desire (ulf wa hawa), and (4) laziness regarding the demands of religious duty (kasal 'an lawazim al-taklif). These obstacles are not due to any deficiency in the proof itself.

Key Terms
شريعة الإسلام
الاعتقادات
العبادات
المعاملات
المزاجر
الآداب الحسنة
الشيم الحميدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think prophethood is proven only by supernatural miracles, and the content of the message itself cannot provide certain proof.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the comprehensiveness and perfection of the message itself serve as a rational proof, leading to certain knowledge without need for external miracles.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi claim about the components of religion?
A) They are limited to beliefs only.
B) They vary across religions.
C) They are universal across all religions.
D) They are not important for proving prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Muhammad's comprehensive and perfect message is itself a rational proof of his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the perfection of Muhammad's message is the proof of his prophethood, what does your own inconsistency in living by its components—beliefs, worship, transactions, manners—say about your depth of conviction? Will you commit today to strengthening one area where you fall short?
?
When you encounter a ruling in Islam that challenges your desires or modern norms, do you dismiss it as outdated, or do you trust that it is part of a perfect, comprehensive message from Allah? How will you train your heart to submit to the whole?
?
If a skeptic asked you to show them the proof of prophethood through the perfection of the message, would your life reflect that perfection? What one change would make your testimony more convincing, and when will you start?

Obstacles to Accepting the Prophet's Call
Al-Nasafi lists four obstacles to accepting the prophet's call.

Rejection often stems from pride, prejudice, habit, or laziness, not lack of evidence.

After establishing the proof of prophethood through the comprehensiveness of Islam, al-Nasafi turns to the reasons why people still reject the call. He identifies four obstacles rooted in human psychology and society, showing that the problem lies in the recipient, not the message.

Relate to the bigger picture

The Two Main Characteristics of Prophethood
Al-Nasafi classifies the special characteristics of prophethood into two main divisions with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi classifies the special characteristics of prophethood into preparatory and nurturing divisions, with their subcategories.

Core Idea
Prophethood has both preparatory and nurturing characteristics that together ensure its perfection.

Al-Nasafi provides a systematic classification of the special qualities (khawass) of prophethood, dividing them into two main categories: those that establish its foundation and those that nurture its strengths. This classification helps us appreciate the comprehensive nature of prophethood, encompassing lineage, personal perfection, revelation, and divine correction. The passage then confirms that all these characteristics were present in Prophet Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two main divisions
Read the passage to identify the two overarching categories: preparatory characteristics (for establishing the foundation) and nurturing characteristics (for strengthening the powers).
The Arabic uses 'القسم الأول' and 'القسم الثاني'.
2
Examine the first division's subcategories
Look for the two sub-parts under the first main division: lineage selection (الاختيار الجنسي) and possession of virtuous faculties with absence of defects.
Lineage selection ensures nobility; virtuous faculties include perfect intellect, character, and physique.
3
Examine the second division's subcategories
Identify the two sub-parts under the second main division: continuous connection to revelation (اتصال مواد الوحي) and divine warning about minor slips (تنبيه عن مواقع الزلل).
The author supports each with a Quranic verse: 93:3 and 9:43.
4
Confirm the completeness in Prophet Muhammad
Note the concluding statement that all these characteristics with their divisions were available in our Prophet Muhammad.
This shows the perfection of his prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يتحدث عن خواص النبوة ويجملها في قسمين الأول: لتمهيد أمرها وتوطيد أساسها. والثاني – لتربية قواها . ويقسم الأول إلى قسمين أحدهما - الاختيار الجنسي، ويتوفر فيه الشرف. والثاني - توفر القوى الفاضلة فيه، وإبعاد العلل عنه، فلقد كان كامل العقل، والخلق، والخلقة. أما القسم الثاني فيقسمه إلى قسمين: الأول اتصال مواد الوحي به، ويؤيد ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ مَا وَدَعَكَ رَبُّكَ وَمَا قَلَى ﴾ [الضحى : ٣] والثاني – تنبيه عن مواقع الزلل مثل قوله تعالى : ﴿ عَفَا اللَّهُ عَنكَ لِمَ أَذِنتَ لَهُمْ ﴾ [التوبة: ٤٣] . والخواص كلها بأقسامها توفرت في نبينا محمد .
And after that he speaks about the special characteristics of prophethood and summarizes them into two divisions: the first: for preparing its matter and establishing its foundation. And the second: for nurturing its strengths. And he divides the first into two parts: one of them: lineage selection, and in it nobility is available. And the second: the availability of virtuous faculties in him, and removal of defects from him, for he was perfect in intellect, character, and physical constitution. As for the second division, he divides it into two parts: the first: connection of the materials of revelation to him, and that is supported by His saying: {Your Lord has not forsaken you nor does He hate} [Ad-Duha: 3]; and the second: warning about points of stumbling, such as His saying: {May Allah forgive you; why did you permit them} [At-Tawba: 43]. And all the characteristics with their divisions were available in our Prophet Muhammad.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage classifies the special qualities (khawass) of prophethood into two main categories: (1) those that prepare and establish the groundwork for prophethood, and (2) those that nurture and strengthen its powers. The first category is subdivided into (a) lineage selection (ikhtiyar jinsi), meaning the Prophet was from a noble lineage, and (b) the presence of excellent faculties and absence of defects, as he was perfect in intellect ('aql), character (khuluq), and physical constitution (khilqa). The second category is subdivided into (a) continuous connection to revelation (ittisal mawadd al-wahy), supported by Quran 93:3, and (b) divine admonition for minor slips (tanbih 'an mawaqi' al-zalal), as in Quran 9:43. Al-Nasafi concludes that all these characteristics, in all their divisions, were present in Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him.

Key Terms
خواص النبوة
الاختيار الجنسي
القوى الفاضلة
اتصال مواد الوحي
تنبيه عن مواقع الزلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think prophethood is only about performing miracles and receiving revelation.
Correction: This passage shows that prophethood also involves divine preparation through noble lineage and personal perfection (intellect, character, physique), as well as ongoing divine nurturing through revelation and gentle correction for minor slips.

Checkpoint
What are the two main divisions of the characteristics of prophethood according to al-Nasafi?
A) Lineage and intellect.
B) Preparatory and nurturing.
C) Spiritual and material.
D) Revelation and miracles.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's classification shows that prophethood involves divine preparation through lineage and personal perfection, and ongoing nurturing through revelation and correction – all fulfilled in Prophet Muhammad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet ﷺ was perfect in intellect, character, and constitution. In which of these areas do you feel most deficient, and what concrete step will you take this week to improve yourself in that area?
?
Allah provided gentle correction to the Prophet ﷺ in the Quran. Think of a recent mistake you made. Did you accept correction with humility, or did you become defensive? How can you emulate the Prophet's response to divine admonition?
?
Prophethood required noble lineage and divine selection. Your standing before Allah is not based on your ancestry. How does this truth challenge your pride or your sense of unworthiness? What can you do today to live as if your worth is determined only by your piety?

The Comprehensiveness of the Prophet's Message
Nasafi enumerates the six components of religion that Muhammad perfectly delivered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet Muhammad's message comprehensively covered all aspects of religion and why this demonstrates his truthfulness.

Core Idea
Muhammad's message encompasses belief, worship, transactions, penalties, manners, and noble traits.

In this passage, Nasafi highlights the all-encompassing nature of the Prophet's message. He shows that the Prophet brought everything necessary for religion, from beliefs to daily interactions, in a way that astonishes sound intellects. This completeness is a sign of his truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about comprehensiveness
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the six categories Nasafi lists.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the six categories
Find each of the six areas of religion mentioned by Nasafi: beliefs, worship, transactions, deterrents, good manners, and praiseworthy traits.
These cover both inner states and outer actions.
3
Reflect on the evidence of truthfulness
Consider why bringing such a perfect and comprehensive system is evidence that the Prophet was truthful. Nasafi says sound intellects marvel at it.
Think about how no human could invent such a complete system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يتكلم عما هو راجع إلى شريعة الإسلام فيقول: إن مدار أمور الدين والملة في جميع الأديان يكون متعلقا بالاعتقادات والعبادات، والمعاملات، والمزاجر، والآداب الحسنة، والشيم الحميدة، وقد أتى محمد بكل هذه الأمور بما تتعجب منه العقول الصحيحة، وتقدره أجل التقدير. ويتحدث بالتفصيل عن كل أمر من الأمور السابقة، وبعد ذلك يقول بالحرف: - وبالوقوف على ما تقدم عرف أنه عليه السلام كان صادقا فيما ادعاه من رسالة ربه معرفة لا يشوبها شك ، ويعتبر الموانع عن الإزعان لدعوته عليه السلام ترجع دائما إلى أمور منها: أ - العصبية الجنسية. ب الأنفة المانعة من اتباع الغير. ج والإلف والهوى. د - الكسل عن لوازم التكليف.
And after that he speaks about what pertains to the Law (sharīʿa) of Islam, saying: Indeed the pivot of the affairs of religion and the community (al-milla) in all religions is connected to beliefs (iʿtiqādāt), acts of worship (ʿibādāt), transactions (muʿāmalāt), deterrents (mazājir), good manners (ādāb ḥasana), and praiseworthy traits (shiyam ḥamīda). And Muḥammad brought all these matters in a way that sound intellects marvel at and appraise with the highest estimation. And he speaks in detail about each one of the aforementioned matters. Then he says verbatim: 'And by contemplating what preceded, it is known that he, peace be upon him, was truthful in his claim of his Lord's message – a knowledge untainted by doubt. And he considers the obstacles to yielding to his call, peace be upon him, to always revert to things including: a) racial partisanship (al-ʿaṣabiyya al-jinsiyya). b) Refusal (al-anafa) preventing following others. c) Familiarity (al-ulf) and desire (al-hawā). d) Laziness (al-kasal) regarding the necessities of obligation (lawāzim al-taklīf).'
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that the Prophet Muḥammad's message is comprehensive, covering all aspects of religion: beliefs, worship, transactions, legal penalties, good character, and noble traits. Sound intellects are amazed at his achievement. After detailing these points, the author quotes the Prophet's conclusion of his own truthfulness beyond doubt. Then he lists four obstacles that prevent people from responding positively: (a) racial or ethnic bias, (b) pride that prevents following someone else, (c) attachment to customs and desires, and (d) laziness regarding the demands of religious obligation. These are psychological and social barriers, not intellectual ones.
