Refuting the Literal Claim of Jinn Speech in Possession
Nasafi refutes literal jinn speech with two arguments and a prophetic analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi argues that a jinn cannot literally speak through a possessed person, and how metaphorical attribution works in such contexts.

Core Idea
Jinn are not literal speakers; possession speech is metaphorical.

This passage tackles a common misunderstanding: when people say a jinn speaks through a possessed person, they often take it literally. Imam Nasafi cuts through this confusion with two clear arguments and a powerful analogy from Islamic tradition. Let's see how he distinguishes literal impossibility from metaphorical attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow.
Notice the strong opening: 'proof of insolvency'.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Nasafi gives two reasons why the jinn cannot literally speak. Spot them: first, the tongue is not within the jinn's power; second, even if the jinn inspires, the speech is metaphorical.
3
Study the 'Umar analogy
The hadith about the angel speaking on 'Umar's tongue is key. Understand why it is metaphorical and how it parallels the jinn case.
The angel does not literally speak; it inspires 'Umar to speak.
4
Understand the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that attributing speech to the jinn is metaphorical, not literal. Reflect why this distinction matters in theology.
Metaphor preserves the created nature of the jinn and the unity of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من قول الناس في كلام المصروع أن الجني يتكلم على لسانه دليل الإفلاس، فإن ذلك ليس بقول من يلزم الانقياد لقوله والتعلق مثله لا يجدي نفعا. ثم نقول: إذا لم يكن لسان المصروع محلا لقدرة الجني، ومخلوق ما لا قدرة على ما هو خارج عن حيز قدرته عندنا فلا يتصور عندنا أن يكون ذلك الكلام فعلا للجني. ثم نقول : لو ثبت أن أحدا يقول ذلك يريد به أن الجني يلقي في قلب المصروع شيئا فيجرع المصروع ذلك على لسانه، فأضيف ذلك إلى الجني لكونه حاملا للمصروع على إجراء ذلك على لسانه يحققه ما روي أنه قال: "إن الملك لينطق على لسان عمر لم يكن ذلك فعلا للملك لأنه لو كان كذلك لم يكن لعمر منقبة بل كان فعلا لعمر إلا أن الملك لما كان يلقي ذلك في قلبه حتى أظهره عمر بلسانه أضيف إلى الملك على طريق المجاز فكذا هذا، وقد روي في بعض الروايات: "إن الحق لينطق على لسان عمر وقيل إن الحق هو الله تعالى وليس ذلك بكلام الله تعالى. وهذا مما لا خفاء به. وبالله المعونة.
And what is mentioned of the people's saying concerning the speech of the possessed, that the jinn speaks upon his tongue, is a proof of insolvency, for that is not a statement of one whose statement must be followed, and clinging to the like of it does not avail. Then we say: Since the tongue of the possessed is not a locus of power for the jinn, and a created thing has no power over what is outside the sphere of its power according to us, then it is inconceivable according to us that that speech could be an act of the jinn. Then we say: If it were established that someone says that, intending by it that the jinn casts something into the heart of the possessed, and the possessed utters that upon his tongue, then it is attributed to the jinn because it is what impels the possessed to utter that upon his tongue. This is verified by what is narrated that he said: 'Indeed the angel speaks upon the tongue of 'Umar'—that was not an act of the angel, because if it were so, 'Umar would have no merit; rather, it was an act of 'Umar. But because the angel cast that into his heart until 'Umar expressed it with his tongue, it was attributed to the angel metaphorically. So likewise this. And it has been narrated in some narrations: 'Indeed the Truth speaks upon the tongue of 'Umar,' and it is said that the Truth is God Almighty, but that is not the speech of God Almighty. This is something not hidden. And with God is aid.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that a jinn speaks through the tongue of a possessed person (masru'). The author begins by dismissing such talk as a sign of weakness (iflas), not worth following. Then he gives two main points: First, the tongue of the possessed is not within the jinn's power, and a created being (makhluq) cannot act beyond its sphere of power, so the jinn cannot literally perform speech through that tongue. Second, even if one claims that the jinn inspires something in the heart of the possessed who then speaks it, this attribution of speech to the jinn is metaphorical, not literal. The author supports this with the well-known report that 'the angel speaks upon the tongue of 'Umar,' where it is not literal speech by the angel but rather the angel inspires 'Umar, who then speaks. Thus the speech is actually 'Umar's, attributed metaphorically to the angel. Similarly, the phrase 'the Truth speaks upon the tongue of 'Umar' (where Truth is God) is metaphorical and does not mean that God is literally speaking. The author concludes that this is obvious.

Key Terms
مصروع
جني
مجاز
ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that the phrase 'the jinn speaks on his tongue' means the jinn literally produces speech through the possessed person's vocal cords.
Correction: Imam Nasafi argues that such attribution is metaphorical, similar to the angel inspiring 'Umar, not literal speech.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can't the jinn literally speak through the possessed person's tongue?
A) Because the jinn has no power over the tongue
B) Because the possessed person is not a jinn
C) Because speech requires a soul
D) Because the jinn is not a speaker

Key Takeaway
Attributing speech to a jinn in possession is a metaphor, not a literal reality, just as the angel's 'speaking' on 'Umar's tongue is metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been quick to attribute a difficult situation to supernatural forces (like jinn possession or black magic) without first considering natural or metaphorical explanations? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own assumptions and seek deeper understanding before jumping to conclusions? Will you commit to pausing and reflecting before attributing events to unseen causes?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said 'the angel speaks on the tongue of 'Umar.' Have you ever felt a sudden insight or inspiration that you suspected came from a good source? How can you tell whether that inspiration is truly from God or an angel, or just your own thoughts? This lesson teaches that inspired words are still your own act and responsibility. Will you take ownership of your words and thoughts, knowing that even divine inspiration is expressed through your own agency?
?
This lesson distinguishes literal from metaphorical attribution. Think of a time you used religious language ('the devil made me do it') to excuse a sin. Did you really believe it was literal? How does Nasafi's rigorous approach—refusing to attribute actions to forces outside your power—call you to take full responsibility for your choices today? What one specific habit will you change to reflect this accountability?

Prophetic Truthfulness and Divine Foreknowledge
Dialectical argument forcing opponent to accept divine foreknowledge or fall into contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical argument that forces the opponent to either affirm the truthfulness of prophetic future reports or fall into contradiction.

Core Idea
Prophetic truthfulness about the future is inseparable from divine foreknowledge.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents a powerful dialectical argument against those who deny that Allah's future reports can be considered truthful. Using a dilemma and Quranic examples, the author shows that rejecting the truthfulness of these reports leads to absurdity or disbelief. Let's trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
The argument opens with a forced choice: is the Prophet truthful or a liar? See the two dead ends.
Notice that the opponent cannot escape without self-contradiction or disbelief.
2
Understand the extension to future reports
The author argues that lying applies to future reports just as to past ones, especially on the opponent's own principle.
The opponent had used a similar argument about divine forgiveness.
3
Examine the Quranic examples
Three Quranic verses that predicted future events now past are cited. Notice how these demonstrate that the reports were true despite being about the future.
The examples show that the events actually took place.
4
See the final trap
The opponent must either admit the verses are true (invalidating his argument) or call them lies (disbelief).
This completes the reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the theological point
This argument upholds that Allah's knowledge encompasses all times and His reports are truthful.
The passage does not imply that Allah's knowledge changes; rather the known changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكاذب هو عليه السلام أم صادق؟ فإن قال : هو صادق، فقد أبطل احتجاجه ووقع فيما عاب، وإن قال : هو كاذب فقد كفر ، وإن قال : هو صادق لأن ثبوت ذلك قد تقرر في علمه كما يتقرر ثبوت ما وقع فإذا قال: منعت قيل فاقبل منا هذا القدر. ثم يقال له: أليس أن الكذب كما يتحقق في الإخبار عن الماضي بأن كان إخبارا عن المخبر لا على ما هو به، يتحقق في المستقبل أيضا إذا كان إخبارا عن المخبر لا على ما هو به خصوصا على أصلكم، حتى نسبتم القائلين بجواز عفو الله عمن مات من أصحاب الكبائر قبل التوبة إلى القول بتكذيب الله تعالى. فلابد من بلى، فيقال: أليس أن الله تعالى قال : قُل لِلْمُخَلَّفِينَ مِنَ الْأَعْرَابِ سَتُدْعَوْنَ إِلَى قَوْمٍ أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ تقَتِلُونَهُمْ أَوْ يُسْلِمُونَ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : أليس أن هذا قد مضى لأن المخلفين من الأعراب قد انقرضوا ؟ فلما كان الدعاء لهم لم يوجد حال حياتهم وانقرضوا قبل الدعاء، كان هذا إخبارا عن المخبر لا على ما هو به يدل عليه أن بعض أهل التأويل قال : المراد من الآية دعاؤهم إلى قتال بني حنيفة. وقال بعضهم: المراد منه، دعاؤهم إلى قتال أهل فارس، وقد دعوا إلى كل واحد منهما ومضى ذلك. وكذا قوله تعالى: لَتَدْخُلُنَّ الْمَسْجِدَ الْحَرَامَ قد مضى. وكذا قوله تعالى: وَهُم من بَعْدِ غَلَبِهِمْ سَيَغْلِبُونَ قد مضى ذلك ولا وجود لشيء من ذلك في المستقبل بعد مضي هذه الحوادث. فإن قالوا: نعم كل ذلك قد مضى. قيل: أكذب هذه الآيات أم لا ؟ فإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا دليلهم وإن قالوا: نعم. كفروا.
Is he, peace be upon him, a liar or truthful? If he says: "He is truthful," he invalidates his argument and falls into what he criticized; if he says: "He is a liar," he disbelieves. And if he says: "He is truthful because the confirmation of that is established in His knowledge as the confirmation of what occurred is established," then if he says: "I deny that," it is said: "Then accept from us this much." Then it is said to him: "Is not lying, just as it is realized in reporting about the past when the report is not in accordance with what is reported, also realized in the future if it is a report not in accordance with what is reported, especially according to your principle, until you have attributed to those who allow God's forgiveness for someone who died from amongst the major sinners before repentance the statement that they accuse God of lying?" So he must say "yes." Then it is said: "Is it not that God Almighty said: 'Say to those who were left behind among the Bedouins: You will be called to a people of great might; you will fight them or they will submit.' So he must say 'yes.' It is said: 'Is not that this has passed because the left-behind among the Bedouins have perished? Since the calling to them did not occur during their lifetimes and they perished before the calling, this was a report not in accordance with what is reported.' This is indicated by the fact that some commentators said: 'What is meant by the verse is their being called to fight Banu Hanifa.' And some said: 'What is meant is their being called to fight the people of Persia, and they were called to each of them and that has passed.' And likewise His saying: 'You will certainly enter the Sacred Mosque' has passed. And likewise His saying: 'And they, after their defeat, will overcome' has passed, and there is no existence of any of that in the future after the occurrence of these events.' Then if they say: 'Yes, all that has passed.' It is said: 'Do you declare these verses to be lies or not?' If they say: 'No,' they invalidate their argument, and if they say: 'Yes,' they disbelieve.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument (jadal) against an opponent who denies that God's future reports can be considered true. The author poses a dilemma: if the opponent says the Prophet is truthful, he invalidates his own objection; if he says he is a liar, he commits disbelief. Then the author argues that lying can also occur in future reports, especially according to the opponent's own principle regarding forgiveness. He cites Quranic verses that predicted future events that have now passed, yet were true. The opponent cannot deny their truth without disbelief, so his argument collapses.

Key Terms
كذب
صدق
علم الله
إخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a future report from Allah does not occur exactly as stated, then Allah's knowledge is false or changes.
Correction: The passage shows that Allah's reports about the future are always true because they come to pass exactly as predicted. The examples given have already occurred, proving their truthfulness.

Checkpoint
In the dialectical argument, what is the consequence if the opponent says that the Prophet is truthful?
A) He invalidates his own argument
B) He becomes a disbeliever
C) He accepts divine foreknowledge
D) He denies the Quran

Key Takeaway
The truthfulness of prophetic reports about the future is necessary, as denying it leads to contradiction or disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a prophetic report about the future because it seemed unlikely to you? In doing so, did you consider that you were effectively accusing the Prophet of lying? How will you change your attitude towards such reports from now on?
?
The opponent tried to use Allah's knowledge as an excuse to avoid the implication. Are there truths from revelation that you rationalize away rather than submit to? What would it cost you to simply accept them?
?
If you were in the opponent's position in this argument, would you have admitted defeat or stubbornly insisted? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to your own opinions over the truth of Allah's word? Commit to one change in how you approach difficult theological questions.

The Linguistic Rule of 'Ja'ala' with Two Objects
Nasafi establishes the linguistic rule of 'ja'ala' to refute the creationist interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the linguistic analysis of 'ja'ala' refutes the claim that the Qur'an is created based on verse 43:3.

Core Idea
When 'ja'ala' takes two objects, it means naming, not creating.

The opponents of the Maturidi position cite the verse 'Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Qur'an' to argue that the Qur'an was created. However, a careful linguistic analysis of the verb 'ja'ala' reveals that when it governs two objects—as in this verse—it denotes naming or declaring, not bringing into existence. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows why the verse does not support the claim of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the first part of the Arabic passage (from 'فأما حل الشبهة' to 'مفعولين') along with its translation. Focus on how the author introduces the doubt and the linguistic principle.
Notice that the author begins by stating that the opponents have no support from this verse.
2
Understand the linguistic rule
Identify the two cases: 'ja'ala' with one object means creation; with two objects means naming. The author provides Qur'anic examples. Write down the rule.
3
Apply the rule to the disputed verse
The verse 'إنا جعلناه قرآنا عربيا' has two objects: 'ه' and 'قرآنا عربيا'. Therefore, it means 'We named it an Arabic Qur'an', not 'We created it.' Confirm this with the author's interpretation.
The author interprets it as 'جعلنا العبارة عنه بلسان العرب' (We made the expression about it in the Arabic tongue).
4
Review the examples provided
The author cites two additional verses where 'ja'ala' takes two objects: about the angels and about dividing the Qur'an. See how these reinforce the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على الشبهة السمعية ]
فأما حل الشبهة السمعية فنقول : لا تعلق لهم بقوله تعالى: {إِنَّا جَعَلْنَهُ قُرْءَانَا عَرَبِيًّا} ( ) لأن معناه والله أعلم ، جعلنا العبارة عنه بلسان العرب وأفهمنا المراد به وأحكامه باللسان العربي. ثم إن أهل اللغة قالوا: إذا تعدى الجعل إلى مفعول واحد كان بمعنى الفعل والخلق كقوله تعالى: {الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِى خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ } وَجَعَلَ الظُّلُمَتِ وَالنُّورَ (٢) وإذا تعدى إلى المفعولين لا يكون بمعنى الخلق بل يكون بمعنى الحكم والتسمية. قال الله تعالى - خبرا عن الكفرة -: {وَجَعَلُوا الْمَلَائِكَةَ الَّذِينَ هُمْ عِبَدُ الرَّحْمَنِ إِنَاثًا} (۳). والمراد منه التسمية لا التخليق. وكذا في قوله: {الَّذِينَ جَعَلُوا الْقُرْءَانَ عِضِينَ} (٤) المراد منه التسمية لا التخليق، وفي هذه الآية تتعدى إلى مفعولين.
(۷)
(ه)
والمراد من قوله تعالى : {مَا يَأْتِيهِم مِّن ذِكْرٍ مِّن رَّبِّهِم مُحْدَثٍ} (0) يحتمل أن الذكر هو الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم على ما قال : {ذَكَرَ رَسُولًا} (1) فيكون تأويله ما يأتيهم (۸) من رسول محدث (إلا) استمعوه أي) (۹) (سمعوا قوله ) (۱۰) ويحتمل أن يكون المزاد منه ما يأتيهم من وعظ من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم (وهذا لأنهم ما كانوا يضحكون عند قراءة القرآن بل كانوا يعظمون ويفخمون شأنه، ويحتمل من ذكر حديث العهد بهم، لا أن يكون في نفسه حديثا، كمن له غلام فاشترى آخر قد يقول: ادعوا غلامي الحديث وإن كان أكبر سنا من الأول، على أن صرف هذه الآيات إلى هذه العبارات المحدثة ممكن فلم يبق للخصوم في محل النزاع دليل (١١) .
[The Response to the Scriptural Doubt]
As for the resolution of the scriptural doubt, we say: They have no support in His statement, the Exalted: {Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Qur'an} (43:3) because its meaning—and God knows best—is that We made the expression about it in the tongue of the Arabs and We conveyed the intended meaning and its rulings in the Arabic tongue. Then the linguists said: When "ja'ala" (made) is transitive to one object, it means action and creation, as in His statement: {Praise be to God, who created the heavens and the earth and made the darkness and the light} (6:1). And when it is transitive to two objects, it does not mean creation but rather means declaring (hukm) and naming (tasmiya). God said—informing about the disbelievers—: {And they made the angels, who are servants of the Most Merciful, females} (43:19). The meaning here is naming, not creation. Likewise in His statement: {Those who made the Qur'an into portions} (15:91), the meaning is naming, not creation. And in this verse, it is transitive to two objects.
And the intended meaning of His statement: {No mention comes to them from their Lord that is newly-conveyed} (21:2) is that "dhikr" (mention) could be the Messenger, peace be upon him, as He said: {He has mentioned a messenger} (65:10-11). So its interpretation is that no newly-created (muhdath) messenger comes to them except they listen to him, i.e., they hear his words. And it could mean that what comes to them is an admonition from the Prophet, peace be upon him—this because they did not laugh when reciting the Qur'an but rather revered and magnified its status. And it could be a mention of recent occurrence with respect to them, not that it is in itself new. Like a man who has a servant and buys another; he might say: "Call my new servant," even if he is older than the first. Since it is possible to apply these verses to these newly-created expressions (al-'ibarat al-muhdatha), there remains no proof for the opponents in the point of contention.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a scriptural doubt (shubha sam'iyya) that the Qur'an is created (muhdath). The author begins by stating that the opponents have no support from the verse {Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Qur'an} because 'ja'ala' here means 'made the expression about it in Arabic', not 'created'. He then cites linguists: when 'ja'ala' takes one object, it means creation; when it takes two objects, it means declaring or naming. Applying this, the verse {And they made the angels females} means they named them females, not created them. Likewise, {Those who made the Qur'an into portions} means they divided it by naming parts. The verse under dispute also has two objects, so it means 'We made it an Arabic Qur'an' i.e., We named it or decreed it as such, not created it. Next, the author addresses the verse {No mention (dhikr) comes to them from their Lord that is newly-conveyed (muhdath)}. He gives three interpretations: (1) 'dhikr' refers to the Messenger, so 'muhdath' means a newly-sent messenger; (2) it refers to renewed admonition from the Prophet; (3) it means a mention of recent occurrence relative to them, not that the Qur'an itself is new. He provides an analogy: a man may call a newly-purchased servant 'my new servant' even if he is older. Since these interpretations are plausible, the opponents have no proof for their claim that the Qur'an is created. This refutation relies on linguistic analysis and contextual reasoning.

Key Terms
جعل
مفعولين
تسمية
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'إنا جعلناه قرآنا عربيا' proves the Qur'an is created because 'ja'ala' means 'made'.
Correction: When 'ja'ala' takes two objects, it means 'declared' or 'named', not 'created'. The verse means 'We made it an Arabic Qur'an' in the sense of expression and revelation, not creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the verb 'ja'ala' mean when it takes two objects?
A) Creation
B) Naming and declaring
C) Action
D) Transformation

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verse 'إنا جعلناه قرآنا عربيا' does not indicate creation because 'ja'ala' with two objects means naming, not creating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a verse like 'إنا جعلناه قرآنا عربيا' and assumed it meant the Qur'an was created, without realizing that a simple grammatical rule changes the entire meaning? How many times have you misinterpreted Allah's words due to a lack of linguistic precision? Will you commit to learning Arabic grammar to protect yourself from such errors?
?
If the Mu'tazila used this very verse to argue for the createdness of the Qur'an, how many Muslims might have been led astray? How would you feel if you discovered that your own carelessness in understanding God's speech caused someone else to doubt? What steps will you take to ensure you never treat the Qur'an's language lightly?
?
The rule of 'ja'ala' is a small grammatical point, yet it defends the eternal uncreatedness of the Qur'an. Are you willing to give even 'small' Islamic sciences their due weight? Or will you let intellectual laziness deprive you of the strength of your faith? Commit today to study one such rule deeply.

Interpreting 'Muhdath' as Newly Conveyed
Nasafi provides alternative interpretations of 'dhikr muhdath' to refute the creationist claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple interpretations of 'dhikr muhdath' in verse 21:2 that deflect the claim that the Qur'an is created.

Core Idea
The 'new mention' in the verse refers to a messenger or admonition, not the Qur'an's essence.

Another scriptural doubt comes from the verse 'No mention comes to them from their Lord that is newly-conveyed (muhdath).' Opponents take 'muhdath' as proof that the Qur'an itself is created. Nasafi offers three plausible interpretations that shift the meaning: 'dhikr' can refer to the Messenger himself, or to an admonition from him, or to something that is new relative to the people, not in itself. This lesson explores these interpretations and the analogy that supports them.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'والمراد من قوله تعالى' to the end of the Arabic passage, along with translation. Notice the three interpretations.
2
Identify the three interpretations
List the three possibilities the author gives for 'dhikr' and what 'muhdath' means in each case. Write them down.
Interpretation 1: dhikr = Messenger; Interpretation 2: dhikr = admonition; Interpretation 3: dhikr = something recent relative to them.
3
Understand the analogy
The author gives an example of a man who calls his newer servant 'my new servant' even if he is older than the first. This shows that 'new' can be relative.
4
Conclude the refutation
Since these interpretations are possible, the verse does not necessarily prove the Qur'an is created. The author states that because these applications are possible, the opponents have no proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على الشبهة السمعية ]
فأما حل الشبهة السمعية فنقول : لا تعلق لهم بقوله تعالى: {إِنَّا جَعَلْنَهُ قُرْءَانَا عَرَبِيًّا} ( ) لأن معناه والله أعلم ، جعلنا العبارة عنه بلسان العرب وأفهمنا المراد به وأحكامه باللسان العربي. ثم إن أهل اللغة قالوا: إذا تعدى الجعل إلى مفعول واحد كان بمعنى الفعل والخلق كقوله تعالى: {الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِى خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ } وَجَعَلَ الظُّلُمَتِ وَالنُّورَ (٢) وإذا تعدى إلى المفعولين لا يكون بمعنى الخلق بل يكون بمعنى الحكم والتسمية. قال الله تعالى - خبرا عن الكفرة -: {وَجَعَلُوا الْمَلَائِكَةَ الَّذِينَ هُمْ عِبَدُ الرَّحْمَنِ إِنَاثًا} (۳). والمراد منه التسمية لا التخليق. وكذا في قوله: {الَّذِينَ جَعَلُوا الْقُرْءَانَ عِضِينَ} (٤) المراد منه التسمية لا التخليق، وفي هذه الآية تتعدى إلى مفعولين.

والمراد من قوله تعالى : {مَا يَأْتِيهِم مِّن ذِكْرٍ مِّن رَّبِّهِم مُحْدَثٍ} (0) يحتمل أن الذكر هو الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم على ما قال : {ذَكَرَ رَسُولًا} (1) فيكون تأويله ما يأتيهم (۸) من رسول محدث (إلا) استمعوه أي) (۹) (سمعوا قوله ) (۱۰) ويحتمل أن يكون المزاد منه ما يأتيهم من وعظ من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم (وهذا لأنهم ما كانوا يضحكون عند قراءة القرآن بل كانوا يعظمون ويفخمون شأنه، ويحتمل من ذكر حديث العهد بهم، لا أن يكون في نفسه حديثا، كمن له غلام فاشترى آخر قد يقول: ادعوا غلامي الحديث وإن كان أكبر سنا من الأول، على أن صرف هذه الآيات إلى هذه العبارات المحدثة ممكن فلم يبق للخصوم في محل النزاع دليل (١١) .
[The Response to the Scriptural Doubt]
As for the resolution of the scriptural doubt, we say: They have no support in His statement, the Exalted: {Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Qur'an} (43:3) because its meaning—and God knows best—is that We made the expression about it in the tongue of the Arabs and We conveyed the intended meaning and its rulings in the Arabic tongue. Then the linguists said: When "ja'ala" (made) is transitive to one object, it means action and creation, as in His statement: {Praise be to God, who created the heavens and the earth and made the darkness and the light} (6:1). And when it is transitive to two objects, it does not mean creation but rather means declaring (hukm) and naming (tasmiya). God said—informing about the disbelievers—: {And they made the angels, who are servants of the Most Merciful, females} (43:19). The meaning here is naming, not creation. Likewise in His statement: {Those who made the Qur'an into portions} (15:91), the meaning is naming, not creation. And in this verse, it is transitive to two objects.
And the intended meaning of His statement: {No mention comes to them from their Lord that is newly-conveyed} (21:2) is that "dhikr" (mention) could be the Messenger, peace be upon him, as He said: {He has mentioned a messenger} (65:10-11). So its interpretation is that no newly-created (muhdath) messenger comes to them except they listen to him, i.e., they hear his words. And it could mean that what comes to them is an admonition from the Prophet, peace be upon him—this because they did not laugh when reciting the Qur'an but rather revered and magnified its status. And it could be a mention of recent occurrence with respect to them, not that it is in itself new. Like a man who has a servant and buys another; he might say: "Call my new servant," even if he is older than the first. Since it is possible to apply these verses to these newly-created expressions (al-'ibarat al-muhdatha), there remains no proof for the opponents in the point of contention.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a scriptural doubt (shubha sam'iyya) that the Qur'an is created (muhdath). The author begins by stating that the opponents have no support from the verse {Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Qur'an} because 'ja'ala' here means 'made the expression about it in Arabic', not 'created'. He then cites linguists: when 'ja'ala' takes one object, it means creation; when it takes two objects, it means declaring or naming. Applying this, the verse {And they made the angels females} means they named them females, not created them. Likewise, {Those who made the Qur'an into portions} means they divided it by naming parts. The verse under dispute also has two objects, so it means 'We made it an Arabic Qur'an' i.e., We named it or decreed it as such, not created it. Next, the author addresses the verse {No mention (dhikr) comes to them from their Lord that is newly-conveyed (muhdath)}. He gives three interpretations: (1) 'dhikr' refers to the Messenger, so 'muhdath' means a newly-sent messenger; (2) it refers to renewed admonition from the Prophet; (3) it means a mention of recent occurrence relative to them, not that the Qur'an itself is new. He provides an analogy: a man may call a newly-purchased servant 'my new servant' even if he is older. Since these interpretations are plausible, the opponents have no proof for their claim that the Qur'an is created. This refutation relies on linguistic analysis and contextual reasoning.

Key Terms
محدث
ذكر
رسول
وعظ
حديث العهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'muhdath' in verse 21:2 proves that the Qur'an itself is created because it means 'new'.
Correction: It can refer to a messenger, an admonition, or a relative newness, not the Qur'an's essence. The Qur'an itself is eternal; only its expression is new.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one possible meaning of 'dhikr' in verse 21:2?
A) The Qur'an itself
B) The Messenger
C) The Torah
D) The angels

Key Takeaway
The verse 'ما يأتيهم من ذكر من ربهم محدث' does not prove the Qur'an is created because 'dhikr' can refer to a messenger or admonition, and 'muhdath' can mean relatively new.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'no mention comes to them that is newly-conveyed,' do you assume it refers to the Qur'an, or do you pause to consider other possibilities? What does that reveal about your relationship with scripture—do you accept ambiguity or rush to certainty?
?
Nasafi offers multiple interpretations of a single verse to defend the timelessness of the Qur'an. Are you willing to entertain that a verse might have more than one valid meaning, even when your own understanding seems clear? Or does that feel like a threat to your faith?
?
If the Qur'an were truly created in time, would that change how you view its authority over your life? Reflect honestly: do you treat the Qur'an as a living, eternal address from God, or as a relic of a past age? What would change if you truly believed every verse is 'newly-conveyed' to you today?

Musa's Sufficiency of Signs
Nasafi argues Musa had enough signs, so request was not for a sign

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument that Prophet Musa's request was not for a sign, as he already possessed an abundance of miracles.

Core Idea
Abundant signs make further requests unreasonable for any prophet.

In this passage, Nasafi presents his fourth argument against ru'yat Allah based on the principle that the Prophet Musa already had sufficient sensory signs. The logic is that even the most foolish person would not need more evidence after witnessing such clear miracles, so requesting a further sign would be an act of obstinacy—and prophets are protected from that. This sets the stage for interpreting Musa's request as something other than a plea for a sensory proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation once, noting the list of signs and the rhetorical strength of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أغبى قومه' to grasp the emphasis.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument: Premise 1: Musa had many clear signs. Premise 2: These sufficed even the dullest minds. Conclusion: Requesting another sign would be obstinacy, impossible for a chosen prophet.
This is a deductive argument with an implied major premise.
3
Examine the implication
Reflect on why Nasafi uses the example of Musa to argue against the vision of Allah. If Musa's request was not for a mere sign, it must have been for something else—vision of Allah.
This connects to the broader debate about seeing Allah.
4
Consider the objection
Anticipate a possible objection: Couldn't Musa have wanted a new sign? Nasafi's answer is that prophets do not engage in senseless demands; thus the request was for a higher matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع أن موسى كان وقف على آيات الله تعالى من قلب العصا حية، وتفجير الماء من الحجر، وفلق البحر، واليد البيضاء، وغير ذلك مما خصه الله تعالى به من الآيات الحسية بحيث يستغني عن طلب آية أخرى، بل استغنى عن ذلك أغبى قومه وأبلد خليقة الله تعالى فيما لكثرتها وتراد فيها في أنفسها، وطلب الآيات بعد ظهور ما به الغنية للعاقل كان من تعنت الكفرة، فلا يجوز مثله من موسى مع اصطفاء الله تعالى إياه برسالته وكلامه وبفضله على عالمي زمانه.
And the fourth: that Moses had come to know the signs of Allah Almighty: the turning of the staff into a serpent, the gushing of water from the stone, the splitting of the sea, the white hand, and other sensory signs with which Allah Almighty had distinguished him, such that he was independent of requesting another sign—rather, even the most foolish of his people and the most obtuse of Allah's creatures were independent of that due to their abundance and succession in themselves—and requesting signs after the appearance of what suffices the intelligent person is from the obstinacy of the disbelievers, so it is not permissible for the like of Moses, given Allah Almighty's selection of him for His message and His speech and His favor over the scholars of his time.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument: Musa had already witnessed many miraculous signs (like the staff turning into a snake, water from the rock, the parting of the sea, the white hand). Even the most foolish among his people did not need further signs. To ask for more after what was sufficient is the behavior of obstinate disbelievers. Therefore, it is inconceivable that a prophet of Musa's stature would request a mere sign; his request must have been for something else—vision of Allah.

Musa's multitude of miracles precludes the possibility that his request was for a sensory sign.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Nasafi says Musa was above obstinacy because of his selection. If you claim to love Allah, why do you still hesitate to obey Him after all the signs He has given you? What sign are you waiting for that hasn't already been given?
?
The signs were abundant and consecutive. Yet you still find yourself complaining about your situation. What specific sign in your life today (like Musa's staff) are you ignoring? What would it take for you to be 'independent of requesting another sign'?

Second argument: Prophets would not delay refuting disbelief
Nasafi argues that if vision were impossible, Musa would have immediately corrected his people

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the principle that prophets immediately refute disbelief to argue that the request to see Allah is not itself disbelief.

Core Idea
Prophets refute disbelief instantly; Musa did not refute the vision request, so it is not disbelief.

Nasafi now presents a second argument based on prophetic behavior. If requesting to see Allah were disbelief, then Musa—as a prophet—would have immediately corrected his people, just as he did when they asked for a physical idol. Since he did not reject the vision request but rather asked Allah on their behalf, the request cannot be inherently disbelieving. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the counterfactual premise
The argument begins with a conditional: if ru'yah were impermissible and disbelief, then Musa would have immediately refuted his people when they asked. Understand this as a necessary prophetic duty.
Prophets are sent to eliminate disbelief, not tolerate it.
2
Examine the analogy with the idol request
Recall how Musa immediately rebuked the Israelites when they asked for a physical idol (Quran 7:138). This shows his pattern of instant refutation for true disbelief.
This incident is presented as evidence of how Musa handles kufr requests.
3
Contrast with his response to the vision request
When the same people asked to see Allah, Musa did not rebuke them. Instead he asked Allah for the vision. This contrast proves the vision request is not kufr.
If it were kufr, he would have acted as he did with the idol request.
4
Consider the objection and answer
One might object: maybe Musa did rebuke them but they would not listen, so he asked Allah. The passage begins to address this but is cut off; nevertheless, the core argument stands.
The objection does not weaken the main point—the initial rebuke would still have occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أن الرؤية لو لم تكن جائزة على الله تعالى وكان كفرا لكان موسى عليه السلام لم يؤخر الرد عليهم بل كان يرد عليهم وقت قرع كلامهم سمعه، ولم يتركهم واعتقاد ما لا يجوز اعتقاده لما فيه من التقرير على الكفر والأنبياء عليهم السلام بعثوا لتغييره لا لتقريره، ألا ترى أنهم لما قالوا له: لنَا إِلَنهَا كَمَا هُمْ وَالِهَهُ (٦) كيف لم يمهلهم بل رد عليهم من ساعتهم بقوله: ﴿ إِنَّكُمْ قَوْمٌ تَجْهَلُونَ ) ولا يقال إنه كان يرد عليهم وكانوا لا يقبلون قوله فسأل من الله ذلك
And the second: that if vision were not permissible for Allah Almighty and were disbelief, then Moses (peace be upon him) would not have delayed responding to them, but rather he would have responded to them at the moment their speech reached his hearing, and would not have left them with a belief that is not permissible to believe because of the implied confirmation of disbelief, and the prophets (peace be upon them) were sent to change it, not to confirm it. Do you not see that when they said to him: 'Make for us a god as they have gods' (7:138), how he did not give them respite but responded to them at that very hour with his saying: 'Indeed, you are a people behaving ignorantly' (7:138). And let it not be said that he was responding to them and they were not accepting his words, so he asked Allah for that.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (likely from a separate list or continuation). The argument states: If requesting to see Allah were disbelief, then as a prophet, Musa would have immediately refuted his people when they asked for a similar thing (a physical god). He did not; he instead asked Allah for vision himself. This shows that he did not consider the request as disbelief. The example given is when the Israelites asked for a physical idol; Musa immediately rebuked them. But when they asked to see Allah, he did not rebuke but rather asked Allah. This indicates that the request for vision is not inherently disbelief.

Key Terms
التقرير على الكفر
الرد الفوري
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Musa's delay in refuting the vision request means he approved of it or that the request is acceptable as a form of worship.
Correction: The argument actually shows that Musa's delay indicates he did not consider the request as disbelief, not that he approved of it as a proper act. The request was not kufr, so no immediate refutation was required.

Checkpoint

A) He was afraid of his people.
B) He did not consider it disbelief.
C) He was waiting for a revelation.
D) He was confused.

Key Takeaway
Musa's lack of immediate rebuke for the request to see Allah shows that such a request is not inherently disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone around you holding a false belief about Allah, do you feel the same urgency to correct it that Musa showed with the idol request, or do you stay silent? What does your silence reveal about your own iman?
?
If you were in Musa's position, and your community asked for something that seemed to border on shirk, would you have the courage to rebuke them immediately, or would you hesitate out of fear of losing their approval? What does that hesitation say about where your heart truly lies?
?
Think of a time you delayed speaking the truth about a matter of aqeedah because you thought it would be uncomfortable or ineffective. How does this lesson expose that excuse as invalid? Will you commit today to speaking the truth immediately, even when it's hard?

The Ruling on Attributing Sins to Prophets
Nasafi states that attributing sin to a prophet is disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing sins to prophets is considered disbelief and the underlying principle of presumption against Allah.

Core Idea
Attributing sin to a prophet is disbelief because it implies presumption against Allah.

This lesson addresses a serious theological ruling: the status of those who attribute sins to prophets. The passage from Nasafi clarifies that such an attribution stems from presumption (tajassur) against Allah and questioning what is not permissible for Him. It declares without ambiguity that anyone who does this is a disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Notice the strong language: 'لا شك في كفره' (no doubt in his disbelief).
The passage is short but carries a weighty ruling.
2
Understand the key term 'التجاسر'
The word 'التجاسر' (tajassur) means presumption or audacity. In this context, it refers to daring to question Allah's justice or wisdom by accusing a prophet of sin.
This concept is central to why the act is kufr.
3
Connect the ruling to the sanctity of prophets
Reflect on why attributing sin to a prophet is equivalent to presumption against Allah. Since prophets are protected from sin (isma), accusing them implies that Allah failed to protect them or that He allowed evil in His chosen messengers—both are impermissible regarding Allah.
This connects to the broader doctrine of prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هؤلاء معنى إلا التجاسر على الله تعالى وسؤال ما لا يجوز عليه، ومن استجاز من نفسه أن ينسب نبيا من الأنبياء إلى مثل هذا فلا شك في كفره، والله الموفق.
These people's meaning is nothing but presumption against Allah and asking what is not permissible for Him, and whoever permits from himself to attribute a prophet to such [a thing], there is no doubt in his disbelief. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) concerning those who attribute sin to prophets. It states that the only meaning behind such an attribution is presumption (tajassur) against Allah and asking of Him what is not permissible. It then declares that anyone who allows themselves to attribute a prophet to such a thing is undoubtedly a disbeliever (kafir). The passage emphasizes the sanctity of prophets and the severity of accusing them of sin.

Key Terms
التجاسر
كفر
نسبة الذنب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to attribute minor sins to prophets.
Correction: The passage categorically states that attributing any sin to a prophet is a sign of disbelief, regardless of the sin's magnitude.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on someone who attributes a sin to a prophet?
A) He is a disbeliever
B) He is a sinner but still a believer
C) He is excused if ignorant
D) He should be debated but not judged

Key Takeaway
Attributing sin to a prophet is an act of disbelief because it represents presumption against Allah and disregard for the sanctity of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even in passing, allowed yourself to think less of a prophet's character or actions? What does that reveal about your heart's adab with Allah, and what repentance is required right now?
?
The passage says the motive is 'presumption against Allah'. In what areas of your life today are you presuming upon Allah—whether by questioning His wisdom, expecting His mercy without action, or demanding things not permissible? Make a firm resolve to abandon that presumption.
?
If attributing sin to a prophet is disbelief, how carefully do you guard your tongue and thoughts when discussing the prophets? Will you commit today to seeking knowledge of their impeccable character and defending their honor in every conversation?

Prophethood: Its Necessity and Denial
Al-Nasafi introduces prophethood and states differing views on its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi establishes the framework for discussing prophethood by grounding it in God's wisdom and presenting the opposing views on its necessity.

Core Idea
Prophethood is an expression of God's wisdom, not a random act.

This lesson opens the discussion on prophethood by reaffirming a fundamental principle: everything God does is wise. Al-Nasafi then shows that while all theologians agree on God's existence, they disagree on whether sending messengers is a wise act. The imams of guidance affirm it, while others deny it. This sets the stage for the proofs to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the grounding principle
Notice how Al-Nasafi begins by establishing that God is wise and all His actions are wise. This is the foundation for the discussion.
The phrase 'لا يخرج فعل من أفعاله عن الحكمة إلى السفه' is key.
3
Locate the two opposing positions
Find where Al-Nasafi states that the imams of guidance said sending messengers is wisdom and correctness, and that other groups said it is impossible.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this disagreement matters for the overall proof of prophethood. The commentary will help clarify.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث الفصل الأول الكلام في إثبات النبوة والرسالة ثم إذا ثبت أن الله تعالى حكيم لا يخرج فعل من أفعاله عن الحكمة إلى السفه، ولا يخلو في شيء مما يأتي ويذر عن الوصف له بالإصابة. فبعد انعقاد الإجماع من القائلين بثبوت الصانع على هذه الجملة اختلفوا في أفعال متعينة. أن الله تعالى هل يجوز أن يفعلها، وهل هي في أنفسها حكمة أم هي سفه فمن ذلك إرسال الله تعالى واحدًا قد اصطفاه من خليقته إلى عباده ليبين لهم ما يحتاجون إليه من مصالح الدارين. اختلفوا في جوازه وكونه حكمة فقال أئمة الهدى وقادة الخير وحكماء البشر : أنه حكمة وصواب، وقد أرسل الله تعالى إلى الخلق رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين. وأيدهم بالحجج والدلائل. الذين يقولون لا حاجة للرسل: وقالت طوائف من الناس بامتناعه في نفسه، واختلفوا في علة الامتناع

Among that is the sending by God Almighty of one whom He has chosen from His creation to His servants to explain to them what they need of the interests of the two abodes. This sets the stage for the subsequent refutation.

The Disagreement on Prophethood: Wisdom or Foolishness?
Introduces the topic of prophethood and the general disagreement about sending messengers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial framework for discussing prophethood, including the consensus on God's wisdom and the specific disagreement over sending messengers.

Core Idea
The necessity of prophethood hinges on whether sending messengers is an act of wisdom or foolishness.

This passage sets the stage for the Maturidi argument for prophethood. After establishing that God is wise and all His actions are wise, scholars agree on God's existence but then disagree on specific actions, particularly sending messengers. The imams of guidance affirm that sending messengers is wise. The lesson introduces this foundational disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of God's wisdom
Read the first part where it establishes that God is wise and does not act foolishly. This is the foundation for the entire discussion.
Notice how the author emphasizes God's infallibility in action.
2
Recognize the consensus and the point of disagreement
After the consensus on God's existence, the passage notes that scholars diverged on specific actions. Identify the action in question: sending messengers.
This shows that prophethood is not automatically agreed upon.
3
Identify the two opposing positions
The imams of guidance say it is wisdom and correctness; other groups say it is impossible. Note how the author presents both sides.
The passage does not yet argue for one side, only sets up the debate.
4
Reflect on the significance of this framing
Ask yourself why the author starts with God's wisdom. This is a key premise for the Maturidi proof of prophethood.
The entire later argument will depend on this initial classification.

The book Tabsirat al-Adilla by Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, volume 2. The third section, the first chapter. 1. The discourse on establishing prophethood and apostleship.
Then, when it is established that God most high is wise, not removing any of His actions from wisdom to foolishness, and He is not devoid in anything of what He does and leaves of the description of correctness. So after the consensus of those who affirm the existence of the Maker on this summary, they differed regarding specific actions: whether God most high can do them, and whether they are in themselves wisdom or foolishness. Among that is the sending of God most high of one whom He has chosen from His creation to His servants to clarify for them what they need of the interests of the two abodes. They differed regarding its permissibility and its being wisdom. So the imams of guidance, leaders of good, and wise men of humanity said: it is wisdom and correctness, and God most high has sent to creation messengers as bringers of good tidings and warners, and supported them with proofs and evidences.
Those who say there is no need for messengers:
And groups of people said that it is impossible in itself, and they differed regarding the cause of impossibility.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the discussion on prophethood. It begins by affirming God's wisdom and then notes that despite consensus on God's existence, scholars disagreed on specific actions like sending messengers. The righteous leaders affirm it is wisdom. The passage concludes by mentioning that some groups deny it and differ on the reason for its impossibility.

The Libertine Argument Against Prophethood
Presents the libertine view that prophethood implies foolishness and stinginess in God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the libertine objection to prophethood based on the notions of foolishness and stinginess, and how this view was associated with a group called the khula'a.

Core Idea
Libertines denied prophethood because commands and prohibitions imply foolishness or stinginess in God.

This passage presents a radical objection to prophethood: that sending messengers with commands and prohibitions would imply foolishness or stinginess in God. The author identifies the proponents of this view as the khula'a (libertines), describing their moral character and warning against their hidden influence, especially among those who outwardly practice Sufism but hold permissive beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once to get a sense of the argument and the author's tone. Notice the key terms: سفها (foolishness), بخل (stinginess), خلعاء (libertines).
The text includes some punctuation markers, but the meaning is clear.
2
Understand the libertine argument
The libertines argue that if prophethood were true, it would involve God commanding and prohibiting things that have no benefit or harm to Him, which they consider foolish or stingy. Identify the two charges: foolishness and stinginess.
3
Identify the group: khula'a
The author names the proponents as the khula'a, who have cast off restraint. He describes their behavior and warns that many who outwardly practice Sufism may secretly hold these libertine views.
Note the term 'خلعاء' and its singular 'خليع'.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Though the passage does not provide a direct refutation, it is clear that the author rejects this view. Consider why the argument fails: God's commands are for the benefit of creation, not for God's own benefit.
This connects to the broader Islamic doctrine of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فذهبت طائفة إلى أنه لو ثبت لتضمن سفها، إذ (۱) فيه أمر ونهي وتكليف وتحريم وتحليل والله تعالى لا يليق به الأمر والنهي والتحريم، إذ الأمر بما لا نفع في تحصيله للأمر والنهي عما لا ضرر في فعله للناهي سفه، وتحريم ما لا حاجة فيه للمحرم ،بخل والله تعالى يجل عن السفه والبخل وإلى هذا يذهب طائفة (٢) الناس يسمون الخلعاء الواحد منهم الخليع، وهو الذي خلع عذاره عن ربقة الطاعة وأعرض عما يدعوه (۳) إليه عقله ويزجره عنه وانهمك في شرارته وطغيانه وطاوع هواه ونفسه الأمارة بالسوء في جميع ما يميلان إليه. وعند انكشاف القناع عن مكنون ضمائر كثير من المستتر بالتصوف، وارتفاع الخفاء(4) عن مستور سرائرهم يعلم أنه من هذه الفرقة وهم القائلون بالإباحة طهر الله بلاد الإسلام عنهم وعصم الضعفة من عوام المسلمين عن أنواع مكائدهم ،وغوائلهم ، وصانهم() عن الوقوع في المبثوث من مصائدهم وحبائلهم (٦) .
So a group went to the view that if it [prophethood] were established, it would involve foolishness, since it contains command and prohibition, obligation, making lawful and unlawful, and God most high is not fitting for command, prohibition, and making unlawful, because commanding what has no benefit in its attainment for the commander and prohibiting what has no harm in its doing for the prohibiter is foolishness, and making unlawful what has no need for the one who makes unlawful is stinginess, and God most high is exalted above foolishness and stinginess. And to this goes a group of people called the 'khula'a' (libertines), singular 'khali' (one who has cast off restraint), who has cast off his rein from the yoke of obedience and turned away from what his reason calls him to and restrains him from, and plunged into his evil and tyranny, and obeyed his desire and his soul commanding evil in all that they incline to. And when the cover is lifted from the hidden consciences of many of those who conceal themselves with tasawwuf (Sufism), and the concealment is removed from the concealed secrets of their innermost thoughts, it will be known that he is from this sect, and they are those who say permissibility (ibaha). May God purify the lands of Islam from them and protect the weak from the common Muslims from their types of plots and calamities, and protect them from falling into the spread of their traps and snares.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of a group that denies prophethood on the grounds that it would require God to command and prohibit, which they see as foolish or stingy. It then identifies this group as the 'khula'a' (libertines), describing their moral character and warning against their influence, especially among those who outwardly practice Sufism but secretly hold permissive views.

Key Takeaway
The libertine denial of prophethood rests on the flawed assumption that commands and prohibitions must benefit God, whereas they benefit creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage warns of people who outwardly appear religious but secretly hold permissive beliefs. Have you ever hidden your true doubts or desires behind a façade of piety? If the cover were lifted from your conscience today, what would be exposed that you are ashamed to admit?

The Brahmin Argument: Reason as Sufficient Guide
Presents the Brahmin argument that reason suffices without prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Brahmin claim that human reason alone can provide complete moral guidance, negating the need for prophets.

Core Idea
Reason alone, they say, knows good and evil — no prophets needed.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a key objection to prophethood from the Brahmin school of thought. They argue that since God has endowed human reason with innate knowledge of good and evil, prophets become unnecessary. Understanding this argument is essential because the author will later refute it, affirming the need for revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the Brahmin stance. Notice the structure: a claim followed by reasoning.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the claims: actions are divided into good and evil, reason knows all good and evil, humans are naturally inclined to good and averse to evil.
3
Consider the implication
Reflect on the conclusion: if reason is sufficient for moral knowledge, then prophets are redundant. This is the challenge the author will answer.
This sets up the refutation later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول البراهمة : العقل يغني عن الرسول]:
وذهبت طائفة إلى أن الله تعالى على عباده أوامر ونواهي وأفعالهم منقسمة إلى المحاسن والقبائح، والمحاسن مأمور بها والقبائح مزجور عنها. غير أن الله تعالى أودع في عقول البشر العلم بجميع المحاسن وجبلها على الميل إليها، والمعرفة بجميع القبائح وطبعها على النفور عنها، فكانوا مبتلين مكلفين بعقولهم
The statement of the Brahmins: Reason suffices instead of the messenger:
And a group went to the view that God most high has commands and prohibitions upon His servants, and their actions are divided into good and evil, and good is commanded and evil is prohibited. However, God most high has deposited in the intellects of humans the knowledge of all good and disposed them to incline towards it, and the knowledge of all evil and imprinted them to recoil from it. So they were tested and obligated by their intellects.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage presents the Brahmin argument that divine messengers are unnecessary because God has endowed human reason with innate knowledge of good and evil. Humans are naturally inclined to good and averse to evil, so they are accountable solely through their intellects. This view denies the need for prophetic revelation.

Key Terms
براهمة
عقل
محاسن
قبائح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage endorses the Brahmin view as correct.
Correction: This is a reported objection. The author presents it to refute it later.

Checkpoint

A) Because messengers are impossible
B) Because God has placed knowledge of good and evil in reason
C) Because miracles prove nothing
D) Because messengers cause division

Key Takeaway
The Brahmin argument asserts that human reason, endowed by God with full moral knowledge, eliminates the need for prophetic messengers.

The Brahmins' Dilemma Against Prophethood
Imam Nasafi presents the Brahmins' argument that prophethood is unnecessary or contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Brahmins' rationalist objection to prophethood and the dilemma they pose between contradiction and redundancy.

Core Idea
The Brahmins argued that prophethood is either contradictory to reason or redundant to it.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents the argument of the Brahmins, a Hindu philosophical group, who deny the necessity of prophethood by claiming that reason is sufficient for guidance. Their dilemma challenges the very purpose of divine revelation. Let us examine their reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Try to grasp the overall argument before analyzing its parts.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Look for the conditional statement: if revelation contradicts reason, it is false; if it agrees, reason suffices. This is the core dilemma.
The Brahmins assume that reason and revelation are either aligned or opposed, with no middle ground.
3
Examine the additional arguments
Note the two supplementary points: sending prophets to known deniers is futile and foolish, and preferring one person over another of the same kind is unjust.
These strengthen the case against prophethood from a rational perspective.
4
Reflect on the group's identity
The passage ends by identifying the Brahmins as a group claiming wisdom in India. Consider why this matters for the theological debate.
Understanding the source of the objection helps contextualize the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقررون هذا فيقولون بأن الرسالة إما وردت موافقة لما في العقل (۱) ، أو مخالفة له. فإن كانت مخالفة فهي باطلة لأن العقل حجة الله وحججه لا تتناقض. وإن كانت موافقة للعقل فبالعقل عنها غنية فتكون خالية عن الجدوى فيكون عبثًا مع أن إرسال الرسل إلى من يعلم الرسل أنه لا يجيبه إلى ما يدعوه ويقابل رسوله بالتكذيب ويمد يده إليه بالضرب والتعذيب خارج عن الحكمة داخل في حد السفه. ولأنه لو أرسل لا(۳) فقد فضله على غيره من جنسه، وتفضيل الشيء على ما يجانسه ويساويه في الخلقة ليس بحكمة. وهذه الطائفة هم المعروفون بالبراهمة (٤) وهم قوم ينتسبون إلى الحكمة في الهند ().
And perhaps they establish this by saying that the message (al-risāla) either came in agreement with what is in the intellect (al-‘aql), or in opposition to it. If it is in opposition, it is false because the intellect is God's proof, and His proofs do not contradict. If it is in agreement with the intellect, then the intellect suffices for it, so it would be devoid of benefit and thus frivolous, especially since sending messengers to one whom the messenger knows will not respond to his call, will meet his messenger with denial, and will stretch his hand to strike and torture him, is outside of wisdom and within the bounds of foolishness. And because if he sent [؟] he would have preferred him over others of his kind, and preferring a thing over what is similar and equal in creation is not wisdom. And this group are those known as the Brahmins (al-Barāhima), and they are a people who attribute themselves to wisdom in India.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the Brahmins against the necessity of prophethood. It is a statement of their position, not yet refuted. The Brahmins argue that since human reason is sufficient for guidance, prophethood is either useless or contradictory. They present a dilemma: if revelation contradicts reason, it is false; if it agrees with reason, reason is sufficient and revelation is superfluous. They further argue that sending prophets to people who will reject and harm them is futile and foolish, and that preferring one person over another of the same species without cause is unjust. The author then identifies this group as the Brahmins of India.

Key Terms
الرسالة (al-risāla)
العقل (al-‘aql)
البراهمة (al-Barāhima)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Brahmins are named after Prophet Ibrahim (Abraham).
Correction: The Brahmins (al-Barāhima) are a Hindu philosophical group from India, not followers of Prophet Ibrahim.

Checkpoint
What is the central dilemma the Brahmins use to argue against prophethood?
A) Prophethood contradicts reason.
B) Prophethood is either contradictory to reason or redundant to it.
C) Prophethood is only for the ignorant.
D) Prophethood is impossible because prophets are liars.

Key Takeaway
The Brahmins' objection forces us to defend the necessity and consistency of revelation with reason, showing that prophethood is neither contradictory nor redundant.

The Naturalists' Objection to Miracles as Proof
Imam Nasafi presents and refutes the Naturalists' objection to prophethood based on the impossibility of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Naturalists' argument that miracles cannot serve as definitive evidence for prophethood because they are either imitable by false claimants or impossible due to the fixity of natural laws.

Core Idea
Miracles prove nothing: they are either fakeable or impossible.

This lesson presents the challenge posed by the Naturalists (al-Ṭabī‘iyyūn) to the traditional Islamic proof of prophethood through miracles. They concede prophethood is not impossible in itself but argue that verifying a prophet’s claim is impossible: any alleged miracle is either within the realm of ordinary nature (hence imitable) or violates nature (hence impossible). This objection forces us to reflect on the nature of miracles and the basis for distinguishing true prophets from pretenders.

Guided Path
1
Read the Naturalists' initial concession
Notice that they begin by conceding there is no intrinsic impossibility in prophethood; the impossibility comes from elsewhere.
This is important because they are not denying God's power outright.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities for the evidence (miracle): it either follows continuous custom or violates it. Find these two branches and their consequences.
This is the core of their argument.
3
Note the rejection of specific miracles
The Naturalists deny well-known miracles accepted by Muslims (e.g., Moses' staff, Jesus' healings, Muhammad's water flowing). See why they reject them based on their dilemma.
4
Consider the futility conclusion
They conclude that without decisive evidence, sending a prophet would be pointless and foolish. This is a theological objection to the wisdom of sending prophets.
5
Examine the sorcerer objection
Some Naturalists add that even if God can perform miracles, sorcerers can too, making it impossible to distinguish. This closes the last loophole.
This objection anticipates the traditional Muslim response that miracles are inimitable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت طوائف من الناس إلى أن لا امتناع في ذاته ولا استحالة في نفسه لما فيه من تأييد العقول فيما تحتاج إلى معرفته وتسهيل لطريق وقوفها على ما بها إلى الوقوف عليه حاجة. غير أن الامتناع جاء من ناحية أخرى، واختلف هؤلاء فيما بينهم، فمنهم من زعم أن الامتناع ثبت لما أن لا فائدة فيه إلا بعد ثبوته، ولا طريق للوصول إليه والوقوف على ثبوته، لما أن دعوى المتنبئ تعارض دعوى النبي. ولا يمكن الفصل بينهما إلا بالدليل ولا دليل على ذلك، لأن ما اقترن به من الدليل إما أن كان خارجًا على العادة المستمرة وعلى ما عليه أمور الطبائع، وإما أن كان خارجًا عن ذلك؛ فإن كان خارجا على العادة الجارية والطبيعة الثابتة فلا يعجز المتنبئ عن الإتيان بمثله. وإن كان خارجا عن ذلك فهو محال إذ تغيير الطبائع وخروجها عما هي عليه أمر ممتنع والقول به محال وإلى هذا يذهب الطبيعيون وينكرون جميع ما ثبت بالتواتر من الأمور الخارجة عن المعتاد والأمر الطبيعي من نحو قلب العصا حية، وانفجار الماء من الصخر، وفلق البحر، وإبراء الأكمة والأبرص وإحياء الموتى، وكلام الناقة، والشاه المسمومة ونبع الماء من بين الأصابع وأشباه ذلك. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فلو أرسل الله تعالى رسولاً، وليس في مقدوره إقامة الدليل على صدقه فلا فائدة فيه بدون ثبوت رسالته، ولا وجه إلى إثباته إذ لا إلى إقامة الدليل عليه، لكان ذلك الإرسال خاليًا عن العاقبة الحميدة متعريًا عن النفع والجدوى، وهو سفه والله تعالى يجل عن الوصف به. وزعم بعض هؤلاء أن خروج الأمر عن المعتاد وقلب العادة والطبيعة في مقدور الله تعالى، إلا أن ذلك أيضا مقدور السحرة والمشعوذة فيقع بذلك تعارض بحيث لا ترجيح لأحد الأمرين على الآخر (۳) ، وقد مر أن إرسال الرسول والحالة هذه خال عن العاقبة الحميدة متعريًا عن الجدوى.
And groups of people held that there is no impossibility in itself nor impossibility in its essence because of what it contains of supporting the intellects in what they need to know and facilitating the path of their arriving at what they need to arrive at. However, the impossibility came from another direction, and these differed among themselves. Some of them claimed that impossibility is established because there is no benefit in it except after its establishment, and there is no way to reach it and ascertain its establishment, because the claim of the claimant (al-mutanabbi') contradicts the claim of the prophet. And it is not possible to distinguish between them except by evidence, and there is no evidence for that, because the evidence accompanying it is either outside the continuous custom and what the matters of natures (al-ṭabā’i‘) are upon, or it is outside that. If it is outside the usual custom and the fixed nature, then the claimant is not incapable of producing something similar. And if it is outside that, then it is impossible, because changing natures and their departure from what they are is an impossible matter, and belief in it is impossible. And this is the view of the Naturalists (al-Ṭabī‘iyyūn), and they deny all that is established by tawātur of matters outside the usual and the natural order, such as the turning of a staff into a snake, the gushing of water from a rock, the splitting of the sea, the healing of the blind and leprous, the reviving of the dead, the speaking of the she-camel, the poisoned sheep, the flowing of water from between the fingers, and similar things. And if the matter is so, then if God sent a messenger, and he is not able to establish evidence of his truthfulness, there is no benefit in it without the establishment of his message, and there is no way to prove it since there is no way to establish evidence for it. Then that sending would be devoid of praiseworthy outcome, stripped of benefit and utility, and that is foolishness, and God is exalted above being described with that. And some of these claimed that the departure from the usual and the overturning of custom and nature is within God's power, but that is also within the power of sorcerers and magicians, so there occurs a conflict such that no preference can be given to one of the two matters over the other. And it has passed that sending a messenger in this state is devoid of praiseworthy outcome, stripped of utility.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of the Naturalists against the possibility of proving prophethood through miracles. It begins by acknowledging that there is no intrinsic impossibility in prophethood, but then states that the impossibility arises from another angle. The Naturalists argue that there is no way to verify a prophet's claim because any miracle can be imitated by false claimants, and if the miracle violates nature, it is impossible because natures cannot change. They reject all well-known miracles like those of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad. They conclude that without a way to establish truthfulness, sending a prophet would be futile. Some Naturalists concede that God can perform miracles, but claim that sorcerers can do the same, leading to a conflict with no way to distinguish. The author presents these views as objections to be refuted.

Key Terms
الطبيعيون (al-Ṭabī‘iyyūn)
المتنبئ (al-mutanabbi')
العادة (al-‘āda)
الطبائع (al-ṭabā’i‘)
التواتر (al-tawātur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Naturalists deny God's power to perform miracles altogether.
Correction: The Naturalists actually accept God's power but argue that miracles are either imitable or impossible due to the fixity of natures, and some admit God can do them but claim sorcerers can too, leaving no basis for distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the Naturalists, why can a miracle not serve as evidence for a prophet's truthfulness?
A) Miracles are impossible for God to perform.
B) Miracles can be imitated by sorcerers, so there is no way to distinguish.
C) Prophets never perform real miracles.
D) The intellect alone can prove prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The Naturalists present a dilemma: if a miracle conforms to nature, it is imitable; if it violates nature, it is impossible — therefore, miracles cannot verify a prophet's claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a divine command that contradicts your 'natural' inclination, do you twist it to fit your custom or submit? Where in your life have you acted like a Naturalist, demanding evidence on your terms before obeying?
?
The Naturalists argued that if a miracle is too extraordinary, it is impossible. Are there aspects of your faith (e.g., resurrection, unseen reward) that you find hard to accept because they seem 'against nature'? How does this lesson challenge you to trust God's power over your rational limits?
?
The objection states that without clear evidence, sending a messenger would be futile. Have you ever dismissed a prophetic teaching because you found it 'impractical' or 'benefitless'? What does that reveal about your heart's posture toward revelation?

Refutation of the Naturalist Denial of Miracles
Al-Nasafi refutes naturalists who deny miracles and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's argument refuting the naturalist position that miracles cannot prove prophethood, and how he shows that this position implies foolishness in God.

Core Idea
Denying miracles leads to the impossible conclusion that God acts foolishly.

Al-Nasafi presents the naturalist argument that miracles cannot serve as proof for prophethood because they are either natural and imitable or supernatural and impossible. He then refutes this by showing the absurd consequence: if a prophet cannot provide proof, God's sending would be futile, which is impossible for God. This lesson unpacks that logical rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the naturalist position and al-Nasafi's response. Pay attention to the structure of the argument.
The naturalists are those who deny miracles as supernatural events.
2
Identify the naturalist dilemma
Notice how the naturalists split miracles into two categories: those within custom (imitable by false prophets) and those outside custom (impossible because nature is fixed).
3
Trace al-Nasafi's refutation
Follow how al-Nasafi uses the consequence of futility (safah) to refute the naturalists. If God sent a prophet without proof, that sending would be devoid of benefit, which is foolishness—impossible for God.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the importance
Understand why this argument is crucial: it defends the necessity of miracles as signs of prophethood and upholds God's wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوقوف عليه حاجة. غير أن الامتناع جاء من ناحية أخرى، واختلف هؤلاء فيما بينهم، فمنهم من زعم أن الامتناع ثبت لما أن لا فائدة فيه إلا بعد ثبوته، ولا طريق للوصول إليه والوقوف على ثبوته، لما أن دعوى المتنبئ تعارض دعوى النبي. ولا يمكن الفصل بينهما إلا بالدليل ولا دليل على ذلك، لأن ما اقترن به من الدليل إما أن كان خارجًا على العادة المستمرة وعلى ما عليه أمور الطبائع، وإما أن كان خارجًا عن ذلك ؛ فإن كان خارجا على العادة الجارية والطبيعة الثابتة فلا يعجز المتنبئ عن الإتيان بمثله. وإن كان خارجا عن ذلك فهو محال إذ تغيير الطبائع وخروجها عما هي عليه أمر ممتنع والقول به محال وإلى هذا يذهب الطبيعيون وينكرون جميع ما ثبت بالتواتر من الأمور الخارجة عن المعتاد والأمر الطبيعي من نحو قلب العصا حية، وانفجار الماء من الصخر، وفلق البحر، وإبراء الأكمة والأبرص وإحياء الموتى، وكلام الناقة، والشاه المسمومة ونبع الماء من بين الأصابع وأشباه ذلك. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فلو أرسل الله تعالى رسولاً، وليس في مقدوره إقامة الدليل على صدقه فلا فائدة فيه بدون ثبوت رسالته، ولا وجه إلى إثباته إذ لا إلى إقامة الدليل عليه، لكان ذلك الإرسال خاليًا عن العاقبة الحميدة متعريًا عن النفع والجدوى، وهو سفه والله تعالى يجل عن الوصف به.
There is a need to stop upon it. However, impossibility came from another direction, and these differed among themselves: among them is one who claimed that impossibility is established because there is no benefit in it except after its establishment, and there is no way to reach it and stop upon its establishment, because the claim of a false prophet contradicts the claim of a true prophet. And it is not possible to distinguish between them except by proof, and there is no proof for that, because the proof that accompanies it is either outside the continuous custom and what is upon the matters of natures, or it is outside that; if it is outside the current custom and fixed nature, then the false prophet is not incapable of bringing its like. And if it is outside that, then it is impossible, because changing natures and their departure from what they are is a forbidden matter and saying it is impossible, and to this go the naturalists, and they deny all that is established by tawatur of matters outside the usual and the natural order, such as the turning of a staff into a snake, the splitting of water from rock, the parting of the sea, the healing of the blind and leper, the reviving of the dead, the speech of the she-camel, the poisoned sheep, the gushing of water from between fingers, and the like of that. And if the matter is such, then if God Almighty sent a messenger, and it is not within his power to establish proof of his truthfulness, then there is no benefit in it without the establishment of his message, and no way to establish it since there is no way to establish proof upon it, then that sending would be devoid of a praiseworthy outcome, stripped of benefit and efficacy, and it is foolishness, and God Almighty is exalted above being described with it.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the naturalists who deny the possibility of miracles and prophethood. Al-Nasafi begins by stating that the impossibility (al-imtina') comes from another direction; he presents the naturalist argument: they claim that miracles cannot prove prophecy because either the miracle is natural—thus a false prophet can match it—or it is supernatural—thus impossible because nature cannot change. They point to examples like Moses' staff turning into a snake as supposedly impossible. Al-Nasafi then refutes by showing the consequence: if God sent a prophet without the ability to provide proof, that sending would be futile (safah), which is impossible for God. Thus the naturalist position leads to attributing foolishness to God, which is absurd.

Key Terms
