طبيعة
محال
سفه
متنبئ
دعوى
دليل
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles cannot prove prophethood because they are either natural and imitable or supernatural and impossible.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that denying supernatural miracles leads to the absurdity that God sends prophets without proof, which is foolishness—impossible for God. Thus, miracles must be possible and serve as proof.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's refutation, what would be the consequence if a prophet could not provide proof of his truthfulness?
A) The sending would still be beneficial
B) It would be foolishness (safah) unworthy of God
C) It would be a test of faith
D) It would be impossible for God to send such a prophet

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the naturalist denial of miracles by showing that without the possibility of supernatural proof, God's sending of prophets would be futile, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a reported miracle because it seemed 'unnatural'? How does al-Nasafi's argument challenge that assumption and what does it demand of your attitude toward divine power?
?
If you truly believed that God's actions are never futile, how would that change your response to moments when His wisdom seems hidden from you?
?
Al-Nasafi says that denying the possibility of miracles implies God acts foolishly. Do you live as if you believe God can intervene supernaturally in your life? If not, what does that say about your understanding of His power?

The Objection of Sorcerers Matching Miracles
Al-Nasafi presents the denialist claim that sorcerers can match miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the denialist argument from sorcery challenges the evidential value of miracles and why it is considered refuted.

Core Idea
If sorcerers can also do miracles, then miracles cannot prove prophethood — but earlier arguments show this leads to absurdity.

Al-Nasafi continues presenting objections from those who deny the possibility of proving prophethood through miracles. Here, he reports their claim that since sorcerers and magicians can also produce extraordinary events, no prophet can be distinguished from a sorcerer. This passage sets up the refutation that follows in the original text, but it is important to grasp the structure of the denialist argument itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the sequence: admission of God's power, then claim about sorcerers, then the conclusion of conflict and futility.
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic, so pay attention to the connectors.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
Break the objection into three components: (1) God can perform miracles, (2) sorcerers can also perform them, (3) this creates a conflict with no way to prefer one over the other, making prophethood futile.
The deniers concede God's power but try to equalise it with sorcery.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Al-Nasafi says this objection 'has already passed', meaning it has been refuted earlier. Consider what makes a true miracle different from sorcery: miracles are beyond all natural means, while sorcery is merely illusion or limited power.
This lesson only presents the objection; the refutation is assumed from prior discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم بعض هؤلاء أن خروج الأمر عن المعتاد وقلب العادة والطبيعة في مقدور الله تعالى، إلا أن ذلك أيضا مقدور السحرة والمشعوذة فيقع بذلك تعارض بحيث لا ترجيح لأحد الأمرين على الآخر، وقد مر أن إرسال الرسول والحالة هذه خال عن العاقبة الحميدة متعريًا عن الجدوى.
And some of these claimed that the occurrence of what is beyond the usual and the reversal of custom and nature are within God's power, except that that is also within the power of sorcerers and magicians, so conflict occurs such that there is no preference for one of the two matters over the other, and it has already passed that sending a messenger in this state is devoid of a praiseworthy outcome and stripped of benefit and efficacy.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from the deniers: they concede that God can perform miracles, but argue that sorcerers and magicians can also perform similar marvels. This creates a conflict (ta'āruḍ) where we cannot prefer the prophet over the sorcerer. The deniers then refer to the earlier argument that without a distinguishing proof, sending a messenger would be futile. Al-Nasafi here is not refuting anew but reporting that this objection has already been addressed earlier.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if sorcerers can perform miracles, then miracles cannot prove prophethood because there is no way to distinguish them.
Correction: The earlier refutation shows that sorcery cannot match a true miracle because sorcerers work through means and illusions, while prophets bring clear signs that cannot be replicated. The deniers' argument leads to absurd consequences and has already been addressed.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the denialist argument in this passage?
A) God cannot perform miracles
B) Sorcerers can also perform miracles, so no preference can be given to a prophet
C) Prophets are actually sorcerers
D) Miracles are impossible

Key Takeaway
The denialist attempt to equate sorcery with miracles fails because true miracles are verifiably beyond all created power, whereas sorcery operates within natural limits and illusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear sign from Allah because you saw similar-looking wonders from people who do not follow truth? How does this lesson expose the error of judging by appearances alone? What will you do to ensure you do not fall into this trap again?
?
When you encounter two conflicting claims—one from a source of truth and the other from a clever imitation—what criteria do you use to decide? Are your criteria rooted in what Allah has taught, or in your own flawed reasoning? How can you train yourself to seek the deeper evidence beyond surface-level similarities?
?
The denialists argued that without a distinguishing sign, sending a messenger is futile. In your own life, are you treating the miracles of the Quran and the Prophet ﷺ as truly decisive, or do you allow doubts from worldly comparisons to weaken your certainty? What step will you take today to strengthen your conviction in the proofs of prophethood?

The Objection That Miracles Can Be Acquired Through Practice
Al-Nasafi presents the denialist argument that prophetic signs are reproducible by training.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection to the evidential value of miracles: the claim that they can be replicated by human effort, and why this objection fails to undermine prophetic certainty.

Core Idea
A reproducible feat cannot yield certain knowledge of prophethood.

Al-Nasafi now turns to a third objection: what if the miraculous sign is actually attainable through human effort and practice? Deniers argue that if the prophet's sign could be replicated, then we cannot be certain of his truthfulness. This lesson presents that argument and prepares us to see why it is ultimately unsound.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: a claim that the proof can be acquired, then the reasoning why this prevents certainty.
2
Identify the underlying assumption
What do the deniers assume about the relationship between reproducibility and certainty? Note that they equate possibility of imitation with absence of certainty.
This point is crucial for the refutation later.
3
Consider the scope of testing
The deniers argue that the prophet may not have tested all humans. Reflect on whether this is a fair condition for establishing a miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم بعضهم أن نوع ما أتى به النبي عن الدليل مما يمكن تحصيله لمن له في نوعه تكلف واجتهاد وفي بابه تدرب واعتياد وهؤلاء الذين ادعى مدعى الرسالة رسالته فيهم لعلهم لم يكن لهم في ذلك اعتياد، ولم يمتحن مدعي الرسالة قوى جميع البشر، ولعله لو امتحن لوجد من يقدر على مثله ويعارضه في صنعه، ومع تمكن هذا النوع من الإمكان لم يحصل العلم بصدقه ولا المعرفة بصحة رسالته، إذ لا علم مع احتمال البطلان
And some of them claimed that the type of proof brought by a prophet can be acquired by anyone who exerts effort and diligence in its kind and is trained and accustomed in its field, and these people among whom the claimant of prophethood claimed his prophethood – perhaps they had no practice in that, and the claimant of prophethood did not test the powers of all humanity, and perhaps if he tested he would find someone capable of its like and would oppose him in his act, and with the possibility of this type, certainty of his truthfulness is not obtained nor knowledge of the validity of his prophethood, since there is no knowledge with the possibility of falsehood.
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection: deniers claim that the type of proof brought by a prophet (e.g., eloquent speech or certain miracles) can be achieved by anyone through effort and practice. They argue that the prophet may not have tested all humans; perhaps there is someone who can produce the same. Since it is possible that the feat could be imitated, no certain knowledge of the prophet's truth is attained. Thus, miracles of this kind do not provide certainty (ʻilm).

A) Because prophets are not truthful
B) Because the proof could be achieved by others through practice, so there is possibility of falsehood
C) Because God does not provide proof
D) Because miracles are not real

Key Takeaway
The deniers argue that the possibility of imitation precludes certainty, but this assumes that the miraculous sign is merely a learned skill, ignoring its divine character.

The Objection That Prophetic Laws Are Foolish Despite Possible Miracles
Denialists argue that prophetic laws are foolish, so miracles do not prove them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection against prophethood: that the specific laws brought by prophets appear foolish, so miracles cannot prove their truth.

Core Idea
If a claim is impossible, no miracle can prove it.

The denialists grant that miracles are possible, but they argue that the actual content of prophetic teachings is foolish and impossible. Therefore, miracles supporting such claims are merely specious arguments (shubah), not proofs. This passage presents their reasoning and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This is a dense argument; read slowly.
2
Identify the denialists' main argument
The denialists claim that although miracles are possible, they cannot prove an impossible claim. Look for the phrase 'لا حجة تقوم على تصحيح المحال' (no proof can establish the validity of the impossible).
3
Note the analogy with the muta'allih
The denialists compare the prophets' case to someone who claims divinity (muta'allih) and performs miracles. In both cases, the claim is impossible, so the miracle is only a shubha (specious argument).
This reinforces their point.
4
Examine the examples of foolish laws
The denialists list several Islamic practices they consider absurd, such as washing parts of the body for wudu while not washing the impure spot, throwing stones to no one in Hajj, etc.
These are meant to show the laws are 'outside wisdom'.
5
Consider how Al-Nasafi frames this objection
Notice that Al-Nasafi presents this as a known objection from groups that affirm the Creator but deny prophethood. He does not refute it here, only explains it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المنكرين للرسالة من زعم أن لا خلل في جانب الإمكان بل هي ممكنة جائزة في الحكمة إلا أن جميع ما ادعاها وأتى بالدلالة عليها كان دعواه ممتنعا محالاً، ونقض العادة مما لا يدل على صحة المحال، فكان نقض العادة وقلب الطبيعة جاريًا مجرى الشبه دون الحجج إذ لا حجة تقوم على تصحيح المحال، ولهذا جوزتم ظهور الناقض للمادة على يدي المتأله مع امتناع دعواه وزعمتم أن ذلك من باب الشبه دون الحجج لما أنه لا تصحيح للمحال بالحجة فكذا هذا، قالوا: ودليل كون دعواهم ممتنعا أنهم أتوا بأشياء خارجة عن الحكمة داخلة في حيز السفه من نحو إيجاب غسل الوجه واليدين والرجلين والمسح على الرأس عند خروج الحدث من محل مخصوص، مع الإعفاء عن غسل محل خروج النجاسة، ورمى الحجر لا إلى أحد في الحج، والعدو بين الأماكن، وهز الكتفين وتحريك الأعضاء من غير حضور أحد يرآيه الفاعل، واستلام الحجر أصم لا ينفع ولا يضر وغير ذلك من المطاعن لهم، أعرضنا عن ذكرها لشهرتها. هذا هو بيان لفرق المنكرين للرسالة مع إقرارهم بثبوت الصانع.
And among the deniers of prophethood is one who claimed that there is no flaw in the aspect of possibility, rather it is possible and permissible in wisdom, but all that they claimed and brought as evidence for it was that their claim is impossible and absurd, and breaking custom does not indicate the validity of the impossible, so breaking custom and reversing nature runs in the course of specious arguments rather than proofs, since no proof can establish the validity of the impossible, and for this you permitted the occurrence of the break in matter at the hands of the muta'allih with the impossibility of his claim, and you claimed that that is from the category of shubah not proofs, because there is no correction of the impossible by proof, and similarly this. They said: The evidence that their claim is impossible is that they brought things outside of wisdom, entering into the domain of foolishness, such as the obligation of washing the face, hands, feet, and wiping the head when an event occurs from a specific place, while exempting the washing of the place of impurity exit, and throwing stones to no one in Hajj, and running between places, and shaking the shoulders and moving limbs without anyone present seeing the actor, and kissing a deaf stone that neither benefits nor harms, and other objections against them, which we have refrained from mentioning due to their fame. This is an exposition of the groups of deniers of prophethood while they affirm the existence of the Creator.
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection: some deniers accept that miracles are possible in principle, but they argue that the actual claims of prophets (e.g., specific laws of Islam) are themselves impossible and foolish. Therefore, miracles cannot prove the validity of an impossible claim. They analogize this to the case of a 'muta'allih' (a person who claims divinity) performing miracles; the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools say such miracles are shubah not proofs because the claim is impossible. Similarly, the prophets' laws are foolish, so their miracles are also shubah. They give examples: washing face, hands, feet, wiping head when breaking wudu, while not washing the place of impurity; throwing stones to no one in Hajj; running between Safa and Marwa; shaking shoulders and moving limbs in prayer without anyone watching; kissing the Black Stone which is deaf and cannot benefit or harm. Al-Nasafi says these are well-known objections. He concludes that this is an exposition of the groups of deniers who affirm the existence of the Creator but deny prophethood.

Key Terms
إمكان
حكمة
محال
نقض العادة
شبه
حجة
متأله
سفه
مطاعن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a miracle occurs, it proves the truth of whatever claim it accompanies.
Correction: The denialists argue that miracles cannot prove an impossible claim; if the claim itself is foolish or impossible, the miracle is only a specious argument.

Checkpoint
According to the denialists in this passage, what do they claim about the laws brought by prophets?
A) They are wise and beneficial
B) They are foolish and outside of wisdom
C) They are impossible to perform
D) They are confirmed by reason

Key Takeaway
The denialists argue that if a prophetic law is foolish (impossible), its miracle is only a specious argument (shubha), not a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a divine command that seems irrational or inconvenient to you—like those listed by the denialists—do you immediately judge it as 'foolish' and let your heart feel distance from Allah, or do you pause and admit that His wisdom may be beyond your comprehension? Where exactly do you draw the line between trusting Allah's wisdom and demanding that His laws make sense to you personally?
?
The denialists claimed that miracles cannot prove an impossible claim. Have you ever rejected a truth of Islam not because it was genuinely impossible (like a contradiction), but because it felt foolish or burdensome to your lifestyle? Think of one specific command you find hard to accept completely. Before today, did you treat that command as 'impossible' in your heart, and if so, what would it cost you to surrender that judgment right now?
?
If you saw a miracle today—a clear break of natural law—would you automatically submit to the message it accompanies, or would you first examine the message's content to see if it matches what you already consider 'wise'? What does your answer reveal about where your true trust lies: in miracles or in your own sense of wisdom?

The Imitation Objection Against Miracles
Al-Nasafi reports the objection that miracles could be counterfeited by experts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the proof of prophethood through miracles, namely that miracles could be imitated by trained individuals, thus preventing certainty.

Core Idea
Miracles could be imitated, so they don't yield certain knowledge.

Al-Nasafi begins by presenting the first objection raised by those who deny the validity of miracles as proof of prophethood. They argue that the extraordinary acts brought by a prophet are not unique; anyone with sufficient training and practice could potentially replicate them. This objection challenges the certainty of the prophet's truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the claim, the reason, and the epistemological conclusion.
2
Identify the core claim
The objection states that miracles are attainable through effort and practice. Why does this matter for proving prophethood?
3
Understand the consequence
Because imitation is possible, we cannot be certain of the prophet's truthfulness. Recognize that this is the key point of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم بعضهم أن نوع ما أتى به النبي عن الدليل مما يمكن تحصيله لمن له في نوعه تكلف واجتهاد وفي بابه تدرب واعتياد وهؤلاء الذين ادعى مدعى الرسالة رسالته فيهم لعلهم لم يكن لهم في ذلك اعتياد، ولم يمتحن مدعي الرسالة قوى جميع البشر، ولعله لو امتحن لوجد من يقدر على مثله ويعارضه في صنعه، ومع تمكن هذا النوع من الإمكان لم يحصل العلم بصدقه ولا المعرفة بصحة رسالته، إذ لا علم مع احتمال البطلان
And some of them claimed that the type of thing brought by the prophet by way of evidence can be attained by one who has effort and exertion in its type, and practice and habituation in its field. And those to whom the claimant of prophethood claimed his prophethood, perhaps they had no habituation in that, and the claimant of prophethood did not test the powers of all humans, and perhaps if he had tested, he would have found one who is able to produce its like and oppose him in his deed. And with the possibility of this type being possible, knowledge of his truthfulness is not obtained, nor knowledge of the soundness of his message, for there is no knowledge with the possibility of falsehood.
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This passage reports an objection raised by some deniers of prophethood. The objection argues that the miracles brought by the prophet are of a type that could potentially be produced by anyone who trains and practices in that field. Since the prophet did not test all humans, it is possible that someone could produce an equivalent, thus casting doubt on the certainty of his truthfulness. The objection is epistemological: miracles do not yield certain knowledge because there remains the possibility of counterfeiting.

Key Terms
نقض العادة
إمكان
علم
صدق
رسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles can be imitated by experts who train in the same field, so they do not prove prophethood.
Correction: Miracles are defined as actions that break natural laws and are beyond human capacity; thus, no amount of training can replicate them.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the first group of deniers against the proof of prophethood through miracles?
A) Miracles are impossible
B) Miracles are possible but could be imitated by trained individuals
C) Prophethood is impossible
D) The prophet did not perform miracles

Key Takeaway
The first objection against miracles as proof of prophethood argues that miracles could be imitated, removing certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the objection that miracles could be imitated, does a seed of doubt enter your heart about the prophethood of Muhammad? If so, what will you do to uproot that doubt and replace it with certainty?
?
The objectors claimed that because the prophet did not test every human, there remains a possibility of imitation. Have you ever felt that your faith is based on probability rather than certainty? What would it take for you to say 'I know with certainty'?
?
The passage states: 'there is no knowledge with the possibility of falsehood.' Is there any possibility of falsehood in your belief that the Quran is the word of God? If you find even a trace of doubt, what concrete step will you take today to eliminate it?

Miracles Cannot Prove Impossible Claims
Al-Nasafi reports the objection that prophethood is possible but the actual claims are impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that miracles cannot validate inherently impossible claims, and how this challenges the evidentiary value of prophetic miracles.

Core Idea
A miracle proves a possibility, not an absurdity.

Al-Nasafi now presents a second, more subtle objection. The deniers accept that miracles are possible in principle, but they argue that the specific claims of prophets are inherently impossible. Since no miracle can prove an impossibility, the miracles become ambiguous signs rather than solid proofs. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's core claim
The deniers grant that prophethood is possible in wisdom, but they assert that the actual claims of the prophets are impossible. Read the first few lines to grasp this distinction.
Notice they do not deny the concept of miracle; they deny its applicability.
2
Understand the analogy to their own view
They point out that if a pious person claims something impossible but performs a miracle, you (the theologians) consider that miracle a mere ambiguity. So by the same logic, the prophets' miracles are ambiguous if their claims are impossible.
3
Examine the examples of 'foolishness'
The deniers list several Islamic rulings they consider irrational: details of wudu, Hajj rituals, etc. These are meant to show that the prophetic teachings are outside wisdom.
The author omits the full list due to their fame, but the point is clear.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by identifying these as groups who deny prophethood while still affirming the Creator. Consider how this objection is more refined than a flat rejection of miracles.
This objection is a challenge to the rationality of prophetic claims, not to the possibility of miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المنكرين للرسالة من زعم أن لا خلل في جانب الإمكان بل هي ممكنة جائزة في الحكمة إلا أن جميع ما ادعاها وأتى بالدلالة عليها كان دعواه ممتنعا محالا، ونقض العادة مما لا يدل على صحة المحال، فكان نقض العادة وقلب الطبيعة جاريا مجرى الشبه دون الحجج إذ لا حجة تقوم على تصحيح المحال، ولهذا جوزتم ظهور الناقض للمادة على يدي المتأله مع امتناع دعواه وزعمتم أن ذلك من باب الشبه دون الحجج لما أنه لا تصحيح للمحال بالحجة فكذا هذا، قالوا: ودليل كون دعواهم ممتنعا أنهم أتوا بأشياء خارجة عن الحكمة داخلة في حيز السفه من نحو إيجاب غسل الوجه واليدين والرجلين والمسح على الرأس عند خروج الحدث من محل مخصوص، مع الإعفاء عن غسل محل خروج الحجر لا إلى أحد في الحج، والعدو بين الأماكن، وهز الكتفين وتحريك الأعضاء من غير حضور أحد يرآيه الفاعل، واستلام الحجر أصم لا ينفع ولا يضر وغير ذلك من المطاعن لهم، أعرضنا عن ذكرها لشهرتها. هذا هو بيان لفرق المنكرين للرسالة مع إقرارهم بثبوت الصانع.
And among the deniers of prophethood, some claimed that there is no defect in the aspect of possibility, rather it is possible and permissible in wisdom, except that all that they claimed and brought evidence for was impossible and absurd. And breaking the custom does not indicate the validity of the absurd, so breaking the custom and altering nature are like ambiguities rather than proofs, for no proof establishes the validity of the absurd. And for this reason you permitted the occurrence of the miraculous upon the hands of a pious person while his claim is impossible, and you claimed that this is of the type of ambiguities not proofs, because there is no validation of the absurd by proof, and so too this. They said: The proof that their claim is impossible is that they brought things outside of wisdom, entering into the realm of foolishness, such as obligating washing the face, hands, feet, and wiping the head upon the occurrence of an event from a specific place, while exempting from washing the place of exit of the stone not to anyone in Hajj, and the running between places, and shaking the shoulders and moving limbs without the presence of anyone seeing the doer, and kissing the black stone which is deaf, neither benefits nor harms, and other such objections against them, which we have omitted due to their fame. This is an exposition of the groups of deniers of prophethood while acknowledging the existence of the Creator.

Commentary
This passage presents a second, more sophisticated objection. The deniers accept that miracles (nagd al-‘ada) are possible in principle, but they argue that the specific claims of the prophets are inherently impossible (mumtani‘ muhal). Since a miracle cannot prove an impossible claim, miracles in support of such claims are mere ambiguous signs (shubah), not proofs (hujaj). They support their case by listing various Islamic legal rulings which they consider irrational or foolish, such as ablution details, Hajj rituals, etc. The author notes that these objections are well-known and omits them. The passage ends by summarizing that these are the groups of deniers of prophethood who still affirm the Creator.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any miracle, regardless of the claim, proves the truth of the claimant.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that miracles only have evidentiary value when the claim is rationally possible. An impossible claim cannot be proven by any miracle.

Key Takeaway
Miracles are proofs only when the claim they support is rationally possible; impossible claims cannot be validated by any miracle.

The Priority of Foundational Beliefs in Prophethood Discussions
Al-Nasafi explains that those who deny the Creator necessarily deny prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi insists that debates about prophethood must first establish the existence of God and the reliability of testimony as a source of knowledge.

Core Idea
Prophethood cannot be argued without first establishing the Creator and reliable testimony.

Al-Nasafi turns to two categories of opponents: atheists who deny the Creator and skeptics (the Sumaniyya and Barahima) who reject prophecy because they deny testimony as knowledge. He explains that direct debate about prophethood is futile without first addressing these foundational issues. This passage shows the methodological priority in theological discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the two groups mentioned and the author's response to each.
The passage is compact; read carefully.
2
Identify the two groups and their objections
First, those who deny the Creator (atheists). Second, the Sumaniyya and Barahima who deny prophethood because they reject testimony (khabar) as a source of knowledge.
Each group requires a different foundational discussion.
3
Understand the methodological priority
Why does al-Nasafi avoid discussing prophethood directly with these groups? Because without a Creator, a messenger is absurd; without testimony as knowledge, a prophet's report is meaningless. So the prior issues must be settled first.
This shows the importance of proper theological order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما من أنكر الصانع فلا شك أنه منكر للرسالة إذ القول بالرسول ولا مرسل محالا. إلا أن الكلام معهم يقع في إثبات الصانع. وكذا من أنكر الرسالة من السمنية والبراهمة لما أن الخبر ليس من أسباب المصارف فلا يفيد خبر الرسول معنى فلا فائدة في إرساله. لا نتكلم معه في هذه المسألة ابتداء بل نتكلم فى إثبات كون الخبر سببا من أسباب المعارف، وقد مر في أول الكتاب ما يقع به الفنية بحمد الله تعالى.
As for one who denies the Creator, there is no doubt that he is a denier of prophethood, for the statement of a messenger without a sender is absurd. However, discussion with them occurs in establishing the Creator. Likewise, one who denies prophethood among the Sumaniyya and the Barahima, because testimony is not among the causes of knowledge, so the report of a messenger conveys no meaning, so there is no benefit in sending him. We do not discuss with him in this issue initially, rather we discuss in establishing that testimony is a cause among the causes of knowledge, and it has already passed at the beginning of the book what suffices, by the praise of Allah.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage addresses a different class: those who deny the Creator entirely. The author states that such people automatically deny prophethood because a messenger without a sender is absurd. However, the discussion with them must first focus on establishing the existence of the Creator. Similarly, the Sumaniyya and Barahima reject prophethood on the grounds that testimony (khabar) is not a source of knowledge, so a prophet's report carries no weight. The author postpones discussion with them until after establishing that testimony is in fact a valid source, as already covered in the beginning of the book.

Misconception: One might think that prophethood can be discussed independently of the existence of God.
Correction: Al-Nasafi insists that without a Creator, a messenger is meaningless, so theism must be established first.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are not worth debating
B) Because the discussion must first establish the existence of the Creator and the validity of testimony as a source of knowledge
C) Because they accept prophethood already
D) Because the author does not have arguments

Key Takeaway
For effective theological debate, foundational beliefs—the existence of God and the validity of testimony—must be established before discussing prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Linguistic and Technical Definition of Risalah
Al-Nasafi defines risalah linguistically and in its revealed sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of risalah (prophethood) and why the technical definition emphasizes the role of the prophet as a mediator for human needs.

Core Idea
Prophethood is God's message conveyed through a chosen intermediary to guide human intellect beyond its limits.

Before proving the necessity of prophethood, al-Nasafi argues we must first define it precisely. He distinguishes between the simple linguistic meaning—'conveying a message'—and the deeper technical meaning used in theology, which frames prophethood as a divine intercession that fills the gaps left by human reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Nasafi first states the need for definition before proof.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage presents a linguistic definition and a technical definition of risalah. Find each one and note how they differ. The linguistic definition is broad, while the technical one is specific to prophethood.
The technical definition includes the phrase 'to remove their excuses'.
3
Understand why definition precedes proof
Ask yourself why al-Nasafi insists on defining risalah before proving its possibility. The commentary will help clarify that establishing a clear concept is a prerequisite for any argument about its existence.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبل أن نجيء إلى إقامة الدلالة على صحة الرسالة، لا بد أن نتكلم في تحديد الرسالة لتعلم في نفسها ثم نقيم الدلالة على ثبوتها فنقول: معنى الرسالة والرسول] أما الرسالة في اللغة فهي تحميل جملة من الكلام إلى المقصود بالدلالة، ولا شك في صحة هذا الحد إذ كل رسالة فيما بين الخلق فهي داخلة في جملة هذا الحد وأما الرسالة التي اختلفنا في ثبوتها، فقد قيل في حدها أنها هي سفارة العبد بين الله تعالى وبين ذوي الألباب من خليقته ليزيح بها عللهم فيما قصرت عنه عقولهم من مصالح داريهم. ولا خفاء على من كان عارفا بشرائط صحة الحد لصحته عنده، وبعد حصول المعرفة بحدها يجب صرف العناية إلى إثبات إمكانها.
And before we come to establish the evidence for the soundness of prophethood, we must discuss the definition of prophethood to know it in itself, then we establish the evidence for its establishment. We say: The meaning of risalah and rasul. As for risalah in language, it is the conveyance of a body of speech to the intended person by indication. There is no doubt about the soundness of this definition, for every message among people is included in the scope of this definition. As for the risalah about which we have disagreed regarding its establishment, it has been said in its definition that it is the intercession of the servant between Allah Most High and those of intellect among His creation, to remove their excuses concerning what their intellects have fallen short of in the interests of their two abodes. This is not hidden from one who knows the conditions for the soundness of a definition. And after obtaining knowledge of its definition, one must turn attention to establishing its possibility.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage transitions from the preliminary discussions to the definition of prophethood (risalah). The author explains that before providing proofs for the soundness of prophethood, one must first define what it is. He gives two definitions: a linguistic definition – 'conveyance of speech by indication' – and a technical definition – 'the intercession of the servant between Allah and intelligent beings to remove their excuses regarding matters beyond their intellect, relating to the interests of this world and the Hereafter.' He asserts the soundness of this definition for those familiar with the conditions of definition, and then states that after understanding the definition, one must turn to establishing its possibility.

The Rational Possibility of Prophethood
Nasafi argues that prophethood is logically possible for the owner of all things to send a messenger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that prophethood is logically possible given God's absolute ownership of all creation.

Core Idea
Prophethood is possible because the Owner can communicate with His property.

Nasafi begins by narrowing the discussion to those who already accept God as the sole Creator and Owner. He then argues that since God owns everything, He has the full right to impose obligations and send messengers to His servants. This is not rationally impossible; rather, it follows from His sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent and the precondition
Notice that Nasafi specifies that the discussion only applies to those who affirm God's oneness, ownership, and lordship. If someone denies these, the topic of prophethood cannot be discussed until those fundamentals are proven.
This frames the argument within a shared theological ground.
2
Grasp the key premise: ownership entails disposal rights
Nasafi states that every owner has authority to dispose of their property as they see fit. This is a basic rational principle: ownership implies the right to act upon what is owned.
This premise is used analogically for God's ownership.
3
Apply the premise to God's absolute ownership
God has complete ownership (mulk al-takhlīq) over every part of the world and every human being, as He creates them from nothing. Therefore, He has the right to impose obligations (taklīf) of any kind—command, prohibition, permission, restriction—and to inform people of these obligations by any means, including sending a messenger.
God's ownership is total and direct, so His disposal rights are unrestricted.
4
Conclude that prophethood is not impossible
Since God is the disposer in His own dominion, sending a messenger is not inherently impossible. If it were impossible, that would be due to an external factor, but no such factor exists. Thus, prophethood is rationally possible.
The burden of proof lies on those who claim impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرسالة ممكنة: أما الكلام في الإمكان فنقول: إن خصماءنا في باب الرسالة هم المقرون بالصانع الحكيم الواحد الفرد الذي لا يشركه أحد في ألوهيته، ولا يخرج شيء من المحدثات عن ملكه وقهره، وأنه هو المالك وما سواه مملوك، وهو الرب وما عداه فأما مربوب. من خالفنا في شيء من هذه الفصول فأنكر حدوث العالم أو ثبوت الصانع أو وحدانيته فلا كلام معه في فصل الرسالة، بل الواجب إثبات ذلك عليه بالدلائل على ما بينا. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وكان الاعتراف من خصمائنا بذلك كله سابقا نقول: إن ورود التكليف بالإيجاب والحظر والإطلاق والمنع ممن له الملك في مماليكه ليس مما تأباه العقول ولا مما تدفعه الدلائل، إذ لكل مالك ولاية التصرف في مملوكه بقدر ما له من الملك فيه، والله تعالى في كل جزء من أجزاء العالم وشخص من أشخاص بني آدم ملك التخليق، إذ هو الموجد له عن العدم المخترع له لا عن أصل، فكان له أن يتصرف في كل من ذلك بأي وجه شاء من وجوه التصرف منعًا كان أو إطلاقا، حظرًا كان أو إيجابًا، ثم يعلمهم ذلك بأي طريق شاء، إن شاء بتخليقه العلم بذلك التكليف، وإن شاء بإرسال رسول يوحى إليه ذلك، ويأمره بالتبليغ إلى العقلاء المميزين من عباده، كان ذلك الرسول من جنس المرسل إليهم أو من خلاف جنسهم، ولا يستحيل ذلك منه إذ هو المتصرف في مُلْكه وملكه، وما هذا سبيله فلا استحالة في نفسه، فلو استحال لاستحال لمعنى يقترن به
The message is possible: As for the discussion on possibility, we say: Our opponents in the topic of the message are those who affirm the Wise Creator, the One, the Individual, whom none shares in His divinity, and nothing of originated things escapes His dominion and subjugation, and that He is the Owner and everything else is owned, and He is the Lord and whatever is other than Him is instead a servant. Whoever opposes us in any of these points, denying the temporality of the world or the affirmation of the Creator or His oneness, then there is no discussion with him on the topic of the message; rather, it is obligatory to prove that to him by proofs, as we have explained. And if the matter is so, and the acknowledgment from our opponents of all that is prior, we say: The occurrence of obligation (taklīf) by command and prohibition and permission and restriction from the one who has ownership over his servants is not something that intellects reject nor that proofs refute, for every owner has authority to dispose of his property according to the extent of his ownership in it. And God Almighty, in every part of the world and every individual human being, has ownership of creation (mulk al-takhlīq), for He is the one who brings it into existence from non-existence, inventing it not from an origin. So He has the right to dispose of every such thing in any manner He wishes of dispositions, whether prohibition or permission, restriction or command, then to make them know that by any path He wishes: if He wishes, by creating knowledge of that obligation in them, and if He wishes, by sending a messenger to whom He reveals that, and commands him to convey to the rational discerning among His servants, whether that messenger is of the same species as those sent to or of a different species. And that is not impossible from Him, for He is the disposer in His dominion and property, and whatever is of this nature has no impossibility in itself. For if it were impossible, it would be impossible due to some meaning accompanying it.
Source pages p. 671

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the possibility (imkān) of prophethood. Nasafi first specifies the opponents: those who affirm the existence of a single, all-powerful Creator. If someone denies the world's temporality, the Creator, or His oneness, they are excluded from this discussion, as those issues must be settled first. Given that the opponents accept God as the absolute Owner (al-mālik) and everything else as owned (mamlūk), Nasafi argues that the owner has authority to dispose of his property as he sees fit. God's ownership is total: He creates every part of the world and every human being ex nihilo. Therefore, He has the right to impose obligations (taklīf) of any kind—command, prohibition, permission, restriction—on His servants. He can inform them of these obligations by any means He chooses: either by creating direct knowledge in them or by sending a messenger (rasūl) who receives revelation and conveys it. The messenger may be of the same species as the recipients or different; neither is impossible. The impossibility would only arise from some external factor (maʿnā), but since God is the sovereign disposer, no such factor exists. Thus, prophethood is rationally possible.

Key Terms

الرسالة
التكليف
الملك
المالك
المملوك
الرب
المربوب
التصرف

ملك التخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prophethood is impossible because God is transcendent and does not interact with creation.
Correction: Nasafi shows that God's absolute ownership entails His right to communicate and impose obligations, making prophethood rationally possible.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is it logically possible for God to send messengers?
A) Because God is merciful and wants to guide humanity
B) Because God has ownership over all creation and can dispose as He wishes
C) Because prophets are necessary for society
D) Because human reason is insufficient

Key Takeaway
Nasafi proves that prophethood is rationally possible because God, as the absolute Owner of all creation, has the authority to send messengers to His servants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is the absolute Owner and you are His property, are you living as if you own yourself? What specific area of your life do you need to surrender to His authority today, and what step will you take within the next 24 hours to act on that surrender?
?
The passage says God can impose any obligation He wishes. Is there a command of Allah that you have been resisting because you thought it was too difficult or unnecessary? How does recognizing that He, as your Owner, has the right to command you change your reluctance? Will you obey that command today?
?
God chooses to send messengers to inform us of His obligations. Have you been treating the message of the Prophet ﷺ as optional advice rather than a binding directive from your Owner? What would it look like for you to treat every command of the Sunnah as an owner's instruction to his servant?

The Rational Basis for the Possibility of Prophethood
The author presents the rational argument for the possibility of prophethood based on divine ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God's absolute ownership makes it possible for Him to send messengers and impose obligations.

Core Idea
God's ownership is the foundation for the rational possibility of prophethood.

Before diving into proofs of prophethood, we must first establish that it is even possible. The author begins by defining common ground with opponents and then presents a powerful argument from divine ownership. This lesson covers that foundational reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the author's outline of the discussion first, then the definition of the opponent's beliefs, then the main argument.
2
Identify the common ground
The author specifies that the opponents must first acknowledge the wise, singular Creator and His ownership of all things. Understand why this is necessary for the discussion.
3
Understand the ownership argument
The core argument: since God owns everything, He has the authority to impose obligations and send messengers. This is not rejected by intellect or proofs.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that there is no inherent impossibility in sending messengers. Any apparent impossibility would be due to an external factor, not the act itself.
This is a key rational principle: actions are judged by their essence, not by accidental circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنتكلم في إثبات إمكانها أولاً ثم نتكلم في الاختلاف في وجوبها بين المتكلمين. ثم نتكلم في إقامة الدلالة على ثبوتها، ونأتي في كل فصل بما يوجب صحته من الدلائل - إن شاء الله تعالى.
[الرسالة ممكنة]:
أما الكلام في الإمكان فنقول: إن خصماءنا في باب الرسالة هم المقرون بالصانع الحكيم الواحد الفرد الذي لا يشركه أحد في ألوهيته، ولا يخرج شيء من المحدثات عن ملكه وقهره، وأنه هو المالك وما سواه مملوك، وهو الرب وما عداه فأما مربوب. من خالفنا في شيء من هذه الفصول فأنكر حدوث العالم أو ثبوت الصانع أو وحدانيته فلا كلام معه في فصل الرسالة، بل الواجب إثبات ذلك عليه بالدلائل على ما بينا. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وكان الاعتراف من خصمائنا بذلك كله سابقا نقول : إن ورود التكليف بالإيجاب والحظر والإطلاق والمنع ممن له الملك في مماليكه ليس مما تأباه العقول ولا مما تدفعه الدلائل، إذ لكل مالك ولاية التصرف في مملوكه بقدر ما له من الملك فيه، والله تعالى في كل جزء من أجزاء العالم وشخص من أشخاص بني آدم ملك التخليق، إذ هو الموجد له عن العدم المخترع له لا عن أصل ، فكان له أن يتصرف في كل من ذلك بأي وجه شاء من وجوه التصرف منعًا كان أو إطلاقا، حظرًا كان أو إيجابًا، ثم يعلمهم ذلك بأي طريق شاء، إن شاء بتخليقه العلم بذلك التكليف، وإن شاء بإرسال رسول يوحى إليه ذلك، ويأمره بالتبليغ إلى العقلاء المميزين من عباده، كان ذلك الرسول من جنس المرسل إليهم أو من خلاف جنسهم ، ولا يستحيل ذلك منه إذ هو المتصرف في مُلْكه وملكه، وما هذا سبيله فلا استحالة في نفسه، فلو استحال لاستحال لمعنى يقترن به لا لنفسه، وهذا مما لا شك فيه لمن عقل وأنصف من نفسه. والذي يؤيد هذا أن الجبلة البشرية مهيأة لقبول الحكم والعلوم معدة للزيادة وبلوغ درجة الكمال فيها عند إفادة الحكيم المرشد إياها، إذ هى ليست ممن لا يجوز عليه الجهل بالأشياء، ولا مما يمتنع عليه قبول العلوم والحكم مع تفنن أقسامها وتكثر أعدادها . ثم لا شك أن صانع العالم حكيم لا يسفه عليم لا يجهل، ومن هذا وصفه، فليس بممتنع منه إمداد المجبول على النقيصة بما يوجب زوالها عنه ويورث له الكمال وبلوغ الدرجة العالية في العلم والحكمة، بل هو من الممكن الذي جرت به العادة من الموسومين بالحكمة المشهورين بالتحلي بالعلوم إذ لم يخل عصر من الأعصار ممن يقوم من العلماء لإفادة ما سعد به من العلم المحتاجين إليه العارين عنه ليبلغوا ببركته الكمال وتندفع عنهم نقيصة الجهل بذلك.
ذلك.
فالله تعالى مع كمال حكمته ورحمته على عبادة أولى أن لا يمتنع منه
We will speak first about proving its possibility, then we will speak about the disagreement regarding its necessity among the theologians. Then we will speak about establishing evidence for its confirmation, and we will bring in each chapter what necessitates its correctness from the proofs - if God wills.
[The message is possible]:
As for the speech about possibility, we say: Our opponents in the chapter of the message are those who affirm the wise, one, singular Maker who has no partner in His divinity, and none of the originated things escape His dominion and force, and that He is the Owner and everything else is owned, and He is the Lord and whatever is other than Him is something lorded. Whoever opposes us in any of these chapters and denies the temporal creation of the world or the existence of the Maker or His oneness, then there is no speech with him in the chapter of the message, rather it is obligatory to prove that to him by proofs as we have explained. And when the matter is so, and the acknowledgment from our opponents of all that is prior, we say: The occurrence of obligation by command, prohibition, permission, and forbiddance from one who has ownership over his possessions is not something that intellects reject nor something that proofs repel, for every owner has authority to dispose of his property according to the extent of his ownership in it. And God Almighty, in every part of the world and every individual of the children of Adam, has the ownership of creation, for He is the one who brings it into existence from non-existence, the inventor of it without an origin. So it is for Him to dispose of each of that in any manner He wills from the modes of disposal, whether prohibition or permission, forbiddance or obligation, then to inform them of that in any way He wills: if He wills, by creating knowledge of that obligation; and if He wills, by sending a messenger who is inspired with that, and He commands him to convey it to the rational, discerning servants of His. That messenger may be of the same kind as those sent to or of a different kind. And that is not impossible from Him, for He is the disposer in His kingdom and property. And whatever is such, there is no impossibility in itself. For if it were impossible, it would be impossible due to an accompanying meaning, not due to itself. And this is something that there is no doubt about for one who has intellect and is fair to himself. And what supports this is that the human disposition is prepared for accepting judgments and sciences, ready for increase and reaching the degree of perfection in them when taught by the wise guide, for it is not such that ignorance of things cannot occur to it, nor such that it is impossible for it to accept sciences and judgments with the diversity of their kinds and multiplicity of their numbers. Then there is no doubt that the Maker of the world is wise, not foolish, knowing, not ignorant. And one whose description is this, it is not impossible for Him to supply the one who is created upon deficiency with what necessitates its removal from him and gives him perfection and reaching the high degree in knowledge and wisdom. Rather, it is from the possible that custom has proceeded with by those characterized with wisdom, famous for adorning themselves with sciences, since no era has been devoid of those among scholars who stand to teach what they have been blessed with of knowledge to those in need of it, who are bare of it, so that by his blessing they may reach perfection and the deficiency of ignorance may be removed from them by that.
That.
So God Almighty, with the perfection of His wisdom and mercy upon His servants, is more deserving that He should not be prevented from [doing] that.
Source pages p. 671

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the possibility of prophethood (رسالة). The author begins by outlining the structure of the discussion: first proving possibility, then discussing disagreement about necessity, then establishing evidence. He then defines the common ground with opponents: they affirm a wise, singular Creator who owns all things. If someone denies these fundamentals, discussion about prophethood is postponed until those are proven. The main argument: obligation (تكليف) from an owner to his possessions is not rationally repugnant. God owns every part of creation absolutely, so He may impose obligations and prohibitions as He wills. He may inform humans of these obligations either by creating direct knowledge or by sending a messenger, whether the messenger is human or not. There is no inherent impossibility in this; any apparent impossibility would arise from an external circumstance, not the act itself. The author then supports this with two considerations: (1) human nature is naturally prepared to receive knowledge and increase in perfection through instruction from a wise guide; (2) God is wise and not foolish, so He would not withhold the means of perfection from deficient creatures. Customarily, wise people provide instruction, so God, being supremely wise and merciful, would do the same. Thus sending messengers is possible.

Key Terms
إمكان
رسالة
تكليف
مملوك
ملك التخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the passage proves that God must send messengers (necessity).
Correction: The passage only proves possibility. The author later discusses necessity separately.

Checkpoint
What is the main basis for the possibility of prophethood according to the author?
A) The goodness of God
B) God's ownership and authority over creation
C) The human need for guidance
D) The consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is rationally possible because God, as the absolute owner of all creation, has the authority to send messengers and impose obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God truly owns every part of your existence—your life, your health, your thoughts—why do you still resist His commands? What specific command are you struggling to accept, and will you surrender that area to His authority today?
?
The author says that intellectual fairness leads to accepting the possibility of prophethood. In what area of your life are you being intellectually unfair to yourself—refusing to consider that God may speak to you through His book or His messenger? Will you commit to reading the Quran with an open heart this week?
?
God can choose any means to communicate with you—even a messenger from a different species. Are you truly listening for His message, or have you dismissed certain forms of guidance as impossible? Name one way you will actively seek His guidance this week, even if it challenges your assumptions.

Human Nature and Divine Wisdom Support Prophethood
The author provides two additional arguments supporting the possibility of prophethood: human nature is teachable and God's wisdom demands instruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how human disposition and divine wisdom together reinforce the possibility of prophethood.

Core Idea
Human nature is prepared for instruction, and God's wisdom makes sending messengers fitting.

After establishing the logical possibility from ownership, the author adds two supporting considerations: human beings have a natural capacity to receive knowledge and instruction, and God, being supremely wise and merciful, would not withhold the means of perfection. This lesson explores these complementary arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the transition from the previous argument: 'والذي يؤيد هذا' (what supports this).
2
Examine the argument from human disposition
The author states that human nature is prepared to accept judgments and sciences, and can reach perfection through a wise guide. Understand why this makes prophethood plausible.
3
Examine the argument from divine wisdom
Since God is wise and not foolish, it is not impossible for Him to provide the means of perfection to deficient creatures. The author draws an analogy to human custom.
4
Synthesize the two arguments
Together, these points show that sending messengers is not only possible but also in accordance with God's nature and human capacity.

The Objection That Divine Obligation Is Foolishness
The author states the objection that divine obligation is foolishness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific objection raised against divine obligation and its underlying assumption.

Core Idea
Opponents argue that divine obligation is foolish because it brings God no benefit.

This passage presents the central objection from those who deny the necessity of prophethood. They argue that obligating humans makes no sense if God gains nothing. Before we can answer, we must understand the challenge clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the objection in its original form.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
The objection has two components: commanding what benefits no one and forbidding what harms no one. Notice how each is phrased.
The word 'سفه' (foolishness) is the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Ask yourself: why would lack of benefit/harm for God make the act foolish? This reveals the opponents' assumption that command/prohibition must serve the commander's interest.
This assumption is later refuted in subsequent passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على شبه الخصوم]
وأما ما زعموا أن ورود التكليف منه سفه لما فيه من أمر بما لا منفعة فيه للآمر ، ونهي عما لا مضرة في وجوده للناهي وهذا سفه.
The refutation of the doubts of the opponents:
And as for what they claimed, that the occurrence of obligation from Him is foolishness because it involves commanding what has no benefit for the commander and forbidding what has no harm in its existence for the forbiddance, and this is foolishness.
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (شبهة) from opponents of prophethood. They argue that God imposing obligations (تكليف) is foolish (سفه) because it involves commanding something that brings no benefit to the commander (God) and forbidding something that causes no harm to the forbidder. Since God is self-sufficient and cannot benefit or be harmed, the act of commanding and forbidding appears pointless and thus foolish. The author states this objection before proceeding to refute it in subsequent sections. The passage does not include the refutation itself, but sets up the discussion.

Key Terms
سفه
تكليف
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author agrees that divine obligation is foolish.
Correction: The author presents this only as a shubha (doubt) from opponents to be refuted; he does not endorse it.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised by the opponents regarding divine obligation?
A) It is impossible because humans cannot understand it.
B) It is foolish because it commands without benefit and forbids without harm to God.
C) It is unnecessary because humans are already guided by reason.
D) It is contradictory to God's mercy.

Key Takeaway
The objection that divine obligation is foolishness rests on the premise that God only commands or forbids for His own benefit or harm, which is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a command from Allah is pointless or burdensome? How does the objection in this lesson mirror that feeling, and what would it take for you to submit wholeheartedly even when you don't see the benefit?
?
When you struggle to obey Allah, do you sometimes think, 'What is the point for me?' How would you answer that thought knowing that Allah commands solely for your benefit, not His?
?
If you truly believed that every command from Allah is a mercy designed for your success, what one act of obedience would you start doing consistently from today?

The Definition of Foolishness and Why It Does Not Apply to God
Al-Nasafi defines foolishness and refutes its application to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for foolishness (safah) in Islamic theology and why God's actions cannot be considered foolish.

Core Idea
Foolishness is not about benefit — it is about being prohibited by a higher authority.

This passage tackles a key objection against the possibility of prophethood: that sending messengers is foolish because they may be harmed or bring no benefit to God. The author, al-Nasafi, clarifies what truly defines foolishness. He shows that in the witnessed world, actions become foolish only because they are prohibited by a higher authority. Since God has no such authority, His actions—including sending messengers—cannot be foolish.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow: definition, application, examples, and conclusion.
2
Identify the core definition of foolishness
Find the sentence that states the criterion for foolishness in the witnessed world. What is the only reason an action is considered foolish?
Look for the phrase: 'وكل تصرف أو فعل في الشاهد ما كان سفهًا إلا لكونه منهيًا عنه'.
3
Apply the definition to God
Why can't God's actions be foolish? Trace the argument: no authority over God, so no prohibition, so no foolishness. Consider the examples given for human foolishness and why they don't apply to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: إن من يذهب إلى أن إرسال الرسل في حيز الممكنات ولا يترقى عن ذلك إلى القول بكونها واجبة الوجود لا محالة، لا نسلم لكم أن فعلاً ما يكون سفها ما لم يكن منهيًا عنه من قبل من له ولاية الأمر والنهي، وكل تصرف أو فعل في الشاهد ما كان سفهًا إلا لكونه منهيًا عنه، وما كان فاعله سفيها إلا لارتكابه نهي خالقه، ولا نهي لأحد على الله تعالى لما يستحيل دخوله تحت نهي غيره، إذ لا ولاية لغيره عليه ولا قدرة، فلا يتصور كون فعله سفها، فكل من أمر غيره بما لا منفعة له فيه أو نهى عما لا مضرة له فيه أو أرسل رسولاً إلى من يكذب رسوله أو يقصده بمكروه أو فعل فعلاً لا منفعة له فيه كان سفيهًا، لأن صانعه نهاه عن هذه الأفعال فكان سبب كون الفعل سفهًا هذا هو، ويستحيل تعديته إلى الغائب فلم يكن فعلاً ما من الصانع سفها فلم يستقم منكم هذا الكلام.
Then we say: Those who hold that sending messengers is among possibilities and do not go beyond that to saying that it is necessarily existent — we do not accept from you that an action is foolishness only if it is prohibited by one who has authority over command and prohibition. And every disposition or action in the witnessed world is not foolishness except because it is prohibited, and its doer is not foolish except for committing the prohibition of his Creator. And there is no prohibition upon God Almighty because it is impossible for Him to fall under the prohibition of another, since there is no authority over Him nor power. So His action cannot be conceived as foolishness. So everyone who commands another to something in which there is no benefit for him, or forbids something in which there is no harm for him, or sends a messenger to someone who belies his messenger or intends harm to him, or does an action in which there is no benefit for him — he is foolish because his Creator prohibited him from these actions. So the cause of the action being foolishness is this, and it is impossible to transfer it to the Unseen. So no action of the Creator is foolishness; therefore this statement of yours is not sound.
Source pages p. 674

Commentary
This passage defines the criterion for foolishness (safah) and applies it to God. The author argues that foolishness in the witnessed world is always due to the action being prohibited by a higher authority. Since God has no higher authority, His actions cannot be foolish. The opponent's claim that sending messengers is foolish (because they may be harmed or bring no benefit to God) is based on a flawed comparison. In the witnessed world, actions become foolish only because the agent is prohibited from them by his Creator. This prohibition does not apply to God. Therefore, sending messengers is not foolish; it is among possible things (mumkinat).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An action is foolish if it brings no benefit to the doer or causes harm.
Correction: The passage shows that foolishness in the witnessed world is due to being prohibited by a higher authority, not merely lack of benefit. For example, sending a messenger who may be harmed is foolish for a human because God prohibits such actions, not because it lacks benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what makes an action foolish in the witnessed world?
A) Lack of benefit to the doer
B) Being prohibited by a higher authority
C) Harm to another
D) Being useless

Key Takeaway
An action is foolish only if it is prohibited by a higher authority; since God has no higher authority, His actions—including sending messengers—cannot be foolish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or loss, do you ever silently question God's wisdom, assuming that a 'good' action would not allow such pain? How does this lesson challenge you to stop measuring God's actions by your own limited standard of benefit?
?
In your daily life, do you ever act as if you have authority over God—feeling entitled to comfort, health, or success? What would it mean for you to truly accept that God is under no one's authority, including your expectations?
?
Think of a recent situation where you judged someone else's actions as 'foolish' because they seemed pointless or harmful. How can you apply the lesson's definition of foolishness (prohibition by a higher authority) to become more humble in your judgments of others and of God?

Refuting the Objection that Prophets Cannot Be Distinguished
Refutes objections against distinguishing prophets by arguing God is perfectly capable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that prophets cannot be distinguished from false prophets ultimately implies divine incapacity, which is impossible for God, and thus the sending of messengers is rationally possible.

Core Idea
Objections against prophetic distinction imply God's incapacity—but God is all-powerful.

The objection that we cannot tell a true prophet from a false one seems reasonable at first glance. The opponents argued that changing natures is impossible and counterfeits are always possible. But the author shows that such reasoning leads to a conclusion that contradicts God's perfect power. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author states the objection and then immediately points out its implication.
2
Identify the objection and its implication
The passage lists reasons for the impossibility of distinguishing a prophet from a false prophet. What do these reasons imply about God? Find the key phrase 'موجب عجز الباري'.
The implication is that God would be incapable, which the author refutes.
3
Trace the author's response and conclusion
The author affirms God's perfect power and then draws the conclusion about the possibility of sending messengers. Follow how the argument moves from refutation to positive statement.
The conclusion is that sending messengers is among possible things, established by proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما تعلق به الفرق الآخر من امتناع التفرقة بين النبي والمتنبئ لامتناع تغيير الطباع، أو لإمكان المعارضة وغير ذلك، كل ذلك موجب عجز الباري جل وعلا عن التفرقة بين الصادق والكاذب، وقد ثبت بالدلائل الواضحة أن صانع العالم جل وعلا كامل القدرة يستحيل عليه العجز فكان قادرًا على ذلك ضرورة كمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته. وظهر بهذا أن إرسال الرسل ليس بمستحيل في نفسه ولا سفه في ذاته في حيز الممكنات، وكل ممكن يثبت عند قيام الدليل.
And what the other group adhered to, from the impossibility of distinguishing between a prophet and a false prophet due to the impossibility of changing natures, or the possibility of objection, and other things — all that necessitates incapacity of the Creator, exalted and high, to distinguish between the truthful and the liar. And it has been established by clear proofs that the Maker of the world, exalted and high, is perfect in power; incapacity is impossible for Him. So He is capable of that necessarily due to the perfection of His power and the penetration of His will. And it has become evident from this that sending messengers is neither impossible in itself nor foolishness in its essence; it is among possibilities. And every possible is established when the proof arises.
Source pages p. 675

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that it is impossible to distinguish a true prophet from a false prophet. The opponents argue based on the impossibility of changing natures (taba'i'), possibility of counterfeits (mu'arada), etc. The author points out that such objections imply that God is incapable ('ajz) of making the distinction. But God is perfectly powerful (kamil al-qudra) and incapable of incapacity. Therefore, He is necessarily able to distinguish, e.g., through miracles. The conclusion is that sending messengers is not impossible nor foolish; it is among possible things (mumkinat), and its actuality depends on proof (dalil).

Key Terms

متنبئ
تفرقة
عجز
قدرة
إرسال الرسل
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is impossible to distinguish a true prophet from a false one because God cannot change natures or prevent counterfeits.
Correction: God is perfectly capable of making a clear distinction, e.g., through miracles, as His power is complete.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the claim that it is impossible to distinguish a prophet from a false prophet would imply what about God?
A) He is just
B) He is wise
C) He is incapable
D) He is merciful

Key Takeaway
The possibility of distinguishing prophets from false prophets is grounded in God's perfect power, and sending messengers remains a rational possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Definition of Foolishness (Safah) and Its Conditions
Al-Nasafi defines safah and explains why it cannot apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of safah (foolishness) as an action prohibited by a higher authority and why it cannot be attributed to God.

Core Idea
Foolishness is not about benefit or harm, but about violating a prohibition from a higher authority.

Al-Nasafi begins by defining what makes an action foolish. He grounds the concept in the authority to command and forbid, showing that foolishness is a relational category tied to prohibition. This sets the stage for his argument that God, who has no superior, cannot act foolishly.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of safah
Read the first sentence of the passage carefully. Notice that safah is defined not by lack of benefit, but by being prohibited by someone with authority.
2
Understand the application in the witnessed world
The passage explains that in the shahid, actions are foolish because they are prohibited, and doers are foolish because they commit prohibited acts.
3
See why God is exempt
No one has authority or power over God, so He cannot be subject to prohibition. Therefore, His actions cannot be foolish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سفها ما لم يكن منهيًا عنه من قبل من له ولاية الأمر والنهي، وكل تصرف أو فعل في الشاهد ما كان سفهًا إلا لكونه منهيًا عنه، وما كان فاعله سفيها إلا لارتكابه نهي خالقه، ولا نهي لأحد على الله تعالى لما يستحيل دخوله تحت نهي غيره، إذ لا ولاية لغيره عليه ولا قدرة، فلا يتصور كون فعله سفها، فكل من أمر غيره بما لا منفعة له فيه أو نهى عما لا مضرة له فيه أو أرسل رسولاً إلى من يكذب رسوله أو يقصده بمكروه أو فعل فعلاً لا منفعة له فيه كان سفيهًا، لأن صانعه نهاه عن هذه الأفعال فكان سبب كون الفعل سفهًا هذا هو، ويستحيل تعديته إلى الغائب فلم يكن فعلاً ما من الصانع سفها فلم يستقم منكم هذا الكلام.
Foolishness (safah) is only that which is prohibited by one who has authority to command and forbid. Every action or deed in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) is only foolishness because it is prohibited. The doer is only foolish because he commits the prohibition of his Creator. There is no prohibition for anyone against God Almighty because it is impossible for Him to fall under the prohibition of another, since no one else has authority over Him nor power. So His action cannot be imagined to be foolishness. Thus, whoever commands another to something which brings no benefit, or forbids him from something which brings no harm, or sends a messenger to one who will deny his messenger or intends harm against him, or does an action with no benefit for himself – he is foolish, because his Creator prohibited him from these actions. That is what makes an action foolish. It is impossible to extend this to the Unseen (al-ghā’ib). Therefore, the action of the Creator is not foolishness, so this argument of yours is not valid.
Source pages p. 675

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that sending messengers would be an act of foolishness (safah) on God's part. The author begins by defining safah: an action is foolish only if it is prohibited by someone with authority (wilāyat al-amr wa al-nahy). In the witnessed world (al-shāhid), actions are considered foolish because they violate a command or prohibition from a superior. The doer is called foolish only for committing what his Creator has forbidden. However, there is no one who has authority over God; no one can command or forbid Him. Therefore, it is impossible to conceive of His action as foolish. Next, the author explains the basis of the objection: they claim God sending a messenger to someone who will reject him is like a human ordering something useless. But the author points out that in the human case, the act is prohibited by God, which makes it foolish. This prohibition does not apply to God. Hence, the analogy fails. The conclusion is that the charge of foolishness against God is unfounded.

Key Terms
سفه
ولاية الأمر والنهي
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that an action is foolish if it appears pointless or lacking benefit, regardless of prohibition.
Correction: Al-Nasafi teaches that safah is a legal category requiring a prohibitor. Without a prohibition, an action is not foolish even if it seems without benefit.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what makes an action foolish (safah) in the witnessed world?
A) The action having no benefit
B) The action being prohibited by someone with authority
C) The action being disliked by people
D) The action causing harm to others

Key Takeaway
Safah is not a purely rational evaluation; it depends on a prohibition from a higher authority, which does not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from God that seems unreasonable to your mind, do you still submit wholeheartedly, or do you first judge it by your own sense of benefit and harm? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to rely on your own reasoning over divine authority?
?
Have you ever complained about God's decree, thinking it 'unwise' or 'unfair'? Reflect: if safah is defined by violation of a higher command, and God has no higher, then every action of His is beyond foolishness. Will you now repent from questioning His wisdom and instead trust that His actions are perfect?
?
Consider a moment when you disobeyed a clear divine prohibition. Did you treat that prohibition as optional because you saw personal benefit in the act? How does this lesson re-frame that disobedience not just as a mistake but as foolishness (safah) because it violated His authority? What will you do today to reinforce your submission to His commands?

Why God's Actions Cannot Be Foolish
Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that prophethood is foolishness by analyzing examples and showing they don't apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the objection that sending messengers is foolish by showing that the objection's examples only prove foolishness in creatures, not in God.

Core Idea
The objection's examples only prove foolishness in creatures, not in the Creator.

Having established that safah requires a prohibitor, Nasafi now addresses the specific objection: sending a messenger to someone who will reject him seems pointless. He shows that such actions are only foolish when prohibited by God, and since God has no prohibitor, the analogy collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection examples
The passage lists actions that are foolish in the created world: commanding without benefit, forbidding without harm, sending a messenger to a denier, etc.
Notice these are all actions that are prohibited for humans.
2
Understand the basis of foolishness in these examples
These actions are foolish because the doer's Creator has prohibited them. Without that prohibition, they would not be foolish.
3
Realize the impossibility of applying this to God
The prohibition does not apply to God. Therefore, you cannot extend the charge of foolishness to Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سفها ما لم يكن منهيًا عنه من قبل من له ولاية الأمر والنهي، وكل تصرف أو فعل في الشاهد ما كان سفهًا إلا لكونه منهيًا عنه، وما كان فاعله سفيها إلا لارتكابه نهي خالقه، ولا نهي لأحد على الله تعالى لما يستحيل دخوله تحت نهي غيره، إذ لا ولاية لغيره عليه ولا قدرة، فلا يتصور كون فعله سفها، فكل من أمر غيره بما لا منفعة له فيه أو نهى عما لا مضرة له فيه أو أرسل رسولاً إلى من يكذب رسوله أو يقصده بمكروه أو فعل فعلاً لا منفعة له فيه كان سفيهًا، لأن صانعه نهاه عن هذه الأفعال فكان سبب كون الفعل سفهًا هذا هو، ويستحيل تعديته إلى الغائب فلم يكن فعلاً ما من الصانع سفها فلم يستقم منكم هذا الكلام.
Foolishness (safah) is only that which is prohibited by one who has authority to command and forbid. Every action or deed in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) is only foolishness because it is prohibited. The doer is only foolish because he commits the prohibition of his Creator. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that sending messengers would be an act of foolishness (safah) on God's part. The author begins by defining safah: an action is foolish only if it is prohibited by someone with authority (wilāyat al-amr wa al-nahy). In the witnessed world (al-shāhid), actions are considered foolish because they violate a command or prohibition from a superior. But the author points out that in the human case, the act is prohibited by God, which makes it foolish. This prohibition does not apply to God. Hence, the analogy fails. The conclusion is that the charge of foolishness against God is unfounded.
